![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/dd6b1040a541fa9c70ed905545376263909f9bae87b11a3f67ec7443ffd40ccd.jpg)  
Figure 17.12 Silicified plant remains (Si) in mixed ash from the fuel of a corn drying oven at Grateley, United Kingdom. (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/6ac7cd0c9b228fba8b7a7d00a7f91832a04682508993d4b498f75ba620e1ff2b.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/5db7645196a7710670417e642821c8404969e1f4f299eec21e0fde722f07517e.jpg)  
Figure 17.13 Silica ash melt from Northampton, United Kingdom. PPL.

# 17.3 Ash Taphonomy

Once deposited, ash can undergo a wide range of taphonomic processes, including trampling, mixing, colluviation and recrystallization (Brochier 1993, 2002; Karkanas & Goldberg 2010; also see Mallol et al. 2017, this book).

If retained in a dry, undisturbed environment, ash will preserve indefinitely, but this is not the commonest taphonomic outcome. Many outdoor archaeological situations have enough rainfall to dissolve it completely, sometimes leaving a fine charcoal layer to mark where the ash used to be. Areas of restricted rainfall, for example Middle Eastern open-air sites, will retain ash to a considerable extent where burial is rapid (e.g., Shillito et al. 2011; Friesem et al. 2014; Gur-Arieh et al. 2014). The best preservation is generally found in caves and

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/8f121d3ee4d8c327fa4ebca7d6daa97c3e291e8b8291e507764c9e817cd5fe6a.jpg)  
Figure 17.14 (a) Ash from Early Neolithic cave deposits in the Upper Ebro valley with slightly browner central area of phosphatic staining, PPL. (b) Same as (a), with central area of phosphatic staining producing isotropy. XPL. Image courtesy of A. Polo Díaz.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/d7b648dfda90a5c287228f6cb063018a98f0ce9da848179a1540ca3b6c5c942d.jpg)

rock shelters. These have often sustained long periods of occupation, sometimes with repeated burning of animal bedding and manure, resulting in deep ash accumulations. However, even caves contain moist air, dripping water and bat colonies, which together produce a range of effects. The ash itself will sometimes partially dissolve and recrystallize as neoformed calcite, losing some or all of its oxalate pseudomorph characteristics. In the presence of phosphates (from either degrading bat guano or dissolving bones), the ash calcite will convert to apatite or other phosphates (Karkanas et al. 2000; Shahack-Gross et al. 2004) and therefore loose its birefringence.

# 18

# Opal Phytoliths

Luc Vrydaghs $^{1,2}$ , Yannick Devos $^{1}$  and Ákos Pető $^{3}$

<sup>1</sup> Centre de Recherches en Archéologie et Patrimoine, Université Libre de Bruxelles, Bruxelles, Belgium  
$^{2}$  Research Team in Archaeo- and Palaeosciences, Brussels, Belgium  
3 Institute of Nature Conservation and Landscape Management, Department of Nature Conservation and Landscape Ecology, Gödöllö, Hungary

# 18.1 Introduction

Opal phytoliths are a major component of the microfossil record of many archaeological deposits. As they are stable over long periods, they provide a valuable source of information for archaeobotanical research (for an introduction see Piperno 1988, 2006).

Phytoliths are inorganic bodies of plant origin. Their most frequently published chemical composition is calcium oxalate and hydrated opal  $(\mathrm{SiO}_2\mathrm{nH}_2\mathrm{O})$ . Besides silicon (Si), other elements (Al, Fe, Ni, Mn, P, Cu, N and C) play a significant role in the material composition of the phytolith body (Piperno 1988). Current usage tends to restrict the word 'phytolith' to name plant opal particles. This entry will also use this restricted definition.

Opal phytoliths are produced through the polymerization of monosilicic acid  $(\mathrm{Si}(\mathrm{OH})_4)$  within plant tissues. Monosilicic acid is taken up by the roots and transported by the vascular system. Within the plant tissues, three different loci serve as silica deposits: the cell lumen, the intercellular space and / or the cell wall. This polymerization results in the formation of morphologically identifiable opal casts.

Phytoliths accumulate in different plant organs and tissues (Rapp & Mulholland 1992). Therefore, each plant organ can be characterized by an assemblage of different types of phytoliths, each with varying frequencies. This multiplicity (Rovner 1971) is given by phytoliths with specific and less specific morphologies. The silica skeleton (Figures 18.1a, b) illustrates such a structure with interlocked phytoliths.

# 18.2 Micromorphology

# 18.2.1 Optical Characteristics

Opal phytoliths have negative relief, show optical isotropism and are generally colourless in PPL, although they can grade to light tan, brown and black (Gutiérrez-Castorena & Effland 2010) due to carbon inclusions (Stoops 2003), coating, or burning. Stoops (2003) reports that they generally show a clear whitish colour in oblique incident light (OIL) or transmitted dark-field illumination (TDFI). Newly formed phytoliths do not seem to present any autofluorescence (Altemüller & Van Vliet-Lanoë 1990). However, Van Vliet-Lanoë (1980) observed phytoliths presenting strong autofluorescence in Podzols and Andosols.

# 18.2.2 Morphological Characteristics

The size of phytoliths ranges from about 5 to  $250\mu \mathrm{m}$  (Piperno 1988, 2006; Runge 1999). Their shape and ornamentations can vary widely (Rapp & Mulholland 1992). Opal phytoliths are reported for Pteridophyths (Piperno 1988), Gymnosperms (Klein & Geis 1978; Sangster et al. 1997), Monocotyledons and Dicotyledons (Twiss et al. 1969; Brown 1984; Bozarth 1992; Kealhoffer & Piperno 1998; Ball et al. 2006). Three online phytolith datasets provide abundant illustrations of phytoliths:

- the Phytolith Data Base of the Paleoethnobotany Laboratory of the University of Missouri-Columbia: http://phytolith.missouri.edu/(accessed 20 February 2017);

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/5e72241aab7973285bdaa4453774e1d95586891469f206172a3b0aef172d4d93.jpg)  
Figure 18.1 Silica skeletons. (a) This skeleton of Festuca sp. shows typical morphologies (short cells and stomata) with redundant ones (long cell walls and hair cells), PPL. (b) Long wavy phytoliths. The blackish appearance is due to burning (medieval dark earth - site of the Court of Hoogstraeten, Brussels, Belgium - see Devos et al. 2013a). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/3247b1dca36b8e680017a77327c326226a5877ab47226c0a4d5cf38d6e1a081c.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/243b0fe92487432dc8f358b99775f1b2dadc5f3a519b0410f63053c5bb4a9755.jpg)  
Figure 18.2 Dendritic phytoliths distributed according to a 'puzzle-like' pattern and referred to as 'articulated phytoliths'. The phytoliths, which are viewed in a roughly top view, are distributed into two fields of focus (medieval dark earth - Site of Court of Hoogstraeten, Brussels, Belgium - see Devos et al. 2013a). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/f86e0c425fa4e8dcdccc19e8eae75d3bf8d42aecd0562953b0827dbb1056307b.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/3c3250546d312e4f1fef0920a6ab33f891bf172eaa817ef680eb892fd4382258.jpg)

- the Old World Reference collection of UCL: http:// www.homepages.ucl.ac.uk/~tcrndfu/phytoliths.html (accessed 20 February 2017):  
- the ArcheoScience PhytCore database: www.phytcore.org (accessed 21 March 2017).

A series of two-dimensional rounded and angular shapes has been proposed by Stoops (2003). This system, however, does not account for phytoliths' huge morphological diversity or for any botanical identification. Phytolith description and identification relies on their shape and surface ornamentation as defined by the ICPN Working Group (2005).

One should also consider that, in thin sections, phytoliths known as globular echinates (ICPN Working Group 2005), typical of Palmae taxa, can be confused with some star-shaped sponge spicules (see Vrydaghs 2017, this book).

As the width and height of the phytolith is normally lower than the standard thickness of a thin section, a three-dimensional view of the observed material can be obtained (Figure 18.2). Future applications of extended depth focus (EDF - a technique that constructs an electronic picture of the phytolith based on multiple images taken at different focal planes) to the analysis of phytoliths (Wu & Wang 2009) might significantly improve the opportunities and definition of such 3D-oriented analysis. In thin section, phytoliths may appear in various positions (front and rear, side and top view, cross or longitudinal sections of the silicified individual cell or silica skeleton). This can either help or hinder their observation and identification. In an ideal situation, a phytolith appears in top view, implying that the polishing of the surface of the section reaches just the level of the phytolith.

# 18.3 Brief Review of Previous Micromorphological Studies Reporting Phytoliths

The occurrence of phytoliths within thin sections of archaeological deposits has frequently been reported (Macphail 1981; Stoops & Nijs 1986; Macphail et al. 1990; Sordoillet et al. 2007). More recently, several studies associating phytolith analysis and micromorphology were conducted either in relation to present-day materials and as experiments (Shahack-Gross et al. 2004; Villagran et al. 2011; Miller & Sievers 2012), or for the study of ancient deposits (Shahack-Gross et al. 2005; Vrydaghs et al. 2007; Albert et al. 2008; Osterrieth et al. 2009; Cabanes et al. 2010; Mallol et al. 2010; Villagran et al. 2010; Petro & Barczi 2011; Shillito 2011a, b, 2013; Wadley et al. 2011). Most of these contributions might be grouped under two broad categories, with some documenting the presence and others the distribution of phytoliths within archaeological units.

In recent years, morphotypological analysis of the distribution patterns was carried out in relation to the study of European urban dark earths (Devos & Vrydaghs 2009; Devos et al. 2007, 2009, 2013a, b; Nicosia et al. 2017, this book).

So far, mostly phytoliths deriving from grasses have been reported by the analysis of thin sections: grass silica short cells (GSCP), cuneiform (Figure 18.3a), hair (Figure 18.3b), parallelepipedal (Figure 18.3c), dendritic (Figure 18.3d) and conical. The GSCP includes several shapes named as lobate, cross, trapeziform (Figure 18.3e) and saddle. Lobate phytoliths can either be bi- or polylobate.

# 18.4 Deposition and Accumulation in Archaeological Units

# 18.4.1 Archaeological Contexts

Phytoliths can occur in any context involving the incorporation of plant material, such as:

- Ancient agricultural soils (Macphail et al. 1990; Simpson et al. 1998; Wilson et al. 2002; Devos et al. 2011; Lewis 2012).  
- Pits related to domestic activities (Macphail et al. 2008), crop storage and crop processing areas and features (Devos et al. 2009, 2013a, b; Shillito 2011a).  
- Middens (Simpson & Barrett 1996; Shillito 2011b).  
- Ancient floors, floor coverings and bedding, surfaces, walls and roofs (Guelat et al. 1998; Shahack-Gross

et al. 2005; Macphail et al. 2006; Cabanes et al. 2010; Cammas 2010; Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Milek & Roberts 2013).

- Coprolites and dung accumulation (Cammas 2000; Polo Díaz & Eraso 2010; Macphail & Goldberg 2010; Shahack-Gross 2011; Devos et al. 2012; see also Bronnimann et al. 2017, this book; Shahack-Gross 2017, this book).  
- European urban dark earth (Macphail 1981, 1994; Borderie 2011; Devos et al. 2011, 2013a, b; see also Nicosia et al. 2017, this book).  
- Amazonian dark earth (Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2008; see also Arroyo-Kalin 2017, this book).  
- Ash layers and fireplaces (Courty et al. 1989; Gebhardt & Langohr 1999; Canti 2003; Simpson et al. 2003; Gebhardt 2007; Cabanes et al. 2010; Mentzer 2012; Shillito & Matthews 2013; Shillito & Ryan 2013; see also Canti & Brochier 2017, this book; Mallol et al. 2017, this book).

Taking into account that opal phytoliths accumulate within plant tissues, the analysis of thin sections records two types of phytolith occurrence: phytoliths as sedimentary particles and phytoliths within preserved buried plants fragments (Figure 18.4).

The accumulation of phytoliths as sedimentary particles only occurs through the decomposition of plant organs. The basic paradigm of the phytolith taphonomy is that they tend to be released through mechanisms of decay in place. Unless biostratimonical (e.g., what happens between the death of the plant and its deposition - see Madella & Lancelotti 2012) and/or postdepositional events occur, these processes should preserve their anatomical connection (Figure 18.4). Any agent inducing decomposition of organic tissues might be at the origin of phytolith accumulation: soil formation processes, fire (Canti 2003; Madella 2003), dung decomposition (Powers-Jones 1994; Albert 2002) and plant selection (Maier & Harwath 2011).

The preservation of phytoliths depends on the soil conditions, the amount of impurities present in the opal silica matrix (Osterrieth et al. 2009) and the shape (Cabanes et al. 2011; Ryan 2013). In general, phytoliths preserve well, except for very alkaline contexts (pH above 8 or 9 – Cabanes et al. 2011; Ryan 2013). They can show corrosion features, which complicate their identification, as similar features can also be the result of interrupted phytolith 'growth' (Cabanes et al. 2011; Madella & Lancelotti 2012) or the recycling of phytoliths by plants (Alexandre et al. 1997; Blecker et al. 2006). Transport or physical reworking of the soil can cause physical erosion such as breakdown of the phytoliths. Several changes to

Figure 18.3 Different types of grass phytoliths. (a) Isolated angular cuneiform phytolith in side view (medieval dark earth – site of the Court of Hoogstraeten, Brussels, Belgium – see Devos et al. 2013a). PPL. (b) Grass appendage (hair) phytoliths showing a parallel distribution pattern resulting from the preservation of the original pattern within the living plant (herbivore coprolite, site of Petit Rue des Bouchers, Brussels, Belgium – see Devos et al. 2012). PPL. (c) Parallelepipedal phytoliths, deriving from long cells, which are part of the epidermal tissue of leaves, distributed in a fan-shaped pattern (medieval dark earth – Strasbourg, France). PPL. (d) Articulated phytoliths marking the presence of cereals. This system is made of three different morphologies. The long ones with wavy sides are the typical markers for cultivated crops, known as dendritic (ICPN Working Group 2005), and are accumulated within the inflorescence bracts (palea, lemnna and glume) of the cultivated crops (herbivore coprolite, site of Petit Rue des Bouchers, Brussels, Belgium – see Devos et al. 2012). PPL. (e) Isolated trapeziform phytolith in side view, known as rondel (ICPN Working Group 2005). This type of Grass Silica Short Cell is typical for the Pooideae grass subfamily and indicative of temperate or cold conditions (medieval dark earth – Site of Court of Hoogstraeten, Brussels, Belgium – see Devos et al. 2013a). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/9f423504c22c8a7d29eac073f85bebb8eca32632cde0720aa5124b5c6c7f6b21.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/73f7c0a4eb8eaa0c6d5753de39af515aa7af534d41e93515175312ce146d2acd.jpg)

phytoliths exposed to fire or affected by heating have also been noted:

- Traces of blackening on the phytoliths (Parr 2006) (Figure 18.1b).  
Changes in the refractive index (Elbaum et al. 2003).  
- Formation of vesicular, optical isotropic, glassy slag (Courty et al. 1989; Canti 2003; Devos et al. 2009; Mentzer 2012; Canti & Brochier 2017, this book) (Figure 18.5a) and silica droplets (Figure 18.5b).  
- Several authors report the presence of autofluorescent phytoliths within firing contexts (Gebhardt & Langohr 1999; Devos et al. 2009). However, further research is required to confirm these observations.

# 18.4.2 Distribution Patterns

Through the description and explanation of their distribution patterns within the studied units, one of the major contributions of the study of phytoliths in thin sections is the understanding of their taphonomic history. After being released from their organic support, phytoliths can be transported by water, wind, or bioturbation prior or after their burial into the sedimentary environment, leading to different distribution patterns. So far, three main distribution patterns have been recognized: articulated, clustered (Figure 18.6) and isolated (Figure 18.3a and e). However, rare fan-shaped distributions (Figure 18.3c) have also been recorded.

Articulated phytoliths (Figures 18.2, 18.3b and 18.3d) preserve the anatomical connection of the phytoliths and indicate limited postdepositional disturbance.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/f7fb28d8989d1d79e3ebe3fa789f9e809dd291e841bc05b815d436721d689dfc.jpg)  
Figure 18.6 Cluster of dendritic phytoliths. A cluster involves at least two phytoliths to be viewed under different orientations (base, top, side), under different fields of focus or presenting different states of preservation. This pattern implies that some disturbance occurred and as such it documents the taphonomy of the deposit (medieval dark earth - site of rue de Dinant, Brussels, Belgium - see Devos et al. 2009). PPL.

Articulated phytoliths in thin sections were observed as being either contiguous, fan shaped (sensu Stoops 2003) or silica skeletons. Clustered (sensu Stoops 2003) and isolated distributions point to clear reworking of the

soil matrix (see for example Devos et al. 2013a). Systematic or mixed orientation might also be indicative of former surfaces, specific features or (post)depositional histories.

# Siliceous Microfossils from Single-Celled Organisms: Diatoms and Chrysophycean Stomatocysts

Elie Verleyen<sup>1</sup>, Koen Sabbe<sup>1</sup>, Wim Vyverman<sup>1</sup> and Cristiano Nicosa<sup>2</sup>

$^{1}$  Protistology and Aquatic Ecology, Universiteit Gent, Belgium  
<sup>2</sup> Centre de Recherches en Archéologie et Patrimoine, Université Libre de Bruxelles, Belgium

# 19.1 Introduction

A few single-celled organisms, such as diatoms and chrysophytes (together with some lobose testate amoebae, silicoflagellates and radiolarians) produce siliceous cell walls, cysts, skeletons or plates that are preserved in sediments. In some cases, these siliceous structures can provide valuable information on past environments, which is particularly the case for the cell walls of diatoms. Because diatoms have a close-knit relation with environmental factors, they are widely used as bioindicators in archaeological and paleoecological research projects (see Smol & Stoermer 2010 for an overview). This chapter deals with the identification of diatoms and chrysophycean cysts in thin sections and their ecological preferences.

# 19.2 Habitat Preferences of Diatoms and Chrysophycean Cysts

Diatoms occur worldwide in nearly all freshwater, brackish, marine and hypersaline environments in which they are often important primary producers (Round et al. 1990). Some species are adapted to relatively dry conditions and thrive in terrestrial environments such as soils and on mosses and plants (Ettl & Gartner 1995). In general, lakes, rivers and marine environments are inhabited by different taxa because only a few species occur in both lacustrine and marine/estuarine habitats. Diatoms either live suspended in the water column or attached to or moving on substrates (benthic). Holoplanktonic diatoms spend their entire lifecycle suspended in the water column. Meroplanktonic species survive unfavourable conditions as resting stages in the sediment but spend the majority of their live cycle in the plankton. Tychoplanktonic taxa live attached to

substrates but can survive in the plankton as well. Among the true benthic species, a distinction is made based on the substrate on which they live. For example, epilithic species live attached to stones, while epipsammic taxa thrive on sand grains. Epiphytic diatoms occur on plants or macroalgae. A full overview of this habitat-based classification scheme in diatoms and a description of the vast majority of the genera can be found in Round et al. (1990).

Chrysophycean cysts (classes Chrysophyceae and Synurophyceae; hereafter referred to as 'chrysophytes') are generally freshwater organisms and holoplanktonic, while exclusively marine species are rare (see Zeeb & Smol 2001 for a review). Some chrysophytes are benthic and others have an epiphytic lifestyle.

# 19.3 Identification of Siliceous Microfossils produced by Single-Celled Organisms

In general, siliceous microfossils are readily preserved in marine, lake or wetland sediments, provided that the water and sediments are oversaturated in silica. Dissolution can also be an issue in hypersaline and alkaline lakes (Ryves et al. 2006). It can result in the complete destruction of the silica record in sedimentary sequences or in the partial disintegration of microfossils, preventing a reliable identification. Preferential dissolution can also bias assemblages to more heavily silicified taxa, which can complicate palaeo-environmental reconstructions.

As siliceous microfossils are composed of opal, they are transparent and colourless, have a negative relief in PPL, and are optically isotropic in XPL. Because of their characteristic ornaments and shape, microfossils produced by silicifying microbes are easily discerned from those present in multicellular plants (Vrydaghs et al.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/c5b4d485718f50e681efbab037eb9bcaed5735dffb78cefa8e194e9d0638c899.jpg)  
Figure 19.1 Scanning electron microscopy image of a stomatocyst found in Pup Lagoon, an Antarctic isolation lake in the Larsemann Hills. Image courtesy: R. Dasseville.

2017, this book) and animals. Chrysophytes are represented in sediments by two forms of siliceous remains, namely (i) disarticulated scales, bristles and spines and (ii) their resting stages, called stomatocysts or also sometimes statospores or statocysts (Figure 19.1; Duff et al. 1995; see section 19.4 and references therein). However, because not all chrysophytes produce scales, these remains only provide an incomplete view of the true chrysophyte diversity. Moreover, these inclusions are not commonly mentioned in archaeological soil micromorphology literature. This is in contrast with stomatocysts, which are formed by all chrysophytes and therefore enable the reconstruction of the entire community (Duff et al. 1995). Interestingly, cyst morphology is diverse and it is believed that it is species specific (Zeeb & Smol 2010). Stomatocysts are hollow siliceous structures (ca.  $2\mu \mathrm{m}$  to  $>30\mu \mathrm{m}$  in diameter) and have a single pore (Figure 19.1). Given their size, they can occur at various 'depths' within the thickness of petrographic thin sections  $(20 - 30\mu \mathrm{m})$ , resulting in different aspects under the microscope. Because stomatocysts are commonly globose in shape, they can appear as simple circles, or as spheres, the pore eventually being well visible (Figures 19.2 and 19.3). Their surface may be smooth or ornamented. However, apart from being an indicator for freshwater conditions, little additional ecological information is generally available about these cysts and in most cases few morphotypes have been linked to the species producing them. The most comprehensive overview of different forms of stomatocysts and a description of their morphology can be found in Duff et al. (1995) and Wilkinson et al. (2001).

Diatoms are by far the most widely used siliceous bioindicators. Their most characteristic feature is the cell

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/d511a69ae07c657b76be14b30386b86c21def562375513b9dfee804853363034.jpg)  
Figure 19.2 Stomatocyst marked by the arrow. Note the opening (pore) on the left part. Medieval dark earth identified as a ploughsoil from the site of the Poor Clares, Brussels (Belgium). PPL. Image courtesy: Y. Devos.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/754c23ec0a9fbd62bd621facf33ddfa97f86553b571a427f9f0ed1d82e394785.jpg)  
Figure 19.3 Stomatocyst appearing as a sphere with a well-visible pore (arrow). Other stomatocysts appear as circles to the left. Bronze Age ditch fill, site of Fondo Paviani (Verona, Italy - see Dal Corso et al. 2017). Enhanced contrast image, PPL.

wall or frustum, which is composed of two siliceous valves, the epitheca or epivalve (larger) and hypotheca or hypovalve (smaller) that are held together by the girdle bands or cingulum (Figure 19.4).

Diatom frustules can be found in thin sections of soils and sediments in archaeological deposits (Figures 19.5 and 19.6). In thin sections, it is possible to identify diatoms up to the genus and in some cases even to the species level, which provides useful information about the habitat type they require. Nevertheless, their identification and quantification should be carried out according to the standard methods of analysis of

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/784b69a4cbd86b5db01feb6b5e4bd9e84d06abe356d8afd546c7d2878a92920f.jpg)  
Figure 19.4 Scanning electron microscopy image of a marine centric diatom (genus Thalassiosira) showing the two valves, the epitheca (ET) and hypotheca (HT) as well as the girdle bands or cingulum (Ci). Image courtesy: R. Dasseville.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/bd2a03051a7cee5aea603334f67f121f6ce9e57cfdcde9bf6de78475149b2aaa.jpg)  
Figure 19.6 Raphid pennate diatoms (genus Navicula s.l.) filling a crack through a fragment of burnt earth construction material. The fragment was recovered from lacustrine sediment where it probably underwent colonization by algae. Bronze Age pile dwelling site of Bodio Lomnago (Varese, northern Italy - see Nicosia & Vrydaghs 2014). The genus occurs both in marine and lacustrine environments. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/cc6b7539e96b9c145677b7b480e8b1ae395ea44cfd16610008952d4e50c3ebb6.jpg)  
Figure 19.5 Raphid pennate freshwater diatom (genus Epithemia) from a peaty archaeological deposit of medieval age. PLL.

diatom assemblages, which include the removal of organic matter (see Battarbee 1986; Renberg 1990). Traditionally, diatom taxonomy and species delineation were based on the size, shape and architecture of the frustules (Mann 2010). An overview of the terminology used for valve identification and the description of their morphology can be found in Anonymous (1975). Diatoms were originally classified in two orders (see Julius & Theriot 2010), namely the Centrales (centrics; Figure 19.4) and the Pennales (pennates; Figures 19.5 and 19.6). This

classification scheme was based on differences in lifecycle characteristics and the symmetry of their valves, which is radially in the Centrales and along a plane in the Pennales. The most recent classification (Round et al. 1990) treats the diatoms as a division, consisting of three different classes, namely the radially symmetric taxa, and the araphid pennate and the raphid pennate taxa (Figures 19.5 and 19.6). The latter two classes are delineated based on the absence (araphid) or presence (raphid) of the raphe; a structure used in the movement and/or attachment of the frustule through mucilage excretion.

Researchers using diatoms as indicators in an ecological and/or archaeological context should, however, be aware of the unstable nature of diatom taxonomy. This is mainly due to new taxonomic insights related to improved observation techniques, the description of new taxa, and the use of molecular markers for species delineation (Julius & Theriot 2010). Current estimates of the number of species range from 30000 to 100000 in case the most up-to-date species concept is applied (Mann & Vanormelingen 2013). Widely used guides for species identification are Patrick & Reimer (1966, 1975) for North America and Krammer & Lange-Bertalot (1986, 1988, 1991a, b) for Central Europe. For understudied regions, taxa are sometimes force fitted to these identification guides while they are actually different entities with different ecological requirements (e.g., Van de Vijver et al. 2012).

# 19.4 Siliceous Microfossils from Single-Celled Organisms in Archaeological Soil Micromorphology

Diatoms are the most widely used siliceous bioindicators in archaeological and palaeoecological studies (see Cameron 2007; Juggins & Cameron 2010). They can be studied directly in sediment samples from archaeological excavations or from dated sediment cores taken in marine or lacustrine environments close to human settlements. They can provide valuable information on the bidirectional link between past societies and their palaeoenvironment and enable the reconstruction of changes in for example regional and local climate, water quality and land use (see Smol & Stoermer 2010 and references therein). Indicator species can provide qualitative information on environmental changes or, alternatively, numerical methods can be used to quantitatively reconstruct past changes in for example the pH, nutrient concentrations and salinity in lakes (see Birks 2010 for a review).

Diatoms in archaeological deposits for which micromorphological information is available are often reworked and in secondary position. Nevertheless, they can be used as indicators of the origin of materials observed in the thin section. For example, as reported by Devos et al. (2009), the presence of diatoms, phytoliths, bone, charcoal and enhanced phosphate levels was interpreted as evidence for the addition of manure to the soil in medieval Brussels. Simpson et al. (1998) used the presence of diatoms to infer that grassy turf material was added to an anthropic agricultural soil (Plaggen) at Tofts Ness, Sanday, Orkney, whereas Simpson et al. (1999)

# References

inferred the use of turf as fuel based from the presence of diatoms in ash. In a study combining micromorphology and standard diatom extraction and identification, Mücher et al. (1990) were able to infer that brook sediments were added to a Plaggen soil in the Netherlands. The origin of sediments for the construction of the Motte of Werken (Belgium) was also discriminated according to the presence/absence of diatoms in reworked soil material (Gebhardt & Langohr 1999). As diatoms can also be ingested during drinking, they can be found in excrements and urine of animals (see Gebhardt & Langohr 1999; Shahack-Gross 2011; Bronnimann et al. 2017 this book). Another direct application of diatoms in archaeology is the identification of the source area of pottery or building material such as bricks (Flower 2006; Juggins & Cameron 2010). Diatoms can be applied to assess whether a mixture of different clay sources was used for the production of ceramics.

Stomatocysts have been observed in thin sections from dark earth (Devos et al. 2009), in which they were used to hypothesize an input of combusted turf or allochtonous sediments from wet areas. They were also mentioned in manured urban ploughsoils by Vrydaghs et al. (2007) and by Brénot et al. (2013) and interpreted as being indicators of the input of allochtonous sediments from moist habitats. In occupation layers with evidence of ploughing and puddling from the medieval motte of Werken (Belgium), their presence was used to infer high humidity and wet conditions (Gebhardt & Langohr 1999). Brochier (2002, 2005) used the ratio between diatom remains and stomatocysts (from grain mounts in refractive liquid) in Neolithic levels at the Fontbrégoua cave (southern France) to infer rapid eutrophication linked to anthropic impact.

# Opal Sponge Spicules

Luc Vrydaghs1,2

<sup>1</sup> Centre de Recherches en Archéologie et Patrimoine, Université Libre de Bruxelles, Bruxelles, Belgium  
$^{2}$  Research Team in Archaeo- and Palaeosciences, Brussels, Belgium

# 20.1 Introduction

Spicules are accumulated by a variety of organisms (i.e., echinoderms, holothurians, Porifera) and generally result from genetically controlled biomineralization processes. Within sponges (Porifera), fresh-water sponges (Spongillidae, class Demospongiae) produce only opal A spicules  $(\mathrm{SiO}_2\mathrm{n}(\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}))$ , whereas marine sponges (Demonspongiae and Hexactinellida) accumulate opal or calcareous  $(\mathrm{CaCO}_3)$  spicules. The present chapter deals only with opal sponge spicules.

The reader is referred to Boury-Esnault & Rutzler (1997 - under 'Spicules of Demospongiae') for a detailed account of sponge spicule morphology. Based on size, most taxonomists distinguish megascleres  $(>100\mu \mathrm{m})$  from microscleres  $(< 100\mu \mathrm{m}$  with a lower size limit of about  $10\mu \mathrm{m}$ ). However, size does not seem to be a satisfying taxonomical criterion, as environmental factors can also influence the spicule size (Uriz et al. 2003). Intact opal spicules show either blunt, rounded, spear-shaped or knobby extremities. Despite being under genetic control (Uriz et al. 2003; Gutierrez-Castorena & Effland 2010), some spicules might deviate from basic types and/or combine pointed and rounded ends and bear spines (Uriz et al. 2003). Megascleres can also present environment-related morphological variations (ecomorphic features). As such, megascleres can present a wide array of morphologies. Based on morphological criteria, saltwater and freshwater megascleres cannot be discriminated.

Silica microscleres present showel-like, blunt, rounded or pointed ends. They can also be 'C' or 'S' shaped as well as star shaped with rays radiating from a centre (which is sometimes spherical). Under the microscope, some star-shaped microscleres could be confused with phytoliths known as globular echinates (ICPN Working Group 2005), typical of Palmae taxa.

# 20.2 Micromorphology

Spicules are most often imported from the local geological substrate through reworking of the parent material. However, these fossils can also be transported by water, wind, animals or humans. Sponge spicules have been observed by soil micromorphology in archaeological or man-made soils (Mücher et al. 1990; Vrydaghs et al. 2007; Arroyo-Kalin et al. 2009; Devos et al. 2009) and in archaeological stratigraphic units (Matthews 2010; Gur Arieb et al. 2014). They have been reported in earth-based construction materials (Figure 20.1), in herbivore dung (ingested with drinking water – Shahack-Gross 2011) and as temper in pottery (see Arroyo-Kalin 2017, this book). They can also be found in their primary depositional setting in ditches, moats and canals (Figure 20.2), or in archaeological deposits formed in palustrine or lacustrine environments.

Opal spicules are colourless and optically isotropic with a low negative relief. However, these are not absolute criteria for identification. Longitudinally, opal sponge spicules often show straight extremities, indicating breakage. Their appearance in thin section depends also on the way they are cut (transverse or longitudinal directions). In most cases, an axial canal is recorded (Figures 20.1 and 20.2). The latter relate to their formation process, i.e. mineral precipitation along a proteinic filament (Sethman & Wörheide 2008).

Micromorphology only allows for a generic identification of opal spicules. Determination of species has not so far been carried out from soil thin sections. When reworked from older deposits, spicules are sometimes converted into more stable forms of silica (i.e., low cristobalite) or are subject to epigeny. The contemporaneous recording of the presence of sponge spicules, diatoms, chrysophyceae and phytoliths can provide information on the preservation condition of silica of biological origin in the deposit.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/95f843aba7ba4f462a178d7f64eaaec43292b3ec03e4ab8c9f38b6918efc67ea.jpg)  
Figure 20.1 Sponge spicules cut parallel to their axial canal within daub made with lagoonal carbonate muds. Punic site of Pauli Stincus, Italy (see Nicosia et al. 2013), PPL. Image courtesy of C. Nicosia.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/1f0835edf8415f43181af2fb766e3ba0a88d5fa440b2bcae22a7b2fa1722e54b.jpg)  
Figure 20.2 Example of sponge spicule cut along parallel to its major axis. Note the central canal. Fine-grained infilling of a ditch, Bronze Age site of Castello del Tartaro (northern Italy). PPL. Image courtesy of C. Nicosa.

# 21

# Burnt Soils and Sediments

Astrid Röpke<sup>1</sup> and Carlo Dietl<sup>2</sup>

$^{1}$  Labor für Archäobotanik, Ur- und Frühgeschichte, Universität zu Köln, Germany  
$^{2}$  Gesteinslabor Dr. Eberhard Jahns, Heilbad Heiligenstadt, Germany

# 21.1 Introduction

Burning leads to long-lasting transformations of soils and sediments, which can be used to identify and reconstruct fire-related activities. The durability of heat-induced alterations is a crucial advantage, especially when byproducts of primary combustion (ash, charred plant material etc.) are lost due to postdepositional processes (Goldberg & Macphail 2006; Goldberg et al. 2009; Miller et al. 2009; Mallol et al. 2017, this book). Common heat effects are reddening (also referred to as rubification or rubefaction), sintering (vitrification, partial melting) and melting. Reddening occurs frequently in and around fireplaces and hearths (see Mentzer 2014; Mallol et al. 2017, this book). Mallol et al. (2013) demonstrated that reddening primarily develops on inorganic substrates, as confirmed by previous experimental studies (e.g., Canti & Linford 2000; Berna et al. 2007) or by evidence from wildfire (Ulery & Graham 1993; Mataix-Solera 2011), although literature on forest fires mostly focuses on ecological effects of burning such as soil degradation and vegetation changes (Whelan 1995; Bertini 2005; Quintana et al. 2007). Micromorphological indicators of clearance by fire are discussed in Deak et al. 2017, this book, and in references therein.

Production processes related to metal and glass manufacture or smithing involve higher temperatures  $(>1000^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ , which lead to sintering of soils and sediments. Geoarchaeological studies dealing with sintering are rare, as most of them concentrate on the remains of manufacturing such as slags and hammer scale (see Angelini et al. 2017, this book). An exception is the study by Mathieu & Stoops (1972), which describes a heat-induced zonal distinction from reddening to sintering of a kiln wall indicating higher temperatures employed by the firing process. The highest temperatures  $(>1200^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  can be also documented in burnt

architectural remains and result in the melting of soils and sediments. Intentional fires destroyed many settlement sites, for example Borsippa in Iraq (Böhmer & Kaufmann 1980). In prehistoric times, numerous burnt sites known as 'vitrified forts' occurred in Europe (Kresten & Ambrosiani 1992). The case study of the site of Bernstorf serves as an example for this widespread phenomenon. Archaeological materials from the site were studied and compared with experimentally heated samples (see also Röpke & Dietl 2014). Partially molten sediments are also described by Eliyahu-Behar et al. (2008) and Würfel et al. (2010) in secondary deposits within pits.

# 21.2 Physical, Chemical, Mineralogical and Micromorphological Changes

# 21.2.1 Reddening

Reddening of the iron-bearing soils and sediments is due to the oxidation of  $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$  to  $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$  and/or to the loss of water in iron hydroxides (mainly goethite FeOOH), leading to the formation of hematite (Figures 21.1, 21.2a and 21.2b). Gualtieri & Venturelli (1999) suggest that reddening starts at ca.  $250^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , Berna et al. (2007) at ca.  $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , whilst Mathieu & Stoops (1972) assume it does not take place before  $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . According to our own experiments, weak reddening features are visible at  $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (Figure 21.3). However, not all materials show reddening even at higher temperatures (Canti & Linford 2000; Berna et al. 2007). Some of the reasons given are the differences in organic matter content or chemical variations. In soils and sediments rich in organic matter, carbon compounds vanish as a result of combustion. Moldic voids are micromorphologically visible, showing

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/49e1581f9a16202e360b847dbdf5a514bfa504f1750d4b40a292d474c64f48b0.jpg)  
Figure 21.1 Reddened loam from the burnt Bronze Age fortification of Bernstorf (Germany - see Röpke & Dietl 2014). Hematite acts as cement in the matrix. The coarse fraction is mostly composed of quartz (q). The overall fabric is aligned due to the construction process. PPL.

that the plant material has disappeared and only phyto-liths are left (Shillito et al. 2011).

Tropical, reddened soils containing hematite are mostly difficult to distinguish from thermally reddened materials (Marcelino et al. 2010; Stoops & Marcelino 2010). Gradations in the degree of reddening from light brownish to red can nevertheless be used to infer that exposure to heat took place (Figure 21.2). Further analytical methods can be employed in order to ascertain heat-induced reddening. These comprise X-ray powder diffraction (XRD) (see Mathieu & Stoops 1972; Gebhard et al. 2004; Röpke & Dietl 2014), Mössbauer spectrometry (Gebhard et al. 2004) and FTIR microscopy (see Berna 2017, this book).

# 21.2.2 Sintering

Sintering can be defined as the formation of a solid mass from a fine-grained mineral aggregate at temperatures below the melting point of a rock - i.e. without

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/52629640845c6c2a45dd96d902628cc2b861e382cc6fd7d212bd47e4d1cc2929.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/37267e0bc78beff77607e9adccf2a6d0c6e6f1a14cffb465c76755d92dbc5060.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/bbfd04de2ba4b5bf19b2ee32dea73c726721fd683b51d692d3cb50fec72ebce4.jpg)  
Figure 21.2 Fragment from a burnt furnace wall of the Nok culture, Nigeria (Rupp et al. 2008). (a) Overview of the transition zone: reddened matrix close to the heat source (left) with a sharp boundary to the loam not affected by heat. PPL. (b) Detail of dense hematite-rich matrix. PPL. (c) Detail of the unaltered zone showing fresh unaltered quartz and feldspar, suggesting the absence of lateritization. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/f7a9a7d4d842f4ad20ea1994c471ba93c9b45e8c439dd5f06142e98fa25e412b.jpg)  
Figure 21.3 Slightly reddened loam from Bernstorf (Germany), experimentally heated at  $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  for  $24\mathrm{h}$ . Most clay coatings are affected by heat. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/99cd9f4ee04c606e10dcc5733ade0b6ff59a687a9ef193d92e8a1004f587ec80.jpg)  
Figure 21.4 Overview of partially vitrified (arrows) material from the Bronze Age fortification at Bernstorf (Germany - see Röpke & Dietl 2014). The sintered parts appear dark grey and contain vesicles (v) and quartz grains (q). Hematite is replaced by grey / black magnetite due to partially reducing conditions. PPL.

liquefaction (German 1996). According to Deer et al. (2013), vesicle formation and an increase in porosity can take place during sintering due to the presence of a free volatile phase, mainly water. The vesicles are situated within a partially vitrified matrix (Figure 21.4). In sintered layers rich in organic matter, partially molten phytoliths (Figure 21.5a) or even silica slags from phytoliths can be present (Figure 21.5b). Vitrified material appears optically isotropic (Figure 21.5c). In places, microcracks occur in quartz and the presence of a thin glassy matrix film around vesicles and quartz grains is also observable (Figure 21.6). Whether melting takes place depends on the composition of the soil or sediment, namely on the presence and abundance of water-bearing minerals such

as clays and micas. Additionally fluxing by NaCl or CaO can influence vitrification. The starting point of partial melting therefore ranges from 1100 to  $1300^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Amphibolite vitrifies at  $1130^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , shown by XRD (Kresten & Ambrosiani 1992) and basalt needs around  $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (Baitinger & Kresten 2012; using thin section analysis and calculation of crystallization temperatures). Berna et al. (2007) state that the melting of siliceous soils and rocks starts at  $1300^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (with FTIR spectroscopy). Mathieu & Stoops (1972) report sintering of a loam (loess) at  $1100^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  upon experimental burning and through XRD analyses. Experiments with loam (upper Tertiary molasse) from Bernstorf (Bavaria, Germany) yielded partial melting at  $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (Figure 21.7) and almost complete melting at  $1400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (see below). These temperatures were also estimated by Mossbauer measurements of archaeological burnt material from the same temperatures (Gebhard et al. 2004).

# 21.2.3 Melting

Samples exposed to  $\mathrm{T} > 1300^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  generally melt (Kresten & Ambrosiani 1992). They appear optically isotropic in thin section (Figures 21.8 and 21.9). Later alteration might lead to the formation of tightly intergrown clay minerals (Velde 1995) at the expenses of the unstable glass phase, a phenomenon well known from volcanic glasses (Karakaş & Kadir 2000). Usually, newly crystallizing phases do not have time to form euhedral crystals but cool rapidly, forming skeletal and dendritic individuals. This is particularly observable in slags containing dendritic magnetite grains overgrowing fayalite lattices (see Angelini et al. 2017, this book). Another index mineral for the high-temperature (HT) regime is quartz, which is frequently used as an index mineral for deformation processes in a framework (e.g., MacKenzie et al. 1982; Schmid & Casey 1986). The main micromorphological features of quartz under HT conditions are microcracks, which form an irregular pattern within individual quartz grains (Figure 21.10). These microcracks can be distinguished from fracturation of quartz in lateritic tropical soils since those are accompanied by striations, etch features and dissolution pits (Eswaran & Stoops 1978).

# 21.3 Experiments at Different Temperature Regimes

Most experimental studies on the effects of fire on soils and sediments focus on reddening (Canti & Linford 2000; Miller et al. 2009; Mallol et al. 2013). Few experimental studies also include temperatures above  $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (Mathieu & Stoops 1972; Berna et al. 2007) but

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/d4c276d703eeb6a9bed55af9e8f7275af10fd38cbdbb46d415800733a2bbdb5c.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/c15700ca1bbab6a73e1f07cfb5ce9dcaa51a6254c443886c83406817a2ef7690.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/04aaba2678cc2a1564cb8fc982cf099cb621708ad718d3f4902ad60793a198f2.jpg)  
Figure 21.5 (a) Overview of an occupation layer with molten phytoliths from the Bronze Age settlement mound of Niederröblingen (Harz, central Germany – see Lubos et al. 2013), PPL. (b) Detail of (a). Greyish vitrified material (silica slag from melting of opal phytoliths) with vesicles, PPL. (c) Same as (b), XPL. Note optical isotropy of the vitrified material.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/88279f0cb99a32a12505b4dddbd4867167fb933b471a455aa19b919a18a231c6.jpg)  
Figure 21.6 Sediments from the Bronze Age fortification at Bernstorf (Germany - see Röpke & Dietl 2014): microcracks within quartz grains from the almost-vitrified sample (arrow). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/46574c20dba483c774be7db0eecc76039c79c5c0cd90eb17080b3282137f2fba.jpg)  
Figure 21.7 Vitrified loam from Bernstorf (Germany), experimentally heated at  $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  for  $24\mathrm{h}$ . The almost-vitrified portion shows numerous vesicles within a dark-grey to black matrix. PPL.

unfortunately no micrographs are presented. To bridge the gap we concentrated our experiment at Bernstorf on temperature ranges between  $600 - 1400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  and compared it to Mossbauer spectrometry and XRD from the same site (Gebhard et al. 2004).

Four sediment samples from the same block were taken in the surroundings of the Bronze Age vitrified fortification at Bernstorf (Bavaria, Germany) and were air dried to carry out heating experiments. The samples consisted of dull yellowish brown (10YR4/3) loamy sand with a porphyric related distribution pattern, with clay coatings and charcoal (Figure 21.10). They were heated at  $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ,  $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ,  $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (oxygen atmosphere,  $24\mathrm{h}$  heating) and  $1400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (oxygen atmosphere,  $3\mathrm{h}$  heating) in

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/adfe70a62b95e9edbcb4b528c02b14a05db810a6c1a69ac4e2e8196845c550bf.jpg)  
Figure 21.8 Molten soil from a medieval smithy (Montafon, Austria - see Röpke & Krause 2013). Glassy film around large vesicles, iron oxides, and irregular pattern of microcracks within quartz, typical for a HT regime, are visible. PPL.

a muffle furnace. After  $3\mathrm{h}$  the sample at  $1400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  was completely molten. The results of the experiments are summarized in Table 21.1.

According to our experiments, reddening of sediments can occur from at least  $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  up to  $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (Figure 21.11). Sintering and vitrification  $(1000^{\circ}\mathrm{C} - 1200^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  are characterized by the first occurrence of glass seams around individual crystals (optically isotropic), vesicles and microcracks in quartz. The general colour of these samples is grey, macroscopically and in thin section (PPL). At temperatures above  $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , melting features prevail: the sample consists almost entirely of glass and vesicles, and quartz remains, dissected by microcracks. Moreover, newly growing Fe oxides exhibit dendritic shape due to rapid cooling.

# 21.4 Conclusion

Reddening, sintering and melting are heat-induced alterations of soils and sediments, which can be clearly recognized in thin section. They are durable and can help to distinguish fire activities on archaeological sites. However, many of the effects of fire on soils and sediments are still poorly understood. Different texture and mineralogy as well as varying amounts of organic material might influence burning conditions (oxidation/reduction), temperature regime and processes. For example, high amounts of organic matter might lead to reducing burning conditions, whereas its absence might cause reddening. This can even happen within the same archaeological structure. Apart from this, the firing time should also be taken into account. Further studies on

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/0145faebed8b201f78d58f708f76e6851f874f2958d3c0e43f25d3a3e829bb68.jpg)

(a) Brownish, probably iron-rich, striae in the matrix and newly grown magnetite crystals forming agglomerates. PPL. (b) Detail showing the almost entirely molten sediment. The only nonmolten phase is quartz with numerous microcracks. PPL.

# Burnt Carbonates

Matthew G. Canti

Historic England, Eastney, United Kingdom

# 22.1 Introduction

Burnt carbonates have occasionally been recorded in the archaeological literature but only rarely in micromorphological samples. Apart from calcareous mortars and plasters (Stoops et al. 2017, this book), the main examples are shell-tempered pottery (e.g., Canti 2007a) and burnt molluscs (e.g., Murphy 2003). Underidentification may in part be due to problems of recognition, particularly with the lower temperature materials. This chapter therefore examines four major calcium carbonate materials - chalk, mollusc shell, earthworm granules and eggshell under a variety of temperature regimes in order to show the range of morphologies presented.

# 22.2 Thermal Diagenesis of Carbonate Materials

Two basic types of alteration occur in burnt carbonates.

# 22.2.1 Scorching

At moderate temperatures between  $300^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  and about  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , any organic matter within the carbonate structure starts to burn. If oxidation is incomplete, brown scorching is preserved, making the microscopic view browner and darker in PPL and XPL equally, but more noticeably in PPL. The main organic matter in carbonates is the protein content of biominerals, so browning is most likely to be found in eggshell, mollusc shell and earthworm granules, where protein contents are commonly  $0.03\% - 0.2\%$  (Addadi et al. 1991; Canti 2007b). There is some indication that this type of scorching enhances preservation (Murphy 2003).

# 22.2.2 Calcining

At higher temperatures, usually above ca.  $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , calcining takes place with the  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  expelled from the  $\mathrm{CaCO}_{3}$  lattice, leaving pure isotropic CaO. On rewetting, the CaO reacts violently with water to produce  $\mathrm{Ca(OH)}_{2}$ , which has moderate birefringence (0.029) and shows second-order interference colours at 30 microns thickness. After that, normal  $\mathrm{CaCO}_{3}$  slowly reforms in moist conditions, as atmospheric  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  is absorbed. This is the same basic process as lime burning for mortar production, and further information can be found in Stoops et al. (2017, this book).

# 22.3 Carbonate Materials from Different Temperature Regimes

# 22.3.1 Optical Properties of Modern Experimental Materials

All materials were heated in oxidizing conditions in a muffle furnace in ceramic containers of about  $1\mathrm{cm}$  in height. Temperature was brought up to the target as quickly as possible without cracking, and then cooled naturally.

# 22.3.1.1 Burnt Chalk

Chalk is visually unchanged by 30 min heating up to  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (Figure 22.1a and b). If heated for 1 h at  $900^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , chalk undergoes calcination. When the CaO is rewetted, there is a violent exothermic reaction causing considerable disruption and splitting of the chalk fabric. Figure 22.1b and c show calcined chalk rewetted in a small pot without any surrounding material to support

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/0b36064b83f92c6822f48dc21458c0152c3a81b17ba3fb65e704cf9c1ba517bd.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/9c351a304e290228096ecc3f1d3768a202aa5fbf9ff4baa01c644991484dd8a2.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/5552b586a8be50968c7ca0ed5ef3ec752abe9d999ed8d71a08f2043aac4abc51.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/21a32aee74dc264a2c4b5a55f83a6ba539ce804a46f8264f782a1081a62ffaa6.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/9ceb3888bc13c42ba91a291dd335f5445272c998089fd85785968381a8d83627.jpg)  
Figure 22.1 (a) and (b) Chalk heated for  $30\mathrm{min}$  at  $200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  and at  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (both PPL). (c) and (d) Chalk burnt for  $1\mathrm{h}$  at  $900^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  then rewetted (for 10 days) in a pot without support: (c) PPL; (d) XPL. (e) and (f) Chalk burnt for  $1\mathrm{h}$  at  $900^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  then rewetted in plastic clay to provide support: (e) PPL; (f) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/091e864307702f8562ba817c43dc394e90dbfe3783babf133b1b2728f6450927.jpg)

it as it reacted. Figure 22.1e and f show the same material but rewetted by clamping between two plastic clay pellets, offering some constraint on the reacting mass, as if immediate burial had occurred before recarbonation.

In both the supported and the unsupported experiments, the recarbonating material was kept moist for 10 days then dried for 1 week before drying and resin impregnation.

In both cases, the violent reaction has led to fragmentation of the CaO into zones that have then recarbonated progressively from the fissures between them where the air can enter more freely.

In the unsupported treatment (Figures 22.1c and d), loose chaotic zoning has allowed greater gaseous penetration and a high proportion of the fabric has become  $\mathrm{CaCO}_3$  again. Quite thick extensive micrite areas have developed. In the clay-supported treatment (Figures 22.1e and f), a denser mass of zones has resisted recarbonation for longer, leaving large isotropic CaO centres to each zone, with only fringes of micrite.

Due to the timespans available, archaeological examples would mostly be fully recarbonated and the isotropic zone-centres in these laboratory simulations would therefore have disappeared. However, they do persist in some calcareous mortars (Stoops et al. 2017, this book). The zonation also clearly acts as a focus for crystallization, and the fissures between each zone would probably allow impurities from the stratigraphy to wash in over time. It is therefore likely that, even where it is not preserved, some recognizable remains of this zonation would still be visible.

# 22.3.1.2 Burnt Mollusc Shell

Mollusc shell heated for 30 minutes undergoes progressive darkening between  $200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  and  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  but is otherwise unchanged (Figures 22.2a-d).

If heated for  $1\mathrm{h}$  at  $900^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  mollusc shell is calcined and, when recarbonated, it retains much of its original shape so the shell fragments are often still recognizable (Figures 22.2e and f).

Research by G. Stoops (personal communication) suggests that fluorescence is in some cases strongly decreased by burning; this is an area where further research could usefully be carried out.

# 22.3.1.3 Burnt Biospheroids produced by

# Earthworms

Earthworm biospheroids are more-or-less unchanged by heating up to  $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  for  $30\mathrm{min}$  (Figures 22.3a-d). By  $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  they brown slightly and they are dark brown by  $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (Figures 22.3e and f). By  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  they start to calcine (Figures 22.4a and b).

If earthworm biospheroids are burnt for  $1\mathrm{h}$  at  $900^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  then left for 10 days' moist recarbonation inside a clay pellet for support, very fine cryptocrystalline calcium carbonate reforms on the outside of the granule (Figure 22.4f) with the interior still retaining some of the lower interference colours of  $\mathrm{Ca(OH)_2}$ . In archaeological situations, the  $\mathrm{Ca(OH)_2}$  would, of course, completely recarbonate but it is not known whether the extremely fine grained calcium carbonate seen in Figure 22.4 would survive or recrystallize.

# 22.3.1.4 Burnt Eggshell

Eggshell heated up to  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (see Figures 22.5a–f) undergoes relatively minor changes. Very little happens up to  $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  but by  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , there is about  $10\%$  shrinkage and a slight increase in the definition of the individual palisade crystals (see Canti 2017, this book).

If heated to  $900^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  for  $1\mathrm{h}$  and recarbonated, eggshell loses most of its definition, and becomes a mass of cryptocrystalline  $\mathrm{CaCO_3}$  (Figures  $22.5\mathrm{g},\mathrm{h}$ ).

# 22.4 Archaeological Examples

Burnt chalk would be commonly found around lime kilns but examples have not been located. Burnt mollusc shell was recorded at Gilberd School, Colchester, where cockle shells (Cerastoderma edule), a few oysters (Ostrea edulis) and mussels (Mytilus edulis) were actively used as a raw material for lime-burning (Murphy 1992). At Riparo Mochi, Italy, Stiner (1999) found mild burning in up to  $18\%$  of the edible bivalves recovered, suggesting that fire was used to open them up for eating.

As well as the above examples, there is a special case of burnt mollusc occurring in thin sections of shell-tempered pottery (Figure 22.6). In prehistory, shell was commonly added to pottery clays to confer strength (Steponaitis 1983; Feathers 1989; Feathers & Scott 1989). Although there are problems with its decomposition at high temperatures and volume changes attendant on recarbonation (Rye 1976), shell has the advantage over quartz that its platy particles resist crack propagation. Furthermore, calcite expands on heating in a similar way to clay, whereas quartz goes through a volume increase around  $573^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (Hoard et al. 1995).

No archaeological examples have been found of either burnt biospheroids produced by earthworms or burnt eggshell.

# 22.5 Conclusions

It is not possible to do a complete set of temperatures, durations and recarbonations, which would enable all the different morphologies to be experimentally recreated. Burnt carbonates will remain a difficult area for interpretation because of the variations possible within the basic cycles of heating, cooling and rewetting investigated here. The problems posed by different forms of recrystallization add another layer of complexity. It is important, however, for micromorphologists to grasp the fundamentals of the systems in order to know the range of morphologies that they might encounter, and thus avoid simplistic interpretations.

# 23

# Calcareous Mortars, Plasters and Floors

Georges Stoops<sup>1</sup>, Matthew G. Canti<sup>2</sup> and Selim Kapur<sup>3</sup>

Vakgroep Geologie, Faculteit Wetenschappen, Universiteit Gent, Belgium  
$^{2}$  Historic England, Eastney, United Kingdom  
$^{3}$  Department of Archaeometry, University of Cukurova, Adana, Turkey

# 23.1 Introduction

The term 'mortar' is used here in a broad sense, covering not only the material used for joining building stones (mortar sensu stricto) but also the material used for joining pipes, the groundmass of concrete (opus caementitium) and the basis for wall and floor plasters.

Calcareous mortars have been used since prehistoric times. In many Neolithic sites of the Levant, lime floors as old as 12 ky BC have been excavated (Gourdin & Kingery 1975; Matthews 2005; Kingery et al. 1988; Elsen 2006; Goren & Goring-Morris 2008). In pre-Columbian America lime mortars were also extensively used (Barba et al. 2009; Villaseñor & Price 2008; Villaseñor & Graham 2010). Hydraulic mortars of the first millennium BC were reported by Regev et al. (2010) in Israel.

The preparation of calcareous mortars can be summarized as follows: limestone  $\left(\mathrm{CaCO}_{3}\right)$  is calcined (between 800 and  $1000^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ ) in a kiln to produce quicklime  $(\mathrm{CaO})$ . Slaked with water, quicklime is converted into hydrated lime  $\left(\mathrm{Ca(OH)}_{2}\right)$ . By reaction with the  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  from the air this is transformed gradually again to  $\mathrm{CaCO}_{3}$ . More details are given in Gourdin & Kingery (1975); see also Canti 2017, this book. The duration of the calcining process depends upon the raw material used: porous limestone or tuff reacts faster than dense, compact limestone. Other possible raw materials are calcareous soil material and shells (e.g., Villaseñor & Price 2008). After slaking, a temper is added to the lime (binder) to improve its strength, reduce shrinkage and for economic reasons, temper being cheaper. For more details the reader is referred to Rapp (2002). Experimental work on the preparation of lime plasters, combined with micromorphology, was done by Karkanas (2007) and Goren & Goring-Morris (2008). Detailed instructions for the preparation of mortars and plasters in Roman times were

given by several ancient authors, especially Vitruvius. Excellent overviews of this literature are given in Blake (1947) and Lamprecht (1993).

Calcined lime was not only used in architectural constructions, but examples of it were found also in containers and vessels (so-called 'white ware' - Ichumbaki & Pollard 2015), plastered skulls (in Jericho) and beads (Kingery et al. 1988). Quicklime has also been used in some burial sites (Masset & Van Vliet 1974).

# 23.2 Micromorphology

The use of petrographic techniques is especially important in cases where the temper contains limestone or marble fragments, as chemical analyses cannot distinguish  $\mathrm{CaCO_3}$  derived from cement or from temper. This is especially the case when  $^{14}\mathrm{C}$  dating of the binder is considered. The earliest systematic and detailed published micromorphological description of a number of Roman mortars we could trace was that by Stoops (1984), although earlier unpublished research exists (Gourdin 1974). The earliest SEM studies are those of Gourdin & Kingery (1975). Additional information on composition can be obtained using more sophisticated analytical techniques, such as micro XRF (e.g., Burgio et al. 2007; Mentzer & Quade 2013) or Raman microprobe analysis (Burgio et al. 2007; Goodall et al. 2007).

The microscopic study of mortars can be divided in two groups: one dealing with fabric and components, similar to petrographic or micromorphological studies, and the other focussing on the measurement of porosity (e.g., after impregnation with a fluorescent dye). The first method gives information on the origin of the constituents, the techniques used and later alterations, whereas

the second method yields more information on stability, risks of weathering, and so forth.

Quantification of constituents is possible by point counting (e.g., Stoops, 1984, Mertens et al. 2007). Quantification of porosity in thin sections can be based on several parameters: total porosity, pore size distribution, and pore shape. Data can be obtained by point or line counting, or image analysis of samples impregnated with fluorescent resin (Elsen 2006; CON-C1 2001). Discussion of the methodology is beyond the frame of this contribution.

# 23.2.1 Mortars: General Characteristics

Apart from the basic coarse (called temper or aggregate) and the fine (called binder) constituents, additives and accidental components form the groundmass of mortars. The grain size of the temper is generally smaller than  $4\mathrm{mm}$ , but may be quite variable as mainly local sands were used. Sorting and shape also depend on the provenance: for example rounded, well sorted grains of dune and shore sands, subangular and weakly sorted grains of some river beds or residual sands. Occasionally fragments of chalk have been described (Mathieu 1971). In the case of concrete (e.g., opus caementitium) larger pebbles are added. The petrographic composition of the temper yields important information on the source.

Reactive tempers: tempers that react chemically with the strongly alkaline lime are used to prepare hydraulic mortars. They can harden without reaction with  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ , for instance under water. They are impermeable, and were used for constructions in harbours (Tsatskin 1999), aqueducts, baths and so forth. The most common temper is natural unstable volcanic glass (pozzolana, or

pulvis Puteolanus) that reacts with the formation of silicates, responsible for the hardening, and used since the fifth or fourth century BC (Figure 23.1). The coarse grains are mainly optically isotropic volcanic glass, high in Si, sometimes containing volcanic minerals (Villasenor & Graham 2010). Other, nonvolcanic natural reactive materials are, for instance, diatomaceous earth. Reaction rims of calcium-silicate-hydrate (C-S-H) phases were observed around the pozzolana by Miriello et al. (2010a) using SEM and EDS. Binici et al. (2010) assign the earthquake resistance of Ottoman buildings to the C-S-H amorphous phases formed.

For the same purposes ground ceramic materials (cocciopesto) such as tile fragments, pottery or calcined clay have been used since Hellenistic times (Rapp 2002) (Figure 23.2). These mortars, called opus signinum, have a pink colour, and reaction rims in the binder surrounding tile fragments were documented by Rentzel (1997). Milling of the ceramic fragments yields better hydraulic characteristics (Miriello et al. 2010b), a technique already known by the classic architects who advised beating the lime/ceramic mixture for a long time. Opus signinum was used in walls and floors as a waterproof mortar / temper, especially in aqueducts (Surmelihindi et al. 2013) and as wall coatings in cisterns all over the Mediterranean during a period covering a large part of the Late Roman / Byzantine to the Medieval periods.

In some cases straw (Binici et al. 2010) or animal hair were added to improve the tensile strength of mortar (Figures 23.3a and b). Charcoal may be an accidental component (Tsatskin 1999) from the calcination process (Rapp 2002) or added as a pigment. Shell fragments (Figure 23.4) may be part of the temper, if sea or river sand was used (Mestdagh 1990, 1991a), or uncalcined
