![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/2eecb07f88305ac43f8703afe05a67b52d215fe9400ece773d8e3af9cae87601.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/aaa30b7cedd264ff10b587c248d9008d5eb75a5d473a6d9ad690139cc0b03864.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/d6c35ac4a93ad72403527ad44bf9a8e8a222dc22d7d2c639e90970ef7ca34643.jpg)  
Figure 23.3 SEM images: (a) Chaff (see Binici et al. 2010); (b) Goat hair. Ottoman mortars of Eastern Anatolia.

residues of the lime preparation when shells were used instead of limestone. Shells that have been heated show a clear change of internal fabric and sometimes a considerable reduction or loss of autofluorescence (see also Canti 2017, this book). Reuse of fragments of old mortars was documented in several cases, for example by Miriello et al. (2011).

Accidental components in the coarse fraction (e.g., bone fragments, plant and soil fragments, potsherds, slag fragments etc. - see Mestdagh 1994) (Figure 23.5), can be very important for determining the cultural and environmental context.

The use of Mg-rich limestone or dolostone does not result in the formation of a mixture of calcite and magnesite but in the presence of hydromagnesite features (see below); magnesite grains should therefore be considered as an admixture to increase the quality of the plaster (Nardini et al. 2007).

The fine material (micromass or binder) is composed of calcite with a very fine grain size – finer than most natural micrites (Gourdin & Kingery 1975); this is a diagnostic characteristic (Figure 23.6). Scanning electron microscope (SEM) studies reveal  $0.3 - 1\mu \mathrm{m}$  spherical particles of calcite, unless the material aged in humid conditions (Kingery et al. 1988). Compared to natural fine calcite cement, or calcareous mud (lutum mortars), the absence of microfossils, destroyed during heating, is diagnostic (Stoops 1984; Goren & Goldberg 1991) (Table 23.1).

Hydraulic mortars can also be prepared by calcining very calcareous clays, resulting in a natural cement. According to Elsen (2006) these mortars can be

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/7a2a99a140981d8e89e8390c92009b40eb6480c780aa5690b2f7a13d6a519ad5.jpg)  
Figure 23.4 Shell fragments as part of temper in Roman mortar, Aardenburg (Netherlands - see Mestdagh 1990, 1991): (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/3cac15e68934cf0d2d857dc2ecbadc2df8e8bd93d52e737903f91db2b215923a.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/bb13c3c07334a9b72b834d7cb5246df18252067d78b6b90ec62f8dc61f22b374.jpg)  
Figure 23.5 Optically isotropic slag with vesicles in opus signinum. Tielrode (Belgium - see Mestdagh 1990, 1991): (a) PPL; (b) XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/12905cbf78d0c7218b839da420dfec39ade583611c99de13e6ec38710d4759b4.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/ad675ea702d4d72629e09a01ea133cb41ce2aee6c12f2c9e95d87cdfed3d722c.jpg)  
Figure 23.6 Very fine, almost cryptocrystalline calcite in micromass of cement used for joining pipes. Note the general parallel arrangement and the calcite crystallization in the elongated voids: (a) PPL; (b) XPL. Roman age, Pessinus (Turkey - see Stoops 1984).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/189536a0773720de4300b9c08ff2c523c43b858ff8e880e8dd327b84c12f8649.jpg)

Table 23.1 Differentiation of the micromorphological characteristics of calcareous mortars from those of calcareous sandstones and conglomerates.  

<table><tr><td>Properties</td><td>Lime mortar</td><td>Calcareous lutum mortar</td><td>Calcareous sandstone and conglomerate</td></tr><tr><td>Micromass</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Composition</td><td>calcitic, very fine grain size, linked</td><td>argilo-calcitic; micritic grain size</td><td>calcitic; micritic to sparitic</td></tr><tr><td>Inclusions</td><td>absent</td><td>phytoliths, spiculae, diatoms, microfossils, organic remains</td><td>microfossils</td></tr><tr><td>Fissures</td><td>frequent</td><td>absent</td><td>?</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3">Additives and accidental components</td><td>absent</td></tr><tr><td>Straw and animal hair</td><td>possible</td><td>possible</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Ceramics</td><td>opus signinum</td><td>rare</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Charcoal</td><td>common</td><td>possible</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Slag</td><td>rare</td><td>rare</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Features</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Reaction rims around minerals</td><td>common</td><td>absent</td><td>absent</td></tr><tr><td>Lime lumps</td><td>common</td><td>absent</td><td>absent</td></tr><tr><td>RecrySTALLIZATION around pores</td><td>frequent</td><td>absent</td><td>possible</td></tr><tr><td>Microstructure</td><td></td><td></td><td>absent</td></tr><tr><td>vughy</td><td>frequent</td><td>possible</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>vesicular</td><td>possible</td><td>possible</td><td></td></tr></table>

recognized in thin sections by a more cloudy limpidity and the presence of nonhydrated cement grains (mainly belite,  $\mathrm{Ca_2SiO_4}$ ).

As a result of incomplete calcining and reaction between temper and binder, several features may be formed. Limestone fragments in the coarse material sometimes show a diffuse boundary with the micromass. Gourdin & Kingery (1975) suggest that these are fragments of incompletely burned limestone, with rims transformed to quick lime. These features were also observed in experimentally calcined limestone and prepared lime plaster (Goren & Goring Morris 2008). Incompletely burned limestone fragments show internal fissuring (Figure 23.7 - see also Canti 2017, this book). They are very important for studying the type and source of limestone used for calcination (e.g., Barba et al. 2009).

Lime lumps are explained as overburnt quicklime, unreacted or half-reacted quicklime or crudely slaked or mixed lime (Karkanas 2007). Poorly reacted quicklime appears as colourless or light beige zones in thin sections with very low relief and almost optically isotropic (Figure 23.8). An incipient reaction leads to very fine, poorly crystallized colourless portlandite  $(\mathrm{Ca(OH)}_2)$  with low relief and lower order grey interference

colours. Larger crystals display first-order blue or second-order red interference colours. Olsen (2004, 2006) points out characteristic rounded shape and porous internal fabric of the poorly reacted quicklime lumps. They are more common in dry slaked lime. Vitruvius warned against producing these small lumps during the preparation of plasters (Lamprecht 1993). Renzel (1997) mentions that mortars containing unreacted quicklime have a higher potential to fill later fissures by calcite crystallization.

Both limestone fragments and lime lumps are not part of the temper and should not be considered as such when calculating the temper/binder ratio but as binder. Residual limestone fragments are responsible for incorrect  $^{14}\mathrm{C}$  dates.

Some minerals may show a clear reaction rim at the contact with the calcite binder. This is frequently the case for instance with glauconite (Figure 23.9) (e.g., Stoops & Stoops 1996).

Pores are often lined by coarser  $(10 - 20\mu \mathrm{m})$  calcite coatings (Figures 23.2, 23.6) (Stoops 1984), formed mostly in a later stage. If common, they may influence  $^{14}\mathrm{C}$  dating. Renzel (1997) describes void infillings by needle shaped calcium-aluminium compounds in Roman opus signinum.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/6b7bdd10d83d2c766b9e91442ef821707951cc5341cc6ae128eb70d39cbf0964.jpg)  
Figure 23.7 Fragment of incompletely calcined limestone with internal fissures and diffuse reaction rim: (a) PPL; (b) XPL. Gravensteen medieval Castle (Gent, Belgium).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/637f70e8fb638c96310ce366dc692f1911cceb6fad779c7227f15f6a155ab3e6.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/3b7aa86ee7d0f2a912f2e57fa49d73722442606d5cb642c42513d2cf4548ecf0.jpg)  
Figure 23.8 Brownish, almost optically isotropic lumps of poorly reacted quicklime: (a) PPL; (b) XPL. Gravensteen medieval Castle (Gent, Belgium).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/157280b6033b3c90fb53814985626ae594c1ab432105dc11751cb990dcb0e864.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/7dbfbd29983474fc622963039e89a705d9fa27663a1072accaff71b690fe9206.jpg)  
Figure 23.9 Reaction rim on glauconite. Medieval wall plaster: (a) PPL; (b) XPL. Sint Pietersabdij (Gent, Belgium – see Stoops & Stoops 1996).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/ecd71f4b042b14956cd866948c835a87bf7ce8fcbb90611f9b353f71769e8d4b.jpg)

In the case of the use of Mg-rich limestone (e.g., dolostone) as primary material for calcining, acicular hydromagnesite may form appearing in thin sections as rosettes (Villaseñor & Price 2008).

# 23.2.2 Mortar Sensu Stricto

Mortar sensu stricto is the material used to join stones in constructions, to fit pipes, or as binder in concrete (opus caementitium).

Grain size and sorting of the temper may be influenced by pretreatment. For example, in Roman constructions the use of screens to sieve the sand, resulting in a better sorting, started in the second century AD (Blake 1947). In Belgium and surrounding areas, the addition of ceramics has been widely used in Roman or late Roman mortars but was not systematically recorded for later periods (Mertens et al. 2007; Mestdagh 1991b; G. Stoops personal observations). In Byzantine and Ottoman constructions in Turkey hydraulic mortars were used commonly, improving the stability of the constructions (Moropulou et al. 2002; Binici et al. 2010).

The c/f related distribution pattern is determined by the amount of temper added to the lime. In most cases it is close to single spaced porphyric. For Roman mortars, several different values recommended by ancient authors for the optimal temper/binder ratios are summarized by Blake (1947). Marcus Porcius Cato suggests 2/1, Plinius 3/1 and Vitruvius 3/1 when quarry sand is used, or 2/1 when marine or fluviatile sand is used. In the latter case he advises the addition of one volume of crushed ceramics to improve the stability. Microscopic studies yield the following ratios by volume: 1.2-2.6 (Stoops 1984) and 0.18-0.55 (Miriello et al. 2010a) for Roman mortars and 0.4-2.6 in Romanesque architecture (Mertens et al. 2007). In Medieval mortars chitonic and gefuric patterns are observed (Stoops unpublished).

In most publications on microscopic studies of mortars and plasters, not using the present micromorphological concepts and terminology, this ratio is called the binder/ aggregate ration (b/a). The measurement and calculation of the latter ratio is explained in detail in COM-C1 (2001).

Fine accommodating irregular planar voids (Figure 23.6), dissecting the micromass, are a diagnostic feature (Stoops 1984, Goren & Goring-Morris 2008), resulting from shrinkage of the binder upon drying (Karkanas 2007). Sometimes this can evolve into circular patterns. The microstructure ranges between vughy and spongy, and voids are randomly distributed and oriented, except for mortars fixing pipes, where parallel arrangements have been noticed (Figure 23.6). A porosity ranging between  $8 - 18\%$  was determined in Roman mortars by point counting (Stoops 1984). Mestdagh (1991a) observed that porosity decreases with increasing amount of ceramic fragments in Roman mortars. At the microscopic scale arrangement of constituents is generally random.

# 23.2.3 Wall Plaster, Render and Stucco

Wall plasters generally consist of four layers: (i) the pictural layers of fine calcite and stain, (ii) the fine calcitic plaster without sand, (iii) the roughcast or mortar and (iv) the substrate. Some of these layers may be composed of sublayers.

For classic Roman plasters, Vitruvius mentions up to six layers: three layers of roughcast and three layers of crushed white marble, decreasing in grain size towards the surface. Stoops (1984) describes Roman plasters (stucco) composed of three layers: a roughcast, an intermediate layer with coarse crushed marble and an external layer with finely ground marble (Figure 23.10).

The temper of the roughcast (Figure 23.10) of wall plasters is comparable to that of mortar sensu stricto but it is generally better sorted and finer (Stoops 1984),

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/576c49cb1bfcbfd6e61511a5c3910ad12b00ab5221d86a9e8be80e0a264f9709.jpg)  
Figure 23.10 Section through stucco. Note the three layers: roughcast (right), cover layer (middle) of crushed marble, and thin pictural layer (left): (a) PPL; (b) XPL. Roman afore, Pessinus (Turkey - see Stoops 1984).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/9fe04cdcf61a627e542999d7e519d2b7268b74fc71ca310796fd928b9dcaf8c0.jpg)

although less well sorted that the plaster itself (Miriello et al. 2010a). The temper of the covering stucco can be composed of fine ground white marble in the case of the stucco of important Roman buildings (Figure 23.10) (Stoops 1984). Ground ceramics were added to prepare pink hydraulic plasters (see section 23.2.1).

The fine material (binder) is comparable to that of the ordinary mortars. White wall plasters of unconsolidated soft lime sediments, composed often of multiple superposed layers, were described by Matthews (2005) in Catalhoyuk (Turkey). They are difficult to distinguish in thin sections from fired lime plasters (Goren & Goldberg 1991; Affonso 1996; Karkanas 2007) but a few possible criteria are given in Table 23.1.

# 23.2.4 Floor Plaster

Floor plasters are amongst the oldest examples of calcined lime used (Rapp 2002; Karkanas 2007; Matthews 2005). They often contain a staining substance, such as finely ground hematite for red floors as observed in Babylonian buildings (Stoops & Stoops 1994). Karkanas (2007) mentions that in prehistoric floor plasters a mixture of soft calcareous sediment and calcined lime is quite common, together with burned dung, bones, mineral material and lime. Vesicles were reported in floor plasters by several authors (e.g., Karkanas 2007).

# 23.3 Alteration of Mortars and Plasters

The exposure of lime plasters to alternating wet and dry conditions over long periods of time, results in a coarser grain size and perhaps a harder and stronger consistency of the binder (Kingery et al. 1988).

At the surface, or near the borders, specific features may form as a result of environmental factors, such as salt efflorescence (Figure 23.11) and neoformation of pyrite (Figure 23.12). Ettringite  $(\mathrm{Ca}_{6}\mathrm{Al}_{2}(\mathrm{SO}_{4})_{3}(\mathrm{OH})_{12}\cdot$ $26\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O})$  has been observed in the Early Ottoman hydraulic mortars (Binici et al., 2010) in Dergah (Dervish Lodge), Ankara (Figure 23.13), whereas amorphous hydraulic formation of calcium silicate hydrate was observed at the interfaces of the crushed-brick powder / binders used for the construction of Justinian's Hagia Sophia.

Dissolution of the binder is a common feature, especially in more humid climates. Root activity can attack the binder when it is underground. The dissolution of mortar and the consequent release of mineral temper in

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/21551ad51e449fd2faf6c8aa53bf5e4b5ed70fc8b991047914384784275b46f3.jpg)  
Figure 23.11 SEM image of Halite crystallization on Byzantine mortar surface in Late Roman/Byzantine Elaiussa Sebaste archaeological site, Southern Anatolia.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/da0fcd66fb0fc1758e8a8d18bd37f86639ddfb2f5c3904af2b86ee90eee7c936.jpg)  
Figure 23.12 Framboidal pyrite intruding on external part of Medieval mortar: (a) PPL; (b) XPL. Gravensteen medieval Castle (Gent, Belgium).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/cb847bd1ffee4eafaedcdd2572fa01212ad35f108c26fa06d180da9a318f7c83.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/99299973cbe33f70de5ed5e12b2b3bb882dd82e4d3eb26d037aaa771e22579c7.jpg)

the deposit is one of the formation processes reported for European dark earths (see Nicosia et al. 2017, this book, and references therein).

# 23.4 Conclusion

Petrographic studies contribute to the understanding of the techniques used, the source of the material and often also to the environmental and cultural context. Thin-section studies enable the distinction of calcined mortars from calcareous lutum mortars as well as from natural rocks (calcareous sandstones, conglomerates –Table 23.1).

The number of detailed microscopic descriptions of calcareous mortars and plasters is too limited to formulate a micromorphological subdivision of mortar types in time and space. Moreover published information deals almost only with Neolithic mortars and plasters of the Levant, Roman mortars and plasters of Turkey, Israel and Belgium, and pre-Columbian plasters in Central America. Information is scarce for other periods and regions.

It is advised that, in future investigations, more attention should be given to detailed information on micromass, temper, c/f ratio, additives and features. Quantitative data are scarce up to now - even semiquantitative estimations. A protocol for the micromorphological description of mortars and plasters would be most useful, taking into account the recommendations formulated in COM-C1 (2001).

(eds) Cinquante-deux Réflexions sur le Proche-Orient ancien Offertes en Hommage à Léon De Meyer. Mesopotamian History and Environment, Occasional Publications, Vol. II. Peeters, Leuven, pp. 477-486.  
Stoops, G. Sr. & Stoops, G. Jr. (1996) Sint-Pieter's Pleister. Studie van twee stalen uit de reftervleugel van de Sint-Pietersabdij. Stadsarcheologie. Bodem en Monumenten in Gent 20, 5-7.  
Sürmelihindi, E. G., Passchier, C. W., Spotl, C. et al. (2013) Laminated carbonate deposits in Roman aqueducts: Origin, processes and implications. Sedimentology 60, 961-982.

Tsatskin, A. (1999) The petrography of hydraulic and other building materials in Caesarea. In: Holum, K. G. Raban, A. & Patrick, J. (eds) Caesarea Papers 2. Journal of Roman Archaeology, Supplementary Series 35, Ann Arbor, pp. 419-429.  
Villaseñor, I. & Graham, E. (2010) The use of volcanic materials for the manufacture of pozzolanic plasters in

the Maya lowlands: a preliminary report. Journal of Archaeological Science 37, 1339-1347.  
Villaseñor, I. & Price, C. A. (2008) Technology and decay of magnesian lime plasters: the sculptures of the funerary crypt of Palenque, Mexico. Journal of Archaeological Science 35, 1030-1039.

# 24

# Gypsic Mortars and Plasters

Georges Stoops<sup>1</sup>, Alexander Tsatskin<sup>2</sup> and Matthew G. Canti<sup>3</sup>

Vakgroep Geologie, Faculteit Wetenschappen, Universiteit Gent, Belgium  
$^{2}$  Zinman Institute of Archaeology, University of Haifa, Haifa, Israel  
3 Historic England, Eastney, United Kingdom

# 24.1 Introduction

Gypsum plasters were used in Neolithic times (Gourdin & Kingery 1975; Matthews et al. 1997; Olsen 2006). In Pharaonic Egypt gypsum mortars were used in the construction of pyramids and monuments (Regourd et al. 1988; Nakhla & Abd El Kader 2006). According to Ingham (2011), gypsum was used to slide large stones into place, rather than for consolidating them. In excavations of Neobabylonian (Verhoeven 1991) and Iberian (Poch 1998) sites, gypsum plasters were also recognized. In a study on Roman mortar and plaster samples of 18 localities in and around Belgium, Mestdagh (1991) did not discover any gypsum materials, nor does Lamprecht (1993) mention the use of gypsum in Roman constructions. In Medieval times gypsum mortars were used, for instance in the Paris, Chartres and Lübeck, regions where gypsum rocks are abundant (Elsen 2006). In Britain it was not used until the thirteenth century (Ingham 2011). Elsewhere in Europe its use was probably restricted to plasters in the interior of buildings because of the relatively high solubility of gypsum.

The preparation of gypsum plaster or mortar is based on a dehydration and rehydration process. When heated between 130 and  $160^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , gypsum forms the hemihydrate bassanite (so-called plaster of Paris) (Rapp 2002). Microscopically, this transformation is characterized by the appearance of fibrous subcrystals with higher interference colours, comparable to those of anhydrite, with basic parallel optical orientation and arranged parallel to the cleavage of gypsum (see Mees & Tursina 2010). When dehydration takes place above  $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , anhydrite is formed (so-called 'dead burnt gypsum'). When mixed with water, the hemihydrate rehydrates to form gypsum

again; anhydrite does not react with water, and is therefore useless for construction. The chemical composition and optical characteristics of these materials are summarized in Table 24.1.

# 24.2 Micromorphology

Petrographic descriptions of ancient gypsum mortars, plasters or bindings are very scarce in literature. The descriptions by Regourd et al. (1988) of Egyptian mortars used in pyramids are rather vague and do not allow for comparisons with other cases. The microscopic descriptions given below are mainly based on the systematic descriptions of gypsum plasters of Byzantine grain storage constructions in Israel (Tsatskin 1999), a report on plasters in Neobabylonian buildings in Iraq (Verhoeven 1991) and in an Iberian fortress (eighth century BC) in Catalonia (Poch 1998). A series of thin sections from Israeli archaeological sites was studied in order to present in this chapter some general micromorphological characteristics of gypsum mortars and plasters.

# 24.2.1 Coarse Material (Temper)

Major Constituents The studied samples contain grains of quartz and quartzite and different limestone and calcareous sandstone fragments. Grain size ranges between 100 and  $3000\mu \mathrm{m}$  and there is no evidences of specific sorting, except in one case where a layer containing finer, parallel-oriented quartz grains is superposed to a less well sorted material. Verhoeven (1991) describes a two-layer plaster, the outer layer containing less coarse material and the binder being more pure than the inner

Table 24.1 Optical characteristics of gypsum-related materials in thin section.  

<table><tr><td>Mineral</td><td>Formula</td><td>Colour</td><td>Relief</td><td>Interference colours</td><td>Specific features</td></tr><tr><td>Anhydrite</td><td>CaSO4</td><td>colourless</td><td>moderate positive</td><td>up to third-order green</td><td>xenotopic fabric</td></tr><tr><td>Bassanite</td><td>CaSO4.1/2H2O</td><td>colourless, often faint brown</td><td>moderate positive</td><td>first-order yellow</td><td>fibrous aspect</td></tr><tr><td>Gypsum</td><td>CaSO4.2H2O</td><td>colourless</td><td>moderate negative</td><td>first-order grey</td><td>distinct cleavages</td></tr></table>

layer. As far as we could deduce from literature, only inert temper was used.

Additives and Accidental Components Most samples contain fragments of charcoal, probably remains of the fuel used for the preparation of the plaster. Silica phytoliths, found in a few cases, may have the same origin.

# 24.2.2 Fine Material (Binder)

In recent gypsum plasters, prepared from natural material (as opposed to the gypsum derived from industrial waste products), gypsum crystals can barely be resolved at the highest magnification of the petrographic microscope, forming interlocking crystals (Ingham 2011).

In all samples studied by Tsatskin (1999), the fine material consists of a continuous xenotopic mass of fine  $(< 10\mu \mathrm{m})$  equidimensional gypsum crystals with random orientation. In PPL the greyish mass shows a speckled, often dotted limpidity, probably caused by fine charcoal particles. Isolated, fine calcite grains are commonly observed.

According to Gourdin & Kingery (1975) the coherence of the rehydration products is caused by its specific fabric: an interlocking of fine  $(0.5 - 5\mu \mathrm{m})$  well-formed, needle-shaped crystals of gypsum is clearly visible in SEM (Figure 24.1). Plasters exposed to wet conditions from time to time over several millennia are expected to have a coarser grain size, sometimes larger than  $10\mu \mathrm{m}$ , as a result of so-called Ostwald ripening (Kingery et al. 1988).

# 24.2.3 C/f related distribution pattern

In all cases a porphyric  $c/f$  related distribution pattern were observed, ranging from open to single spaced. No specific orientation or distribution patterns were noticed.

# 24.2.4 Porosity

All samples were characterized by a rather high porosity. Only small vesicles (about  $400\mu \mathrm{m}$ ) and larger vugs were observed, without specific distribution or orientation patterns. Verhoeven (1991) mentions frequent vesicles in a Neobabylonian plaster.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/56a5d2ded5553b61b1621395f5ce2e03d50c6c375df8b79d6c56a587d6aab863.jpg)  
Figure 24.1 SEM photomicrograph of gypsum mortar. Au-coated sample in secondary electron (SE) mode. Note a densely packed 'forest' of rehydrated needle shaped homogeneous gypsum crystals and a few scattered cavities (voids). Byzantine staircase, Caesarea Maritima, Israel. These and the following thin sections courtesy of A. Raban.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/7c893949cac3cfce22f18556da93c3cfd2afc2116fc77e78e6f178ab251d4260.jpg)  
Figure 24.2 Xenotopic coatings of coarser gypsum on one side of vesicles. The micromass consists of fine-grained xenotopic gypsum. Byzantine granary, Israel (see Tsatskin 1999). XPL.

# 24.2.5 Features

Thin coatings or hypocoatings of larger (up to  $100\mu \mathrm{m}$ ) gypsum crystals are frequently observed around pores or coarse constituents (Figure 24.2). These coatings

generally have a xenotopic fabric composed of equidimensional grains, but sometimes they consist of parallel oriented elongated crystals (up to  $250\mu \mathrm{m}$  long - Figure 24.3). This feature has also been reported in modern plasters (Ingham 2011). In one sample, the coating occurs only on one side of the pores or coarse constituents. As the exact orientation of the thin section is not known, it is not possible to distinguish between cappings or pendants.

Angular fragments of more brownish, coarse gypsum can be considered as unburned residues of the raw material. In the same thin section angular, elongated gypsum crystals  $(2000\mu \mathrm{m})$  with a fibrous fabric, composed of elongated, parallel oriented crystals (up to  $250\mu \mathrm{m}$  long) may be rehydrated coarse bassanite grains formed during plaster preparation (Figure 24.4). Lenticular bassanite pseudomorphs after gypsum were observed

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/293aa47830eaf1f68f091e53f24f9070e146173d526e0ccde57e460d98e7d759.jpg)  
Figure 24.3 Coating of coarser, elongated gypsum crystals on pore wall. Byzantine granary, Israel (see Tsatskin 1999). XPL.

by Verhoeven (1991). They are most probably unreacted hemihydrate residues and suggest that pedogenic gypsum (e.g., a gypsic horizon) was used for the preparation of the plaster.

One plaster composed of very coarse gypsum (several mm) with a poikilotopic fabric is probably the result of recrystallization. The crystals enclose grains of coarse material and larger charcoal fragments (Figure 24.5).

Regourd et al. (1988) describe anhydrite grains showing centripetal transformation to gypsum. There is, however, no real proof that it does not concern rehydrating bassanite grains from the cement, rehydration of anhydrite being a very slow geological process.

# 24.3 Alteration

Microscopic aspects of the alteration of gypsum plasters were not described in the literature, except for the increase in crystal size resulting from alternating wet and dry conditions mentioned above. One can expect dissolution as a result of leaching. Middendorf (2001), cited by Ingham (2011), reports the partial dissolution of the gypsum binder, reprecipitated as larger crystals. Transformation to bassanite and/or anhydrite as a result of burning, as far as this is not erased by later rehydrations, could be expected.

# 24.4 Conclusion

Very little information is available on the microscopic study of gypsum plasters and therefore it is not possible to define their general fabric characteristics. More systematic studies are needed, including medieval mortars.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/2125af6907d9bce11f368310072c8acb73213567676581ccc98b70491189e05f.jpg)

They are probably alteromorphs after bassanite formed by heating of gypsum. The micromass is composed of fine-grained xenotopic gypsum. Byzantine granary, Israel (see Tsatskin 1999). PPL. (b) Same as (a), XPL.

# 25

# Ceramic Materials

Lara Maritan

Dipartimento di Geoscienze, Università di Padova, Italy

# 25.1 Introduction

Ceramic materials are probably the most abundant findings in prehistoric and historic archaeological sites and represent, after lithic artefacts, the most ancient form of production. The appearance of pottery occurred in different moments in the various region of the world: around 26000 years BP in Europe (Verpoorte 2001; Budja 2006), 20000 BP in China (Xu et al. 2012), 16000 BP in Japan (Shelach 2012), 10000 BP in Africa (Huysecom et al. 2009; Jesse 2010) and 7500 BP in South America (Roosevelt et al. 1996), whereas fired bricks date back to 3500 BC (Campbell & Pryce 2003).

Since technologically advanced ceramic materials developed in the last century, the term 'ceramic' has assumed a wide meaning, comprising all those inorganic, nonmetallic and solid materials obtained by heating and subsequent cooling. In the traditional sense, the word ceramic, from the Greek keramos, is used to identify burnt clay or earthenware, while pottery is referred to 'the bulk of low-fired, unvitrified materials treated by anthropologists and pre-historic archaeologists' (Rice 1987). Pottery includes a series of different ceramic materials that can be distinguished, on the basis of their physical characteristics, especially porosity, vitrification grade, firing temperature and surface treatments, into:

Terracotta: wares with medium-coarse unvitrified ceramic paste, high porosity (30% or more), firing temperature generally lower than  $900^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , unglazed surfaces, eventually covered with slip or englobe; it comprises bricks, roof and floor tiles, and most of prehistoric pottery.  
- Earthenware: porous (normally  $10 - 25\%$ ) and unvitrified ceramic pastes, glazed or unglazed, fired in a wider temperature range  $(900 - 1200^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ ; it comprises bricks, roof, wall and floor tiles, tableware and pottery.

- Stoneware: low-porosity, vitrified ceramic pastes, with glazed or unglazed surfaces, fired at high temperature  $(1200 - 1350^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ , which appeared in China between 1400 and 1200 BC and in Europe (Germany) in the fourteenth century AD; it comprises tableware, crucibles, artware and refractory bricks.  
- Porcelain: low-porosity, vitrified, white-paste ware, produced by firing kaolinite-rich clay at high temperature  $(1300 - 1450^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ , which appeared in China in the ninth and tenth centuries and in Europe (Germany and France) in the eighteenth century AD; it was and is used for the production of fine tableware and artware.

Despite this 'technical classification', pottery is archaeologically distinguished on the basis of its function, shape, decoration type, production period and body paste. Numerous types/classes can be reported in literature referring to specific geographic areas and/or historical/prehistorical periods. Most of the pottery found in archaeological sites is very fragmentary and often cannot be assigned to any specific ceramic class (such as tableware, cooking ware, storage pottery, fired bricks and tiles) or shape but is classified into macro or micro-fabrics on the basis of the ceramic paste features as observed macroscopically or with a petrographic microscope, respectively. Analogously, since potsherds in archaeological deposits are normally small, the distinction between different types of pottery, such as vase objects and ceramic building materials, is frequently not possible.

# 25.2 Micromorphology

Pottery is the result of clay firing, therefore it can be distinguished from clay materials, such as argillaceous rock fragments and unfired bricks, on the basis of those

mineral-physical changes produced during firing, which include:

- Change of colour of the clay matrix (microscopically defined as micromass and corresponding to the finest fraction of the ceramic material, smaller than  $10\mu \mathrm{m}$ ) and eventually of inclusions (fragments of mineral, rock and vegetal material larger than  $10\mu \mathrm{m}$ ).  
- Formation of secondary porosity.  
- Destabilization of some of the mineral phases with subsequent formation of new microcrystalline or amorphous phases, the obliteration of the original microstructures and/or the development of reaction rims.

The most evident characteristic is the colour of the ceramic paste (also defined as 'fabric'), which frequently also differs microscopically from that of the archaeological deposits in which the potsherds are embedded. It can vary in PPL from white to red to black, in relation to the mineralogical composition and the possible occurrence of organic matter in the base clay, the firing temperature and redox conditions during firing. Microscopically, a fragment of pottery appears as formed by different components: micromass, inclusions, voids and eventually a coating (slip, glaze - Figure 25.1a). The features of each of these components can supply information on the production technology and/or provenance (for a more complete reading on ceramic petrography, consider: Maggetti 1982; Williams 1983; Whitbread 1989, 1995; Reedy 1994, 2008; Freestone 1995; Betancourt & Peterson 2009; Quinn 2009, 2013; Ingham 2010).

# 25.2.1 Micromass

The micromass, defined as clay matrix, is all the material less than  $10\mu \mathrm{m}$  in size, composed of fine silt and fired clay. The colour of the micromass, observed at low magnification in PPL, depends on the type of base clay (the clay material used for the pottery production, intended under mineralogical, textural, and chemical viewpoints), the firing conditions and the occurrence of postdepositional phases. White or very light beige colours of the ceramic paste are related to iron-free base clay, such as kaolin fired in oxidizing conditions or reducing conditions when organic matter is lacking or very little in the base clay. Yellow and beige point to calcareous and iron-bearing clays  $(\mathrm{CaO} > 5\%)$  fired at high temperature  $(>850^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  in oxidizing atmosphere (Molera et al. 1998; Nodari et al. 2007), while red to iron-bearing clay materials (often chloritic, illitic and smectitic-rich clay materials containing iron oxide and hydroxides) fired in oxidizing conditions (Nodari et al. 2007). Grey and black indicate organic and/or iron-bearing clay materials or containing iron oxides and hydroxides, fired in reducing

atmosphere (Maritan et al. 2005). A sandwich structure, in which the section of the potsherds shows chromatic differences between the margin and the core, indicates changes in the redox conditions during firing. For instance, black-core pottery indicates reducing heating and oxidizing cooling (Nodari et al. 2004), or oxidizing firing conditions of a base clay very rich in organic matter (Maritan et al. 2006).

When the micromass shows interference colours it is defined as optically active, and testifies that the firing temperature did not exceed the breakdown of clay minerals or small crystallites (such as calcite, dolomite). Such a micromass can be described in terms of birefringent fabric (b-fabric) according to Bullock et al. (1985) and the recent revision of Quinn (2013). B-fabric can be distinguished on the basis of the type of minerals and the size and orientation of clay domains (aggregates composed of particles with parallel orientation) in the micromass, using the same terminology adopted in soil micromorphology: crystallitic, strial, striated (Figure 25.1b), and speckled (Figure 25.1c).

The orientation of clay domains, in relation to the ceramic fragment (potsherd), depends on the forming technique, although a straightforward correspondence does not exist. In fact, the spatial arrangement of clay domains (Courty & Roux 1995) and of elongated inclusions and voids (Rye 1981) depends on the stress orientation (compression or shear) during the object shaping and it is conditioned by the section orientation (Quinn 2013).

Variations in composition, colour and texture characterize heterogeneous micromass and indicate that the base clay was not seasoned, or at least not for long time, or was not adequately homogenized during working. Due to large-scale production, ceramic building materials (bricks and tiles) are often characterized by heterogeneous micromass (Quinn 2013). Extreme heterogeneity reveals the mixing of clays, a technique adopted to obtain more workable paste or specific aesthetic patterns (Cairo et al. 2001).

Postdeposition processes may affect the micromass composition by precipitation of secondary phases (Quinn 2013), such as calcite, aragonite, gypsum, and pyrite, and oxidation, reduction or hydration of oxides (Secco et al. 2011).

# 25.2.2 Inclusions

All the particles with a size larger than  $10\mu \mathrm{m}$ , which compose a ceramic and can be easily distinguished with the optical microscope, are classified as inclusions. Inclusions can represent the coarser fraction of the base clay used to produce the ceramics, or the material deliberately added by the potter (called 'temper'), to change

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/65876b082129315f772563b5c4870c7a380594df45543ad733e5f90dfca96b88.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/8261189e201e2b9b1c6886bfe405d8fbd57cd856386959301cb09da896021306.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/97624be463e101133260bd0bc07ef8e576a3bb347fb56e05462c2e3f79f6406a.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/8a83791b863e4dc1de91a7a3322ba267e175f4efab48e952d896cb8b5d996a27.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/9de12b152aac15d04c73560bb8050b4151cb057e6ce7120dec0bb7fee4e3a26b.jpg)  
Figure 25.1 (a) The components of a ceramic fragment: coating, matrix, inclusions, voids. PPL. (b) Optically active groundmass, with a parallel, striated b-fabric, and a large rounded fragment of rhyolite. XPL. (c) A fine-grained ceramic paste, with a speckled b-fabric. XPL. (d) A medium-grained ceramic paste, with a bimodal grain-size distribution of inclusions and well-rounded sand-sized grains of quartz. XPL. (e) A coarse-grained ceramic paste, with a bimodal grain-size distribution of inclusions and presence of fragments of grog and rounded limestone. PPL. (f) A coarse-grained ceramic paste with angular inclusions of sparitic calcite XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/3c5b8ebdc0cd3d7416062bd744e2aef6bfc38d3a4952e9d2b3314889939160d9.jpg)

the workability of the clay or the characteristics of the fired object. Abundance and size of inclusions are used to define the ceramic texture. The quantification of these features, as well as the shape, is fundamental in the microscopic analysis of ceramic materials. In recent years, the use of digital image processing has become more widely used to describe the petrofabrics of ancient ceramics and to constrain, through the determination of morphological and textural features, the provenance and the production recipes. These include, for example, the deliberate addition of temper, revealed by the grain-size distribution of inclusions in the ancient ceramic materials (Bouchain & Velde 2001; Reedy 2006; Livingood & Cordell 2009; Grifa et al. 2013; Dal Sasso et al. 2014; Eramo et al. 2014).

As for the inclusion size, potsherds are normally classified as fine grained (Figure 25.1c), medium grained (Figure 25.1d) and coarse grained (Figure 25.1e) when the modal size of inclusions is less than  $0.125\mathrm{mm}$ , between 0.125 and  $0.5\mathrm{mm}$ , or larger than  $0.5\mathrm{mm}$ , respectively.

Inclusions can be composed of fragments of rock, mineral, or organic origin (vegetal and animal remains, such as shells, bones, sponge spicules, phytoliths, plant matter), or human-made materials (pottery, glass, slag). All types of magmatic (intrusive and effusive) (e.g., rhyolite in Figure 25.1b), metamorphic (e.g., serpentine in Figure 25.2a) and sedimentary rocks (e.g., limestone in Figure 25.1e), and those containing fossils (e.g., foraminifera in Figure 25.2b), can be found in potsherds, depending on the composition of the base clay, of the material eventually added to the clay paste and on the geology of the production area if the clay is alluvial. The possibility of assigning a rock or mineral inclusion to a specific lithotype does not subsist when inclusions are very small, as they do not exhaustively represent the lithology. Mineral markers, specific mineral phases that can be univocally attributed to a specific geographic region, are often very difficult to find in most of the common pottery productions, due to the absence of specific minerals in the lithotypes outcropping in the production area from which the clay and/or temper were collected. Occasionally, heavy minerals can be used to constrain the provenance (Mange & Bezeczky 2007).

As for the inclusions of organic origin, their presence in potsherds can supply important information on the type of clay used or on the production technology. For instance, the occurrence of microfossils in the clay matrix, such as foraminifera, indicates that the clay is of marine origin (Quinn 2008), and if the species association can be identified, the clay can be geologically dated and consequentially connected to specific outcrops. The presence of vegetal remains normally testifies the deliberate addition of a temper in the clay paste to produce light ceramic materials. This type of temper was and is used to

produce large objects, such as storage jars, or in some cases bricks with lower specific weight. Due to the low decomposition temperature in oxidizing conditions, vegetal remains are often burnt out during firing, and large pores, preserving their original shape (moldic voids), are the only attestation of their former presence (Figure 25.2c). The plant temper is preserved in pottery (Jiang & Liu 2006; Tomber et al. 2011; Mariotti Lippi et al. 2011) when firing did not exceed the decomposition temperature of organic matter (less than  $500 - 600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ), was short, or took place in reducing conditions. In some cases, types of plants added as temper can be identified on the basis of the analysis of phytolits preserved after firing (De Paepe et al. 2003; Kreiter et al. 2014).

Temper can be distinguished from naturally occurring inclusions of the base clay on the basis of the grain-size distribution, shape and composition. Generally, bimodal or polymodal distributions of rock and mineral inclusions indicate tempering with sand, when particles are rounded in shape (Figures 25.1d and 25.1e), or with ground-up materials, when especially large inclusions are angular shaped (Figure 25.1f). Moreover, some types of inclusions, such as fragments of bone, shells, synthetic materials, chaff, straw, rice and hair, suggest deliberate addition by the potter, as they are not natural component of the base clay. Only when they occur in very small amounts can they be considered as accidental, introduced into the ceramic paste by chance. Grog or chamotte is a particular temper composed of fired ceramic (Figure 25.1e), the occurrence of which indicates recycling of such material (Whitbread 1986; Cuomo Di Caprio & Vaughan 1993).

Optical changes in inclusions, and eventually the presence of reactions rims can provide important constraints for defining the firing temperature of the pottery. This is the case for carbonate inclusions (limestone, calcite, shells) which underwent partial or complete decomposition or reactions with the surrounding groundmass at temperatures higher than  $700 - 800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (see Riccardi et al. 1999; Maritan et al. 2007). Another example is the change in colour, for example from green to red of glauconite and serpentine over  $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  when fired in oxidizing conditions (the breakdown of the mineral lattice makes the iron free to oxidize into hematite, which gives the characteristic red colour to the fired inclusion) (Figure 25.2a).

# 25.2.3 Voids

Potsherds are characterized by the occurrence of primary and secondary voids due, respectively, to shrinkage during drying and to the loss of volatiles, mainly  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$  and  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ , from the decomposition of clay minerals, hydrated minerals, hydroxides and/or carbonates during firing at temperatures exceeding those of breakdown of these phases. Large moldic voids, due to the

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/9524d43b4453540fd75cf3ce1a008dd35dc9ada264159c4b3dbf37d633ad2729.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/fbb12f972b721a96b87a4adad3a32e49460fcdf92b3b766138debf1354db6622.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/711f1bdd270820be3de94b41f4bdda709b8ac8a0f18ce99678f6aa98326d3d4d.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/cf399b58f6f76153499a2b758c48be982e18bb03850d372f8f41c15a3182dc87.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/acb9fba60424f32b8ccf2eb7e7251129489082f609b393a83a7d16bbdfe88bf3.jpg)  
Figure 25.2 (a) Fragments of serpentine, with a yellowish colour due to the crystallization of hematite after serpentine decomposition during firing. PPL. (b) The characteristic folding of inclusions (large fragments of foraminifera) and voids (channels, planar voids, and vughs) parallel to the rim of the vessel due to the shaping operation. PPL. (c) Elongated voids produced from the decomposition of vegetal inclusions during firing. PPL. (d) Large vesicles in a fine-grained paste. PPL. (e) Food remains on the external surface of a potsherd. ![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/8fc48b9e769e508d5c37fd6c7b099ecffa3d633d209d1452b6894a409badac12.jpg)

decomposition of organic matter during firing, can also be observed when plant remains (grass, straw, rice, seeds) were added to the ceramic paste (Figure 25.2c). Voids in pottery are quantified microscopically (percentage area) and described in terms of size and shape as in soil micromorphology (Stoops 2003). Increasing the firing temperature, voids tend to assume more regular shapes, from vugs to vesicles (Figure 25.2d).

Secondary phases can precipitate in voids, and occasionally also cover the external surfaces, showing the postdepositional chemical-physical conditions. Secondary calcite, gypsum, pyrite, jarosite, vivianite, mitridatite and halite have been reported in literature (Freestone 2001; Cau Ontiveros et al. 2002; Maritan & Mazzoli 2004; Secco et al. 2011).

# 25.2.4 Microstructure

The microstructure of the ceramic paste refers to the orientation of the voids, inclusions and clay domains (portions of the micromass) generally in relation to the walls of the potsherds. An oriented microstructure cannot be linked in a straightforward way to specific pottery-forming techniques (pinching, moulding, coiling, slab building, wheel throwing) when potsherd fragments are included in soil thin sections, as it is not possible to define how they were cut (longitudinally, transversally, obliquely). In some cases, technological information can be gathered. Ceramic materials formed by pinching (small objects), moulding (ceramics produced in large scale, such as bricks, tiles, plates, cups) and slab building, are characterized in thin section by elongated inclusions and voids and clay domains parallel oriented to the external surfaces. Objects obtained by wheel throwing are generally characterized by fine pastes, in which the innermost part, mainly affected by compression, has elongated inclusions and voids and clay domains have a parallel orientation, while the outermost portion near the surface shows the effects of vertical shearing with intersected clay domains, such as cross-striated b-fabric. A concentric orientation of voids, inclusions, and clay domains is caused by the rolling of clay and indicates that the pottery was coil formed. The presence of cracks isolating distinct portions of pastes also indicates the use of coil technique.

# References

Betancourt, P. P. & Peterson, S. E. (2009) Thin-section Petrography of Ceramic Materials. Institute for Aegean Prehistory, Archaeological Excavation Manual 2. INSTAP Academic Press, Philadelphia.

Bouchain, I. & Velde, B. (2001) Grain distribution by image analysis of thin sections in some Gaulo-Roman common

# 25.2.5 Features

Coatings can be present on the external surface/s of ceramic fragments. They can be distinguished on the basis of their composition into slip (englobe), when composed of a fine-clay material that still shows the interference colour, or glaze, when composed of vitreous optically isotropic materials. The presence of a surface coating can be used to distinguish uncoated from slipped and glazed ware, excluding that the shard is a brick or a roof tile, but cannot indicate whether it is a fragment of pottery or glazed tile (both wall and floor).

# 25.3 Conclusion

Microscopically, possible classifications / descriptions of potsherds as found in soil thin sections can be made on the basis of inclusion grain size, distinguishing fine, medium, and coarse ware, and eventually on the vitrification grade. Only when reference thin section collections of the pottery productions and repertoire of archaeological sites of the area are available can potsherds be assigned precisely to ceramic classes with specific function and / or shape.

On the basis of the microscopic characteristic medium-coarse pottery can be distinguished from bricks and tiles when the contour of the shard can be linked to a specific shape (rim, handle, base, wall). The occurrence of food residues (Figure 25.2e), visible in thin section, when burial conditions allowed their preservation, may constrain the type of pottery to cooking ware when burnt, tableware when the potsherd is fine in paste, or storage-transport jar (e.g., amphora). When a precise characterization is not possible, the broad definition 'ceramic material' should be adopted when describing archaeological soil and sediment thin sections.

The shape of potsherds, as seen in thin sections can supply information on the deposition process. Rounded and subrounded ceramic fragments may point to reworking in a fluvial or wave-dominated environment (Figure 25.2f), whereas angular fragments testify the absence of fluvial transport over longer distances or reworking.

# 26

# Metals and Metalworking Residues

Ivana Angelini<sup>1</sup>, Gilberto Artioli<sup>2,3</sup> and Cristiano Nicosia<sup>4</sup>

$^{1}$  Dipartimento dei Beni Culturali, Università degli Studi di Padova, Italy  
$^{2}$  Dipartimento di Geoscienze, Università degli Studi di Padova, Italy  
3 CIRCe Centre, Università di Padova, Italy  
<sup>4</sup> Centre de Recherche en Archéologie et Patrimonie, Université Libre de Bruxelles, Belgium

# 26.1 Introduction

Metallurgical processes include the operations involved in the extraction, refinement, alloying, working and recycling of metals. The actual operational chain of the ancient metallurgical processes as investigated by archaeometallurgy is rather complex (see Rehren & Pernicka 2008). Here, we are only interested in those steps of the processes that can leave traces in soils and sediments and that can be identified and investigated by micromorphological techniques (i.e., in transmitted light microscopy of thin sections). As a general principle, we may subdivide the materials involved in metallurgy into (i) nonpyrometallurgical materials, such as the starting ores used to extract the metals at the beginning of the cycle, or the corrosion products derived from chemical alteration of the metal during burial at the end of the cycle, and (ii) pyrometallurgical materials – that is all products formed or transformed at high temperature during the pyrotechnological steps of the metallurgical cycle (Figure 26.1). The latter materials include: smelting slags formed during reduction and extraction of the metal (Bachmann 1982), slag fragments and sand produced during crushing of the slags for drill retrieval, technical ceramics and refractory materials used to confine and control the firing process in crucibles and furnaces (Rehder 2000; Bayley & Rehren 2007; Martinón-Torres et al. 2009), and postsmelting slags, hammerscales and metal fragments produced during iron smithing and metal casting and working (Tylecote 1992). Moreover, the high temperatures required for the reduction and extraction of the metal imply that sediments located near the site of the activities were exposed to heat, undergoing reactions such as rubefaction, vitrification and sintering (see Röpke & Dietl 2017, this book; Mallol et al. 2017, this book).

In most cases the identification and interpretation of the metallurgical evidence may be hard, because we are often left with small fragments, frequently displaced from the original place where the metallurgical operation was performed. Furthermore, most of the pyrometallurgical products such as slags and metals are almost invariably totally or partly opaque in thin section when investigated under normal transmitted light microscopy. Additional observations in reflected light mode or even by electron microscopy might therefore be required in order to draw positive conclusions about the nature of the fragments.

The metals and alloys that we primarily take into consideration are those abundantly used in the past: Cu and Cu-alloys, Fe and steel and Pb (Tylecote 1992). They were extracted from mineral ores through a series of steps involving crushing and beneficiation of the ores, roasting of the concentrated ore to partially or totally react the sulphides (if present, as it commonly happens) into oxides, smelting at high temperature (with quartz to separate the Fe into olivine) to produce slags, and possibly refining. All the metallurgical steps that require firing at high temperature normally leave traces in and around the place of the operation. The macro- and microscopic observations, together with the firing evidence in the stratigraphy may be important to evaluate whether the observed metallurgical products in the soil (slags, technical ceramics) are in place or displaced from the firing site. Production of precious metals such as Au or Pt hardly leaves evidence in the soil because they are mostly found in the native state and they do not need reduction processes at high temperature. Extraction of Au from refractory gold ores or separation of Au from silver could be exceptions. The roasting and cupellation of Ag out of Pb ores (mainly galena, PbS or cerussite  $\mathrm{PbCO_3}$ ) is of

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/ecc9cd362b781b77ed6761c6bb632cd75a692544a9f4ab31f248c94d94fe452d.jpg)  
Figure 26.1 Diagram showing the major steps of the metallurgical cycle (left) and the type of evidence resulting from each step (right).

some interest because it invariably involves crucible operations and the substantial production of litharge (PbO) during the reduction of silver (Rehren & Eckstein 2002; Bayley & Eckstein 2006).

# 26.2 Micromorphology

# 26.2.1 Starting Ores

The presence of the ores used to extract the metals indicates a working site at the beginning of the metallurgical cycle, not necessarily in geographical proximity of the mines. Although most of the ore treatment was performed in the absence of stable structures, sometimes wooden constructions used for crushing, washing and mineral separation are identified as part of the metallurgical operations (Oeggl et al. 2008). The retrieval of the precious ore was generally performed very carefully, however crushing and beneficiation of ores normally leaves microparticles of the minerals in the layers deriving from the processing activity. When water was employed, typical water-laid deposits are formed showing sedimentary structures such as laminations and grading. In thin section these deposits consist of alternating sequences of sand- and fine gravel-sized mineral detritus, besides abundant charcoal fragments of all size classes.

Possible sites of ore roasting are actually rather difficult to identify. In many cases they consist of burnt soil layers and charcoal. The common absence of crushed or roasted ore fragments makes the interpretation even harder. The combination of visible effects of firing activity (i.e., reddened sediments - Figures 26.2 and 26.3) with the presence of ore/metal/slag fragments or micro particles (Figure 26.4) may help distinguish metallurgical

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/aa7a4a353a3c760e0ecfe124a593c86a3e98c4e5f04606116ba909fc87d759b8.jpg)  
Figure 26.2 Ore roasting bed from the medieval metallurgical site of Le Loppe (Val del Mis, Belluno, Italy). Below a thick layer of charcoal, still containing remains of roasted ores, a layer of burnt soil (rubefied) is clearly visible.

pyrotechnology from other kinds of firing activities. Sometimes the ores locally dispersed in the soil during the roasting form microlayers enriched in metal-containing secondary minerals (i.e., malachite, brochantite, Fe oxi-hydroxides, etc. – Figure 26.5).

# 26.2.2 Smelting Slags and 'Slag Sand'

Smelting slags are the product of an essential step (slagging) of metal production (i.e., reduction to metal state) and refinement (Bachmann 1982; Artioli 2010; Hauptmann 2014). They are heterogeneous materials produced in a partially or totally molten state. The basic nature of the slags obtained during copper and iron smelting is fayalitic (fayalite, iron silicate  $\mathrm{Fe_2SiO_4}$ ), although their chemical and phase composition can vary substantially depending on the type of mineral charge, the time-temperature path of the reactions, the use of fluxants and the presence of mineral impurities. Under the petrographic microscope, common fayalitic slags appear as angular or subrounded fragments consisting of a groundmass of dark glass (optically isotropic) including olivine crystals with morphological features (idiomorphic, skeletal, hopper, chain, feathery - Figures 26.6 and 26.7) that depend on the cooling conditions and the rate of temperature variations. Variable amounts of other inclusions are commonly present, such as metal or matte droplets, iron oxides (mainly magnetite and wuestite), unreacted quartz and other silicate phases formed by high temperature reactions (pyroxenes, spinels, etc.). The porosity is predominantly vesicular, as it derives from trapped gas bubbles. Slags can be found in large blocks, possibly showing fluidal features derived from tapping and pouring. Slag droplets appear as well

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/b42fa040c26afa1f56bb1027fa370c3d7aec8c5fa96bfcc3a2a27ff8e412ee7b.jpg)  
Figure 26.3 Micromorphology of a layer resulting from a Bronze Age furnace functioning and/or dismantling (northern Italy). Note the presence of rubefied sediment aggregates, charcoal and of a slag (circled). (a) PPL; (b) OIL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/4a656a4018d9d4d516545bd33988e439a6f3d1c7bf9435f0bd4c7c738b634fc3.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/513cc8e5ce4754246f100f2a80484217ac862b6e3f6c005cc8dedf165b9d6cbe.jpg)  
Figure 26.4 Secondary malachite formed on the surface of carbonatic pebbles and rocks from copper percolated through the roasted soil. The malachite crust incorporates a large quantity of surrounding soil material. XPL.

rounded or subrounded particles (Figure 26.8) and can be interpreted as spills during slag tapping from furnaces and/or pouring from crucibles.

Fragments of slags systematically crushed to extract the embedded matte/metal prills appear as irregular particles ('slag sand'). They are produced in copper smelting during the subsequent cycles of extraction of the Cu-enriched sulfuric matte. If water was used to separate the slags from the matte, then sedimentary features similar to those produced during the separation of the ores can be found (i.e., laminations, grading) (Figures 26.9 and 26.10).

# 26.2.3 Technical Ceramics

# and Refractory Materials

These are used to confine and control the firing process in crucibles and furnaces (Rehder 2000; Rehren 2003; Bayley & Rehren 2007; Martinón-Torres et al. 2009), to

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/c60d26903b63993848c806b404ffdeb6439b7b102aefdc1790c8626946ac4394.jpg)  
Figure 26.6 Typical microstructure of fayalitic slags observed in transmitted light (XPL). (a) Skeletal olivines in a fayalitic glass matrix; a couple of sulphide droplets reacted from the charge are present in the right part of the image; (b) Smaller fayalite crystals formed due to rapid cooling, and several large quartz grains indicating incomplete reaction. Copper-age slags from Bressanone (Italy - see Artioli et al. 2015).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/6b04a22e29923d266374787cce99177c49d1987b2aa3237220648ac8b064d2ca.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/39e26a69fafec99b4a9628819d15738ad2588a85d2d4b4fdc167a4e0e8c8edc3.jpg)  
Figure 26.7 Heterogeneous composition of fayalitic slags from Bressanone (Italy - see Artioli et al. 2015) observed in transmitted light (XPL). (a) Hopper (in the centre) and idiomorphic olivines. (b) Olivines with different habits, mainly idiomorphic and hopper crystals.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/5320942918d3ad00cfc9a0acea1530191977181a7bfa68163bfc050b179efec2.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/bb0d48c95d0a4dd2c6f1945c9e210a33322d44b2f42b9dd1629ea65537d4ce3d.jpg)  
Figure 26.8 Rounded droplet slag. Note the vesicular porosity. Medieval site of Rue de Pierres (Bruxelles, Belgium). PPL.

prepare auxiliary tools such as tuyeres, or act as moulds for metal casting. Apart from rare occurrences (Thornton & Rehren 2009), prehistoric crucibles used in melting/ smelting operations are not different from common ceramics and they can be distinguished mainly because the firing was performed from above and within the crucible, rather than from below. It is therefore the internal part of the ceramic vessel that presents extensive vitrification, sintering (see Röpke & Dietl 2017, this book) and the presence of metal-related materials (metal droplets, reaction phases, alteration products - Figures 26.11 and 26.12). The crucibles used for metal alloying or cuppellation often contain specific metal-rich phases. The parts of the furnaces not in direct contact with the metallurgical materials (ores, slags, matte, metal) are virtually indistinguishable from the ceramics used in other pyrotechnological activities (pottery production, cooking).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/6d91c520509e49d0a80144b3ccb3ba963995bb5dbb77156bb1286f1771f5200b.jpg)  
Figure 26.9 Detail of the filling of an artificial ditch associated with Bronze Age metallurgical activities (northern Italy). The sequence derives from sediment setting in still shallow waters. (a) Predominantly mineral layer (silty clays), with common

Refractory ceramics used in medieval or later metallurgical operations have a specific refractory character (Martinón-Torres et al. 2008). Besides the surface vitrification present in the outer part of the vessel, they can also be identified by the high quartz content and/or the high temperature reaction products (mullite).

# 26.2.4 Working Slags, Hammerscales and Metal Fragments

Copper and bronze metalworking generally do not leave extensive records beyond the metal-extraction processes, because the slag, the running matte and the metal are all liquids and neatly separated (immiscible liquids) during the smelting (i.e., the metal is less viscous than the silica slag), unless the reactions are incomplete. Furthermore, the metal can be easily cast at temperatures slightly above  $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (Tylecote 1992). However, in iron metallurgy the slag is less viscous than the metal. At the temperature of operation  $(1000 - 1200^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  the slag is fluid whereas the metal is solid, so that the separation requires a great deal of high temperature metalworking (smithing) of the wrought iron produced in the furnace. Here, the slag is squeezed out of the metal through hammering. Furthermore, liquid-iron technologies (i.e., cast iron) require temperatures in excess of  $1500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , and were uncommon in the ancient world (Craddock 2003). Most of the iron artefacts were therefore produced out of the bloom through laborious forging techniques, which required mechanical work at relatively high temperature and reducing conditions. The iron metalworking thus produces a number of different kinds of physical evidence related to these processes: minute slag droplets squeezed out of the wrought iron, hammerscales produced during mechanical working of the iron bar (Figure 26.13), sintered silica sand used to keep the metal into reducing conditions during forging (Figure 26.14). They are generally abundant on the surfaces of iron workshops.

# 26.2.5 Burnt Soil and Reacted Materials

Large quantities of burnt soil fragments and charcoal derive from the various stages of the metallurgical cycle. However, in thin section, it may be impossible to identify a layer positively as the outcome of metallurgical practices unless burnt soil and charcoal are accompanied by metallurgical materials (see Figure 26.3). In archaeometallurgical contexts there is often direct interaction

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/5c200803faa719e90d18289b6405f817ca76f9bfd6f8443299263759285d15b8.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/62e55c4cd73512fdf791448cb2b3a9660b843a38b17439b7c5b8f7f08ecb1dcd.jpg)  
Figure 26.10 Detail of the 'slag sand' layer present in the sequence of Figure 26.9, level marked with 'c'. The small fragments of slags are opaque in PPL (a) and present the typical high interference colours in XPL (b).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/0cc6a2e6e83c4c559ae1172f624b3fac4e7bdb678b7b2b0baf70a553bb598b6e.jpg)  
Figure 26.11 Fragment of a technical crucible used for tin bronze alloying (Bronze Age site of Beneceto, Parma, Italy).

between metal-rich materials and soil minerals. This can happen, for example, by accidental spill of the slag and/ or matte from crucibles and furnaces, by voluntary tapping of the slags on soil surfaces, by reaction with the hot hammer scales squeezed out of the iron bars, by voluntary immersion of the hot iron bars into quartz sand to prevent oxidation of the iron or, in the case of cupellation of silver from lead oxides, by percolation of the lead oxide into the lime or bone ash lining of the hearth (litharge cakes). In all cases we have hot metal-rich or silicate-rich materials reacting with cold soil or lining minerals, invariably with large temperature gradients and incomplete reactions, sometimes even partially obscured by subsequent alteration. The presence of

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/b5a98cbf4e93acd24ccca6875c861ae9686ee2edbfb7ddaf819ece9ec95e7918.jpg)  
Figure 26.12 Thin section view of crucible shown in Figure 26.11. The ceramic shard clearly shows vitrification, sintering, and metal-containing phases due to the high temperature reactions that occurred in the internal part of the vessel. XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/3105932d8e5b9095b6b03eb86773534297053021fc93c3a8bbb5bc22ee855ccb.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/dcb39a50a407dd6b7ed04456b71231221ba36ec0af3c6257ba3c0ef2caadccc9.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/7f604f74d05a11f2e7486953a8b3b4dcc66e21bec4ff4d42459ae0c67cff6658.jpg)  
Figure 26.14 Example of HT reacted materials as a product of metallurgy (sand layer used during iron metalworking from an Iron Age context in Padova, Italy). (a) Strongly rufefied groundmass. PPL; (b) sand quartz layer showing sintering, with abundant vesicles. PPL; (c) and (d) Percolation of dendritic (i.e. fast-cooled) iron oxide (wuestite). PPL.

metal-rich components is again required in order to positively identify the HT reacted materials as a product of metallurgy with respect to normal pyrotechnology such as that pertaining to glass or ceramics. Figure 26.14 shows an example of a sequence formed as a result of the interactions described above. It includes reddish soil at

the base (Figure 26.14a) overlain by a thick quartz-rich layer showing integranlar sintering, abundant vesicles (Figure 26.14b), and percolation among the grains of a predominantly dark matrix composed of dendritic (i.e. fast cooled) iron oxide (wuestite - Figure 26.14c, d). The observed features provide strong evidence that a hot

Fe-rich material, most likely an iron bar, was put in contact with the pure quartz sand necessary during forging. The sample thus has all the features expected from the sand layer used during iron metalworking.

# 26.2.6 Raw and Refined Metals, Corrosion Products

It is rare to find raw and refined metals in archaeological deposits, because of the rapidity of alteration and degradation under normal soil conditions (Scott 2002; Scott & Eggert 2009). If the alteration is incomplete, then we may observe the advancement of the reaction from metal, through metal oxides and chlorides, to more complex phases derived from the low temperature interaction of the metal ions with the soil fluids. If the metal has completely degraded, unusual concentrations of oxides or other alteration phases containing metallic elements may indicate the past presence of metallic materials. If metal fragments are present, transmitted light optical examination is of little use because all metals and most oxides produced by alteration are totally opaque (Figure 26.15). If a reflected light microscope is available, then a great deal of information can be gained by the metallographic examination of the metal microstructures (Scott 1991), although some sample preparation is required (surface polishing and chemical etching) (Figure 26.16). Identification of other opaque phases such as oxides and sulphides present as raw materials or alteration phases can also be performed by reflected light optical microscopy.

Some of the secondary phases formed during metal degradation may be identifiable by optical microscopy in transmitted light: carbonates (malachite, siderite), sulfates (brochantite, melanterite), phosphates (vivianite).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/8fda4ef42a5ec147e68cb0a507f7ebd298400ec16f07092bc4a3b09a8690aa68.jpg)  
Figure 26.15 Example of a Pb fragment (determined by SEM/EDS as  $91\%$  Pb) in OIL. In transmitted light the fragment is completely opaque. Medieval dark earth, Uffizi Gallery Complex, Florence (Italy - see Nicosia et al. 2012).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/0471f3bda2adf90926127af10d676a5369a9b1dda0c98726259015cb57b5d5f6.jpg)  
Figure 26.16 Bronze droplet with alteration phases of malachite (green) and cuprite (red). Bronze Age site of Castel de Pedena (Belluno, Italy - see Angelini et al. 2015). Reflected light.

# 26.3 Conclusions

This chapter focused on the features that may be used to recognize metallurgical activity by micromorphological examination, generally performed by transmitted light optical microscopy. It was stated that some of the materials, notably metals, oxides, and sulfides cannot be properly identified and investigated by such a technique. If opaque phases are present, therefore, examination of the thin section by reflected light optical microscopy is strongly advised.

Furthermore, as the presence of abundant metal elements and pyrometallurgical products concentrated or dispersed in the sample is crucial to identify metallurgical activity positively, and it may be rather hard to perform a complete diagnosis by simple optical microscopic investigation, other techniques are recommended to map the chemical distribution and interpret the metallurgical origin of the observed features in detail. Several microbeam-based techniques are available to map chemical elements over selected areas of the thin section or at specific points, in order to check the exact chemical and/or phase composition. Scanning electron microscopy, electron probe microanalysis, X-ray fluorescence spectroscopy, proton-induced X-ray emission and many other micro-beam techniques could be employed, and readers are referred to the appropriate literature for understanding their use and potential (Janssens & Van Grieken 2004; Artioli 2010). In many cases the very same thin section used for micromorphological examination can also be used for microbeam analysis, provided the glass cover is not present or can be removed (see Wilson 2017, this book).

Finally, recent studies have shown that chemical profiling of soils and lacustrine sedimentary sequences

yields important information on the presence of metals in the stratigraphy, even in ultra-trace amounts (see for example Hong et al. 1996; Monna et al. 2004). Thus, the chemical and/or isotopic profiling of metal elements in microstratigraphic samples can provide indirect evidence of metallurgical activity and even its evolution

in time, if the layers can be dated. Chemical profiling in the near future is bound to be an extremely powerful technique to complement the archaeometallurgical information obtained by site excavation and analyses, and to project it into a regional and environmental perspective.
