# Lithic Artefacts

Diego E. Angelucci

Dipartimento di Lettere e Filosofia, Università degli Studi di Trento, Italy

# 27.1 Introduction

Stone tools have been produced and used since the appearance of human kind on Earth. The earliest stone tools were obtained through knapping: knapped lithic artefacts have been recorded from ca. 2.5 Ma ago (e.g. Semaw et al. 2003). They are widespread throughout all prehistoric ages and were even produced in historical epochs, until recent times (e.g., González 1991; Woodall et al. 1997). Polished stone tools appeared in the Neolithic and are also found during the Metal Ages (taking into account Old World archaeological chronology).

Thanks to the ubiquitous manufacture in both space and time, and to the durability of the raw material employed for their production, lithic artefacts are frequently found in surface sediments and soils, within sites and outside them. In the latter case, they attest to sporadic occupations by past human groups or the reworking of archaeological objects from nearby sites. For this reason, the occurrence of lithic artefacts as human-modified components is fairly common in soil thin sections collected from every kind of geographic or chronological context.

This chapter focuses on stone knapping as it creates a number of features that are consistent enough to be recognized and described by standard micromorphological description (Stoops 2003). The same is not true for polished stone tools as the technique used for their production does not provide object-specific micromorphological characteristics. Polished stone tools do not seem to have ever been described in soil thin sections.

The techniques for obtaining knapped lithic artefacts vary, even if the primary action consists in detaching flakes from the raw material through percussion or pressure (Tixier et al. 1980; Whittaker 1994). This practice implies the production of several byproducts and unused shatters that are often discarded, thus producing a high quantity of items with variable size and shape (including the so-called microdebitage, see Fladmark 1982). The

latter may be incorporated easily into surface sediments or soil. All flakes produced by knapping show some common elements (Figure 27.1): a proximal butt, i.e. the part that receives the hit, and a distal end, opposite to it; a ventral (lower) side, which is the one removed by the stroke, with a more-or-less symmetrically curved shape, and a dorsal (upper) side, which bears the scars of previous detachments or the natural surface of the nodule from which the flake was removed (e.g. Andrefsky 2005).

The types of raw materials employed for knapping are often selected according to their resistance and durability in order to obtain long-lasting and efficient tools (Tarrino 2006a). Natural materials (minerals or rocks) that match these requirements are mainly: siliceous sedimentary rocks, by far the most used, as chert/flint (Mangado Llach 2004), volcanic glass such as obsidian, fine-grained metamorphic quartzite, crystalline quartz. To a lesser extent, basalt and other fine-grained volcanic rocks, some types of limestone, quartzitic sandstone (quartzarenite) and siltite (more details in Tixier et al. 1980) are also encountered. The micromorphological features of lithic artefacts dealt with in this entry are related mainly to chert/flint and quartzite tools.

Micromorphologists acquainted with archaeological stratifications have often identified lithic artefacts in soil thin sections or shown them in figures and micrographs. Courty et al. (1989: p. 115) describe flint as 'easily recognized by its petrographic properties, which include low birefringence and cryptocrystallinity. In thin section, however, it is exceedingly difficult to distinguish natural from human worked fragments of flint.' Lithic artefacts in thin sections are depicted in several papers: 'microflakes' are reported and illustrated by Courty (2001: p. 221, Figure 8.7, pictures b and c), while a 'burned flint flake' in thin section, under oblique incident light, is shown by Goldberg and Macphail (2006: p. 56, Figure 3.11).

Macphail & Goldberg (2010) report a number of factors that allow the micromorphologist to recognize

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/877b62b9c283864e962bbc0c908641c954721a173618b46d79881dc865525fc8.jpg)  
Figure 27.1 Basic nomenclature for knapped lithic artefacts.

chert/flint in thin section; the same authors show a knapped lithic tool from the lower Palaeolithic site of Boxgrove. A resume on the main characteristics of knapped lithic artefacts in thin section was given by Angelucci (2010).

# 27.2 Micromorphology

Lithic artefacts are coarse constituents that fall within the 'anthropogenic elements' category (according to Stoops 2003) and show specific micromorphological features that allow the micromorphologist to identify them (see also Angelucci 2010). The most noticeable features of knapped lithic artefacts are summarized in this section.

# 27.2.1 Grain Size

Knapped stone tools belong to the class of mineral coarse components. They are extremely heterogeneous in size, from few hundred microns to some centimetres, their size often being unrelated to the typical grain size of the embedding matrix.

# 27.2.2 Composition

Knapped lithic artefacts are composed of the types of rock or mineral suitable for knapping, as chert, obsidian, quartzite or quartz, lithotypes that are often (but not always, e.g. near mining places) exotic to the local

geological context of the site because of human importation (Macphail & Goldberg 2010).

# 27.2.3 Weathering or Alteration

Lithic artefacts usually show little or no weathering, less than alteration class 1 in the sequence of Stoops et al. (1979) (i.e., less than  $2.5\%$  by volume). As far as chert artefacts are concerned, differential alteration is often a criterion to distinguish stone tools and natural fragments composed of the same lithology within an archaeological deposit (see Angelucci 2010).

# 27.2.4 Shape

The artefacts manufactured by knapping are mostly planar, acicular or oblate in three dimensions, thus their (bidimensional) appearance in thin section is usually tabular or platy. Most knapped lithic artefacts are platy in thin section and often exhibit the same 'habit', regardless of their geographic or chronological provenance (Figures 27.2 and 27.3). This sort of 'morphological convergence' primarily originates from the flaking technique, whose main aim is to produce tools with a definite shape.

Other morphological characteristics are often diagnostic for the recognition of knapped artefacts. In particular, the presence of scars or crests along the object's contour or the occurrence of very acute angles at its corners are features that recall the shape of flaked products (see also Figure 27.1).

Knapped lithic artefacts are almost always very angular to angular, usually with regular and smooth surfaces and sharp and prominent boundaries. They exhibit a more-or-less regularly curved or straight outline.

# 27.2.5 Some Examples of Lithic Artefacts in Soil Thin Section

A selection of images from thin sections featuring knapped lithic artefacts obtained from chert/flint is shown in Figures 27.2 and 27.3. The thin sections come from archaeological deposits located in different regions and dating from distinct chronologies. A probable quartzite artefact is depicted in Figure 27.4; the figure also shows individual knapped artefacts from archaeological collections.

# 27.2.6 Remarks on Lithic Artefacts Affected by Thermal Impact

Lithic artefacts affected by thermal impact are fairly common in the archaeological record. The effects of thermal impact on the artefacts may vary according to the nature

Figure 27.2 Knapped lithic artefact composed of cryptocrystalline chert (the artefact is set vertically due to postdepositional discontinuous frost action). (a) and (b) View of the entire artefact: (a) PPL; (b) XPL. (c) and (d) Detail of the artefact's butt: (a) PPL; (b) XPL. Riparo Dalmeri, Italy, late Upper Palaeolithic living floor (Angelucci & Peresani 1996; Dalmeri et al. 2006; Angelucci et al. 2011).  
(d)

and characteristics of the raw material used. On flint / chert artefacts, the main features observed as the result of heating are (Ahler 1983; Luedtke 1992; Andrefsky 2005):

- Chromatic variation.  
- The modification of surface lustre.  
- Heat damage as: (i) crazing, i.e. the development of an internal fine pattern of cracks, which is also detected on the object's surface. Experimental studies have shown that crazing is already well visible at the temperature of  $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (Domański et al. 2009); (ii) the detachment of pot lids, i.e. small concave hemispherical flakelike objects that are easily differentiated by artificially obtained flakes as they lack striking platform and bulb of force.

- Microstructural changes related to the recrystallization of quartz; for example, flint objects heated to  $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  show an increase of crystallinity index, which reflects a significant increase in average crystal size (Domański et al. 2009).

In thin section, crazing of chert / flint artefacts is easily recognizable and appears as a dense network of fine cracks that affect the whole bulk of the object. The network pattern resembles a poorly developed (class 0, Stoops et al. 1979, i.e. between 0 and  $2.5\%$  by volume) irregular linear alteration pattern, with serrated cracks, which may show slightly parallel orientation pattern. Examples of crazing in knapped lithic artefacts collected from heavily thermally altered contexts (for instance,

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/e9dd9474656ad61dbb340152880d73b55d7c73e70da7b84d9fc4fb4846244aa0.jpg)  
(a)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/02189e00e70b36b3c2960bae768c8f54aec0994cad8abd14f5dad924983c4ba6.jpg)  
(b)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/5aa63a3436d7f59047bc25cda4075b5cd404e108a36ecc7928e34148e0820352.jpg)  
(c)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/babca05142ba7c3f7a6b7780c5a18979e5ffbc87ffe073d28425f8056166b9e9.jpg)

(d)

within hearths or from burnt animal dung) are shown in Figures 27.5 and 27.6.

Other effects of thermal impact, such as chromatic or microstructural changes, may be detected by comparing unburned and burned artefacts obtained from the same raw material.

# 27.3 Discussion

Knapped lithic artefacts can be identified easily in soil thin section as they show a number of recurrent and diagnostic micromorphological features, which are listed in Table 27.1. The observed variability of these features is

low, thus allowing the micromorphologist to identify the fragments of chert/flint or quartzite that display the complete set of features as knapped lithic artefacts.

Other features are also diagnostic. Firstly, the overall shape of the lithic artefact, which is related to the mechanical behaviour of the raw material during knapping and to the manufacture technique used for the extraction of flakes from their blank, is a diagnostic criterion. The artefact's shape often shows a specific delineation of the contour, with more-or-less regularly arched boundaries and very acute angles, as well as the possible presence of scars along the object's contour (Figures 27.2 and 27.3). The anomalous dimension of lithic artefacts with respect to the grain size of the embedding matrix

can also be a diagnostic characteristic. These features are not detected in natural geogenic components of the archaeological matrix, such as fragments of the same lithology that may occur in thin section as natural components (see also Angelucci 2010).

Thermal alteration on chert / flint artefacts may also be recognized thanks to some diagnostic features such as crazing.

There are still some critical points related to the identification of stone tools in thin section, namely: (i) the identification of lithic artefacts obtained from lithotypes other than chert / flint or quartzite; (ii) the identification of polished stone tools; (iii) a better characterization of the micromorphological features related to thermal impact. Future research will have to concentrate on these aspects, if needed.

# Part II

Current Topics in Archaeological Soil and Sediment Micromorphology

# 28

# Soils Disturbed by Vegetation Clearance and Tillage

Judit Deak<sup>1</sup>, Anne Gebhardt<sup>2</sup>, Helen Lewis<sup>3</sup>, Maria Raimonda Usai<sup>4</sup> and Heejin Lee<sup>5</sup>

<sup>1</sup> Office du Patrimoine et de l'Archéologie Neuchâtel, Section Archéologie, Switzerland  
$^{2}$  UMR 7362, LIVE, Équipe Dynamique des Paysages, Université de Strasbourg, INRAP, France  
$^{3}$  School of Archaeology, University College Dublin, Republic of Ireland  
$^{4}$  Department of Architecture and Design, University of Sassari, Alghero, Italy  
$^{5}$  Department of Archaeology and Art History, Korea Institute for Archaeology and Environment, Korea University, South Korea

# 28.1 Introduction

Vegetation clearance by people is performed through uprooting trees, digging out plants, and/or burning ground vegetation, sometimes with the aid of various implements (spade, ard, etc.). The main consequences of these actions are disturbance of soil horizons, the accumulation of burnt residues and the formation of bare earth surfaces. In certain environmental conditions, in situ, but complete soil transformations have also been identified (see Goldberg & Macphail 2006; Mikkelsen et al. 2007; Lewis 2012), but discussion of these situations is beyond the scope of this work. The disturbance of soils due to vegetation clearance and subsequent agricultural activities is well known from field evidence that ranges from soil profiles to landscape scales. These field indicators include concentrations of large charcoal fragments from forest burning, eroded topsoils and transported archaeological finds, inverted soil profiles, implement marks, exceptionally bioturbated horizons related to grazing, and even gravel pits and gravel accumulation on field borders, indicative of 'clearing' of coarse material from fields (Figures 28.1 to 28.5). Since the 1980s, numerous studies have included micromorphology as one of several techniques that permit a better understanding of ancient anthropic impacts on soils through agricultural practices (e.g., Romans & Robertson 1983; Macphail et al. 1990). These investigations reveal that in addition to macroscopic field evidence, indicators occur that are sometimes only visible under a microscope. Soil micromorphology is the only technique currently in use, besides field excavation, with which it is possible to unravel the superposition of events, a vital issue when investigating the effects of land-use change over time.

Not everyone agrees that soil micromorphology is useful for detecting or better understanding certain past agricultural activities, such as tillage (e.g., Carter & Davidson 1998). This view is based on the recognition that several processes may produce similar morphological results (e.g., 'dusty coatings' below), and thus that the interpretation of features may not be straightforward or unequivocal. In addition, land management practices may be repeated (for example multiple ploughing episodes), or superimposed (ploughing with grazing, or alternating with fallow) and they may disturb or sometimes erase micromorphological traces of previous processes. Indeed, the micromorphological features resulting from land clearance or produced by implements are often the first to be affected by erosion, and although frequently found archaeologically, are relatively rarely preserved after burial, considering how frequent tilling is and was in the past. Despite these caveats, a wide range of soil characteristics, when studied in an appropriate manner, can give valuable hints about past soil disturbance and soil management. As highlighted by several scholars (e.g., Macphail 1998; Gebhardt 2000; Macphail & Cruise 2001), each method of investigation has its potentials and limitations. By being aware of the limitations, we can more effectively use the potential emerging from many years of research using soil micromorphology for investigating ancient agriculture. The purpose of this chapter is to summarize existing knowledge about possible micromorphological traces of vegetation clearance and tillage, and their effects on soil formation processes, and highlight the current strategies that can be employed to resolve controversial interpretations. Several other aspects of ancient agricultural land management could not be included in this overview.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/853b174504d3e23dcc611f20ed277b4e29076c41bf5860172ffc24c37e3588aa.jpg)  
Figure 28.1 Experimental forest vegetation clearance by fire. In front: fireplace for Ember production. Behind: initial phase of burning of a future crop field (see also Figure 28.6a). Forchtenberg project (Germany), 2012. Image courtesy of Laténium.

However, for any analysis designed to identify ancient land use practices it is recommended that micromorphological studies concerning pasture (Courty et al. 1989; Gebhardt 1995), terracing (e.g., Goodman-Elgar 2008; Castillo et al. 2014), manuring and other amendments (Courty et al. 1991; Dockrill & Simpson 1994; Englemark & Linderholm 1996; Simpson et al. 1998; Guttman et al., 2004; 2008), and the creation of 'dark earth' (Macphail et al. 1998; Cammas et al. 1995, 1996; Fondrillon 2012; Nicosia et al. 2013a; see also Nicosia et al. 2017, this book) and of 'Anthrosols' (e.g., Macphail et al. 2003; Arroyo-Kalin 2008; see also Arroyo-Kalin 2017, this book) should also be considered.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/698bfd6424a5ab25e8fdf611ac8ceca9153be6c3e507f1194ba1822d86fcbac5.jpg)  
Figure 28.2 Large charcoal fragments on the surface of an abandoned field cleared by burning, a few years after abandonment. Forchtenberg project (Germany), 2005. Image courtesy of Laténium.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/a4a545a007303682490a42fb15c27d04777eb6bbf07f199087a046897ad687da.jpg)  
Figure 28.3 Pit with gravel attesting to prehistoric or historic field clearance. The more-or-less coarse gravels and blocks that hamper ploughing have been removed and buried. Bevaix Plateau (Neuchâtel, Switzerland). Reproduced with permission from Bednarz et al. 2006, p. 329.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/a2e2d7b4943af092d22719dbf8d67dfc0561287b656c52793724f8b2af6b8a8e.jpg)  
Figure 28.4 Tilled fields on slopes in spring. In such landscape positions, bare soils that have little structural and textural stability can be easily reworked by rain (see also Figure 28.17). Neuchâtel region (Switzerland). Image courtesy of Laténium.

# 28.2 Micromorphological Features related to Vegetation Clearance by Fire

# 28.2.1 Formation Processes and Micromorphological Features

Burning of vegetation results in charcoal and ash production (Figures 28.6a and b), and in some cases fire can affect the mineral soil components as well (see Röpke & Dietl 2017, this book). Although these features are sometimes visible to the naked eye, in archaeological soils they are often better identified under the microscope. Indicators left by the burning of vegetation cover are strongly dependent on the type of fire and of organic matter burnt, and on environmental conditions, including the nature and intensity of bioturbation. Thus, burning woody vegetation will result in large charcoal fragments, visible to the naked eye, while burning of grass vegetation will mainly result in microcharcoal and ash. The nature of fuel can also be indicated by the micromorphology of ash left after burning (Wattez & Courty 1987; Wattez 1988; Courty et al. 1989). Recent experimental studies have focused on questions raised

by archaeological sediments and give a thorough overview of the morphological aspects and chemical composition of various ashes (Canti 2003; Braadbaart et al. 2012; see also Canti & Brochier 2017, this book). Wood ashes are rich in calcium oxalate and calcium carbonate crystals, and are light, pale grey coloured (PPL) and show highest order creamy white interference colours. Grass ashes are siliceous due to a high opal phytolith content, are dark grey coloured (PPL) and mostly optically isotropic. They are often associated with silica remains of plant fragments and vesicular glassy slags.

The burning of large surface areas such as agricultural fields is not normally associated with reddish (oxidized) burnt soil. Indeed, as indicated by the Forchtenberg Project, which carried out land clearance by fire, temperatures may not rise above  $60^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  under  $2\mathrm{cm}$  depth (Rösch et al. 2002). However, reddened soil might be associated indirectly with land clearance by fire. Burning fields through slash-and-burn cultivation may need a high quantity of embers, and their production requires a major fire lasting for several hours (Rösch et al. 2004; see also Figure 28.1). The fireplaces used for Ember production might be associated, at least on their external rims,

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/6d96ce686fc335fcbdf8c945c8fe0699676031fbd9f196639b5f7f2df3281cde.jpg)  
Figure 28.5 Thick prehistoric and historic colluvium testifying to the erosion / sedimentation processes related to various land-use techniques since the Neolithic. The darker coloured soil-sedimentary sequence in the lower part of the section indicates reworking of the original surface, which has become more or less mixed with parts of the subsurface horizons. The lighter-coloured upper part suggests that the soils were so strongly eroded that the soil parent material is the major sediment source. Bevaix Plateau (Neuchâtel, Switzerland). Image courtesy of M. Juillard, Laténium.

with reddened surfaces and subsoil. Thus, identifying a 'fireplace' archaeologically in the absence of any other type of evidence for domestic or ritual activity (pottery, food processing remains, etc.) might indicate fire activity with the aim of vegetation clearance.

Uprooted trees can also be burnt in situ for vegetation clearance (see also section 28.3.5). In this case, temperatures in the confined environment of the tree hollow are sufficiently high to bring about reddening of the subsoil (Canti & Linford 2000). The concentration of charcoal of one tree species is an argument for in situ burning of a fallen tree, but there is no way to distinguish a natural wind throw from a tree hollow associated with vegetation clearance (Goldberg & Macphail 2006).

Limpid clay coatings in soils with unfavourable conditions for dispersion or flocculation processes might also be indirect indicators of a large amount of ash, and thus of possible vegetation clearance by burning. Colloidal clay mobilization is known to occur in well-drained soils with a pH range of 4.5/5 and 6.5, under a more or less continuous vegetation cover or in a saline soil environment (Quénard et al. 2011). Thus, in slightly alkaline and extremely acidic soils the mobilization of clay colloids and the accumulation of limpid clay coatings might be explained through the addition of material such as ash, which can create an exceptional rise in soil pH (Courty et al. 1989; Herrmann et al. 2007; Braadbaart et al. 2011).

Vegetation clearance by burning may therefore be recognized directly by: (i) a high concentration of large charcoal fragments (visible to the naked eye); (ii) a high concentration of small charcoal fragments (visible under the microscope); (iii) the presence of ash, and (iv) burnt (reddened) or charcoal-rich soil fragments. In addition, the presence of limpid clay accumulations in soils where they are not in agreement with local soil-forming factors can be considered as a potential indirect indicator of burning.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/9d1de7d8ad61ca31a5ed049d171749c0735edcb4724f831618ff0560230752eb.jpg)  
Figure 28.6 Forest vegetation clearance by fire. (a) Burning of a future crop field. (b) Soil surface with abundant charcoal and ash after burning. Forchtenberg project (Germany), 2012. Image courtesy of Laténium.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/123754d86d06c8fa278fc3a137df0ea7b8614ec6b0c15577b29ce3b1837a1c75.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/054b519d281deacb46f3e016f0a7756e4ede271bf2f38affe412553dc4099957.jpg)  
Figure 28.7 Traces of vegetation clearance by fire preserved under a Neolithic tumulus. (a) Ash accumulated on the soil surface from final burning of vegetation just before monument construction. As the ash was not washed away or dissolved, we can infer that its burial was very quick. PPL. (b) Detail of undisturbed fragmented microcharcoal concentrated at the top of the ancient soil surface and produced by burning of low vegetation (bushes, grass) before monument construction. PPL. Neolithic tumulus, Er Grah, Locmariaquer, Morbihan, France] (see Gebhardt & Marguerie 2006).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/a1aac849d41cc9f4dbaab02df4d6df0a65193dd177d6f116a37fc05a513cda3f.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/382efcbef9b829ad1160388cb72910fd464510c808cd16198de16960b1c89b45.jpg)  
Figure 28.8 Large charcoal fragment in a Neolithic soil, indicating forest clearance by burning. PPL. Bevaix Plateau (Neuchâtel, Switzerland). Image courtesy of Laténium.

# 28.2.2 Postdepositional Transformations

Surfaces subjected to burning and their associated features might be well preserved (e.g., under Neolithic tumuli – Gebhardt & Marguerie 2006; Figure 28.7a and b), but the preservation of indicators of vegetation clearance by fire is not always good. As with all other micromorphological features, both direct and indirect traces of burning might be erased or transformed by pedogenic processes, be lost by erosion, or be incorporated into the soil by pedoturbation (biological activity, swelling/shrinking and tillage/digging). The following facts concerning the preservation potential of such features can be useful in the interpretation of traces of vegetation clearance by burning:

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/3710266be4162f843fe8a6afebb3ec3f221a1da6c34ad53bae60a7778f97473c.jpg)  
Figure 28.9 Charcoal weathered in alkaline soil conditions, showing considerable localized disaggregation, resulting in further fragmentation. Cavities formed by charcoal disintegration have been filled with illuvial clay. PPL. Early Neolithic Pit Fill (Elsloo, The Netherlands). Image courtesy of D. J. Huisman.

- Charcoal is rather resistant to various chemical processes and might be well preserved in buried soils (Figures 28.7b and 28.8). However, studies suggest that in alkaline, potassium-rich environments charcoal might be weathered and fragmented (Huismann et al. 2012 - Figure 28.9). Charcoal from a variety of types of source events can be fragmented mechanically due to repeated ploughing (Figure 28.10), and the occurrence of a high concentration of microcharcoal has been interpreted as such (Bednarz et al. 2006).  
- Under leaching conditions, calcitic ash is rapidly dissolved and washed out of the system unless there are

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/8bbebc21b31b0a77c43d86b944d0cfb6d7b9b85de192abf35c054eb20cfb6fc8.jpg)  
Figure 28.10 Strongly-fragmented charcoal in the surface horizon of a buried soil, suggesting repeated ploughing during the Neolithic. Bevaix Plateau (Neuchâtel, Switzerland). Reproduced with permission from Bednarz et al. 2006, p. 114.

exceptional burial conditions (see Figure 28.7a), and in most cases it will not be visible in thin section, although may be inferred through proxy indicators (see example above regarding limpid clay coatings in acidic soils). In calcareous environments, calcitic ash might be well preserved (Canti 2003; Canti & Brochier 2017, this book). Siliceous ash, rich in phytoliths, is highly resistant to weathering and leaching as long as the soil pH is below 8.5 (Karkanas 2010), and is a valuable indicator of both burning and the herbaceous origin of burnt vegetation. Geochemical studies, however, suggest that with high rates of water flow, phytoliths might be dissolved even under conditions of less alkaline to slightly acid geochemical conditions (Karkanas 2010).  
- Charcoal and burnt (oxidized) soil fragments from the burning of tree stumps or Ember production fireplaces might be resistant to water erosion (sheet wash and rill) and might be found in colluvial deposits (see below). Therefore in such environmental settings they do not bear witness to in situ vegetation clearance by fire but they are indirect indicators of such activities upslope.

# 28.2.3 Possible Misinterpretations and Tools for Accurate Understanding

Indicators of direct and indirect burning from vegetation clearance can be misinterpreted or confused with: (i) fireplaces for various purposes (domestic fireplaces, ovens, kilns, charcoal mounds); (ii) natural fires, particularly of coniferous forest and savannah/steppe; (iii) manuring spreads with charcoal-rich household waste. All of these might give similar micromorphological evidence. High-quality and object-oriented field observations, along with interdisciplinary collaborations are necessary to separate these (see Table 28.1).

Table 28.1 Summary of features indicative of burning and not related to vegetation clearance.  

<table><tr><td>Burning setting</td><td>Criteria to distinguish from traces left by vegetation clearance</td></tr><tr><td>Fireplaces</td><td>Typical field morphologies; associated traces are confined to a circumscribed area; exceptions: fireplaces meant to produce embers (see text)</td></tr><tr><td>Non-anthropogenic forest fires and grass fires</td><td>Site-specific field data combined with palynological and anthracological information</td></tr><tr><td>Manuring</td><td>Association with artefacts (potsherd, bone) and allochtonous soil material or phosphate-rich pedofeatures Chemical data: phosphorus enrichment</td></tr></table>

# 28.3 Micromorphological Features Indicative of Bare Surfaces

# 28.3.1 Environmental Background and Relationship to Anthropic Activity

One of the major consequences of vegetation clearance and/or working of soils by various implements is the creation of more or less continuous bare surfaces. Under such conditions, the soil surface is affected by the impact of rainfall and wind. The emphasis on the features presented below is related to the fact that under natural conditions, regions in the temperate climatic belt were mostly covered by vegetation during the Holocene. Thus any evidence of bare surfaces there might suggest vegetation clearance by human activity. The exceptions to this statement arise from the situation of a particular landscape position (e.g., alluvial plains, steep slopes), where erosion processes regularly create bare surfaces without human intervention. Natural forest fires, which occur mainly in coniferous forests, can also produce both evidence of large-scale burning and subsequent bare-earth conditions. In such cases, it is crucial that the local environment and its known history are accounted for when interpreting any micromorphological indicators of human activity. Under certain environmental conditions, such as in Atlantic Europe or the humid tropics, vegetation regeneration is extremely quick, such that evidence of bare surfaces in the Holocene is often the result of significant human intervention and perhaps maintenance of clearings (e.g., for cultivation, intensive grazing, routeways or settlement). For example, soil studies, including micromorphological investigations of soils covered by and dated through overlying archaeological structures, suggest that the landscape

of northwestern Europe has been affected by human clearance and disturbance since at least the Neolithic, and probably earlier (e.g., Macphail et al. 1990; Gebhardt 1993; Langohr 2001; Bork & Lang 2003; Goldberg & Macphail 2006; French et al. 2007; Dotterweich & Dreibrodt 2011; Lespez 2012; Ertlen et al. 2012; Gebhardt et al. 2014). Less is known about other areas with different environmental conditions, such as under a more continental climate or after a major volcanic eruption. Here the regeneration of naturally disturbed vegetation may take place after many years or even decades, so the use of the microfeatures presented here needs particular caution. More study of past human impact on landscapes in regions with varying environmental conditions is needed in order to expand our understanding of these features, and their inherent meaning for anthropic soil transformation.

The development of various micromorphological features indicative of bare surfaces is strongly dependent on the climate and local weather patterns, timespan of exposure of the bare surface, the texture of the soil, slope parameters and the characteristics of the surface horizon (ISSS 1996). Indeed, strongly organic and highly bioturbated surface horizons are known to be greatly resistant to these processes (relatively speaking) and/or to effectively erase indicators within one season, explaining why in certain situations known past agricultural activities are not reflected through associated soil characteristics. Lewis (2012) provides examples showing 'erasing' of surface and in-profile crusts within one summer season of bioturbation in Rendzinas. With the exception of soils characterized by high stability or high rates of reworking, the impact of rainfall on bare soil surfaces can be traced and the main micromorphological indicators seen are briefly presented below.

# 28.3.2 Micromorphological Features formed on Soil Surfaces: Traces of Splash and Crusts

# 28.3.2.1 Formation Processes and Morphology of Features

When a soil surface is bare, the impact of rainfall may induce several processes. Raindrops disaggregate peds by rain splash (ISSS 1996) causing reorganization of the mineral grains and the finer part of the soil matrix, often described as traces of splash (Figure 28.11). In archaeological samples these features are sometimes visible in the field, and can be recognized in thin sections. Under the microscope, sorting of fine and coarse material provides the morphological evidence for this process (Figure 28.12).

When the soil surface is bare, the formation of surface crusts (Figures 28.13 and 28.14) is also commonly recorded (ISSS 1996). The genesis and morphology of

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/649d0b6e8ea593842da46b0f8d2120f4d268c79c4555dbd99d72bdb278c6c8a6.jpg)  
Figure 28.11 Present-day bare soil surface affected by rain-splash. The coarse and fine soil fragments are detached and sorted by the impact of raindrops. Brno region (Czech Republic). Image courtesy of Laténium.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/8ee64919a23f7d8f64114af64dc6d8e8266f977788938e98837c543df394e1ca.jpg)  
Figure 28.12 Traces of splash in a prehistoric colluvial sequence. Note the sorting of coarse (c) and organic-rich fine (f) material. PPL. Bevaix Plateau (Neuchâtel, Switzerland). Image courtesy of Laténium.

these features were thoroughly reviewed by Valentin & Bresson (1992), who describe two main crust types. Structural or slaking crusts are the result of the in situ breakdown of soil aggregates to individual particles due to rain splash and slaking (loss of soil structure by wetting). Depositional crusts are built up of sediments from the lateral and vertical redistribution of particles and soil fragments by rain splash, overland flow and/or infiltrating water. There has been an agronomical focus on the morphology of surface crusts from as early as the late 1930s (see reviews by Bresson & Valentin 1992; Pagliai & Stoops 2010), and a large amount of research and literature is devoted to this due to their considerable influence on infiltration, water storage and soil erosion. A detailed review with emphasis on crust micromorphology is

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/9ec49e970ac12a6d0a36c1c7820db1a1899e003d4c1ffd4691f08e8f8f3d1b91.jpg)  
Figure 28.13 Present-day soil surface with a structural crust. Raindrops hitting the soil surface, combined with water flowing over the soil, break soil aggregates into individual particles, which settle to form a more-or-less impermeable surface layer. Bevaix Plateau (Neuchâtel, Switzerland). Image courtesy of Laténium.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/6d83ed311515e8d831d66eb0ac9c9196285ac2172b050dc95668c29a90a93cda.jpg)  
Figure 28.14 Silty surface crust reworked in a colluviated sediment accumulated in a lynchet. PPL. Ruffiac, Morbihan, France.

presented by Mücher et al. (1988), Bresson & Boiffin (1990) and Bresson & Valetin (1992; 1994), and recognition in archaeological contexts is mentioned by Courty et al. (1989). In their overview, Pagliai & Stoops (2010) differentiate between physical and biological crusts. Physical crusts have been divided into structural and sedimentary crusts, which overlap with the above mentioned terminology. A detailed micromorphological description of the various subfacies and components of physical crusts is presented by these authors, and the reader is referred to their publications for further details. Only limited micromorphological research has been associated with the study of biological crusts (Pagliai & Stoops 2010), and to our knowledge they have not been recognized in archaeological contexts. In thin sections, slaking crusts are recognizable as layers characterized by reduced porosity and sorting of grains (Figure 28.14). Depositional crusts are characterized by fine layering of size-sorted and reworked soil material (Figure 28.15a), b). The fin-textured component of these crusts can have a more or less strial b-fabric depending on their granulometric composition and the presence/absence of organic inclusions. In calcareous soils crystallitic b-fabric has been also described (Pagliai & Stoops 2010).

# 28.3.2.2 Possible Postdepositional Evolution

Both traces of splash and the various crusts are surface or near-surface features, and are thus extremely vulnerable in terms of in situ preservation. Due to being at the surface they are among the features that are mostly disturbed and reworked by subsequent pedogenesis (bio- and argilloturbation), erosion and cultivation. Unless the original surface is suddenly buried, the preservation potential of aggregates displaced by splash is low, but they can still be found as part of soil clods that have become incorporated deeper in the soil (see section 28.4

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/1873c525f56e75dd78ff4fe664f2408599a31156bf707c198216b3d4d796bfc8.jpg)  
Figure 28.15 Surface crust made of loam/silt segregation by rain-splash effect and drying in a modern corn field. (a) Scanned thin section. (b) Microscopic detail showing fine layering related to overland flow. Gotesheim, Bas-Rhin, France.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/67380fea4c4b2dc3e08ce85a23818e80411f38d6ab368ba0132d6efabe26e339.jpg)

below). On the other hand, surface crusts, once they have formed, are relatively resistant to disaggregation. As a consequence they may often be integrated into the subsoil as crust fragments by bioturbation or tillage. For example, aggregates inverted by tilling or digging can bring surface crusts into the profile, where they have regularly been recognized archaeologically (e.g., Allen 1988; Courty et al. 1989; Macphail 1992a; Lewis 2012; see also below). Thus, even if the surface horizon is eroded, these features can remain as witness to periods of bare surfaces. They can also become integrated into colluvial deposits (Figure 28.14). With other elements, they can thus be valuable indicators of past vegetation clearance and land management.

# 28.3.3 Traces Recorded in the Subsoil:

# 'Dusty' Coatings

# 28.3.3.1 Definition and Morphology

The terms dirty or dusty coatings or infillings refer to textural pedofeatures found on the surfaces of voids and peds. Through careful observation, they are sometimes discernible in the field with the naked eye, but most frequently are identified in thin sections. They are made up of the combination of one or more of the following components: more or less oriented clay, silt, sand and organic matter (Figure 28.16a-e, see also Jongerius 1970; Bullock et al. 1985; Kuhn et al. 2010). In thin sections, dusty coatings are characterized by a speckled or dotted aspect, a major distinction with limpid clay coatings (Figure 28.16f). Dusty coatings are speckled or dotted, as the downward mobilization of disturbed soil material is unsorted and often includes small mineral and organic components. The internal fabric can be nonlaminated, layered or compound layered. In many cases the individual layers may be parallel to the surfaces of voids or structural surfaces on which they have been deposited, although this is not always the case. Grain size may vary from clay to sand particles. Their thickness varies from a few micrometres to  $1\mathrm{mm}$  in exceptional cases. Extinction lines in the fin-textured layers are either diffuse or absent.

# 28.3.3.2 Formation Processes and Relationship to Anthropic Activity

Dusty coatings are the result of mobilization by water of soil components after the soil surface is bare and aggregate bonds are disrupted (Jongerius 1983). Seepage, overland flow and raindrop impact on bare or partly vegetated soil can result in partial soil disintegration, detachment of soil particles and their translocation into soil voids and onto aggregate surfaces (Yariv 1976; Tarchitzky et al. 1984; Rousseva 1989; Macphail 1992a). Buurman et al. (1998) and Kuhn et al. (2010) emphasize that these coatings, resulting from mechanical infiltration, are

fundamentally different to the limpid coatings related to dispersion/flocculation of clay colloids during leaching of soils with a more or less continuous vegetation cover (but see above regarding burning and limpid coatings). It has been observed that their formation can be favoured by low organic carbon, iron, calcium and magnesium, and high silt content (Nettleton et al. 1994; Kuhn et al. 2010).

Dusty coatings were first described by Jongerius (1970) on the surface of planar voids and ped surfaces of horizons immediately below the ploughed layers of Dutch arable and horticultural lands, and were called 'agricutans'. Later, the USDA Soil Taxonomy described similar coatings and infillings accumulated in agric horizons at the base of plough layers (Soil Survey Staff 1999; 2010). Based on this recognition, a trend emerged to attribute the presence of these features to agricultural practices and to link their absence with the absence of such activities. Subsequently other studies revealed that in several cases dusty coatings were not found in layers where, or in which, past agriculture was well documented (e.g., Carter & Davidson 1998; Usai 2001; Adderley et al. 2010). This supports the fact that the formation of such accumulations in agricultural lands depends on the kind of tillage, soil texture, organic matter content, water saturation / drainage, intensity of rainfall, presence of obstacles to the downward movement of particles, presence of suitable voids, pH-base saturation and time of year (Jongerius 1970, 1983). To conclude, their use as diagnostic tools of anthropic activity and, in particular, of past cultivation practices is site specific and has to be handled with care (Macphail 1998).

# 28.3.3.3 Possible Misinterpretations and Tools for an Accurate Understanding

It is also important to keep in mind that more or less sorted soil particle displacement and dusty coating formation have been described in two additional environmental settings where a bare soil surface is not a constraint. In alluvial plains, dusty coating formation has been attributed to downward translocation by flood-induced hydraulic pressures of dispersed particles due to reduction (Brammer 1971). Freeze-thaw activity in soils may also cause internal erosion/sedimentation processes seen in thin sections by dusty accumulations in pores and planar voids (Svestrup et al. 2005; Van Vliet-Lanoe 2010). A focus on site-specific interpretation of the observed feature is the recommended strategy in order to avoid confusion. It is also useful to keep in mind that the creation of bare surfaces and disturbance is not always related to agricultural processes (e.g., settlement space, charcoal mounds etc.), or even necessarily to cultural activities (see above).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/2a663dc96ccb91a58f62ff88d5e90260f158b5c47b11f9b600124fbc36f1614a.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/f595704bf4bca0d2ab5e1fa012ef2dd09079afde34e9da5b1d4904c48ed508b9.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/657b3a34352f3fa08920ab408d9e4417256ece72e5b48a4ba63db68614986c06.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/b98dcec40ff38aa4c66ea1c58fca685dbcdbec82bac1ff169e4e5f2c3146dbbd.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/125f1e86018acd5da6744b2569cf0eb32f53199d099d6577695d656e37ecdfa7.jpg)

They are characterized by a speckled to dotted limpidity in PPL, which is a distinctive characteristic compared to limpid clay coatings (f, marked by arrow, see also Figure 28.19a). ![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/0afa85f155ff9a7e81a9f37f5183bcfea4e3083325c93af9bf509440167a6b3d.jpg)

# 28.3.3.4 Postdepositional Changes

As with many other pedofeatures, dusty coatings can be broken or disturbed by bioturbation, argilloturbation or tilling. When integrated into the soil matrix, they cannot

be easily distinguished from pedoturbated surface crusts. One exception to this has been reported by Lewis (1998, 2012) in experimental studies in which crusts and infillings were distinct.

# 28.3.4 Micromorphology of Colluvial Deposits: Indirect and Landscape-Scale Indicators of Vegetation Clearance and Land Management

# 28.3.4.1 Formation Processes and Morphology

Colluvial deposits are slope sediments. Although the processes involved are similar for natural and human induced slope erosion and colluvial sedimentation events, in this chapter we only consider the environmental settings where these processes are triggered by anthropic activity, using temperate climatic zones and slopes not subjected to major natural gravitational movements as an example (see Leopold & Volkel 2007 for more details). Virtually bare soil surfaces resulting from vegetation clearance and tillage are subjected to wind and water erosion by sheet wash, rill formation and gulling (Figure 28.17a, b; see also Bell & Boardman 1992). In addition, tillage processes contribute substantially to

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/3625c9c5b0bff77569b9c4457e29af33f9ee13d57af0cc7924cfc2d0cec36e09.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/d31fa134503e42690df5ea0b23845968d94c622de326c641ff394caf5abff15a.jpg)  
Figure 28.17 Bare surfaces reworked during a heavy rainstorm. (a) General image. (b) Details. The fine material is the result of sheet-wash processes, while the coarse fragments were displaced by rill-wash processes and/or accumulated as a result of selective erosion of the surrounding fine material. Neuchâtel region (Switzerland). Image courtesy of Laténium.

the lateral and vertical reworking of soils (Kwaad & Mucher 1979; Van Oost et al. 2000). As a result, in certain landscape positions the upper part of soils may be partially or totally lost, and these reworked sediments accumulate as colluvial deposits in other parts of the same landscape (Figure 28.5). As soil erosion and conservation issues are important in agriculture, the factors and processes involved in the formation of colluvium in present-day agroenvironments have been widely studied since the 1960s (e.g., Meyer & Monke 1965; Bell & Boardman 1992; ISSS 1996). Although colluvial deposits are often relatively poor regarding artefact context, archaeo-environmental studies have been undertaken, as the deposits themselves are potential indirect indicators of past human activities (Butzer 1982; Allen 1992; Bell 1992; Evans 1992). They provide evidence for clearance in areas that formerly had a continuous vegetation cover, and of upslope land use in areas already cleared of vegetation (Allen 1988; Courty et al. 1989; Macphail 1992a; Goldberg & Macphail 2006; Bednarz et al. 2006). The presence/absence of colluvium in the landscape is the result of complex interactions between land management techniques, slope type, soil and climatic factors, as well as stream erosion, which removes colluvium, and this complexity is summarized by Leopold & Volkel (2007). Colluvial deposits are in general poorly sorted, but often they are extremely homogeneous over great depths. Although they might partially be created by intermittent depositional events, often they are the result of the buildup of thin sedimentary layers over longer time periods, which are later reworked or mixed by repeated bioturbation and tillage, generating a homogeneous nature.

The micromorphological investigation of colluvial deposits related to water transport was already underway in the 1970s (Mücher 1974). A detailed description of the microfacies associated with a wide range of slope deposits is presented by Bertran & Texier (1999), who also discuss anthropic colluvium. Mücher et al. (2010) summarize the features observable under a microscope. Studies with a micromorphological component of erosion, resilience and soil buildup in terraced field systems are discussed by Goodman-Elgar (2008) and Castillo et al. (2013). Table 28.2 gives an overview of the micromorphological characteristics of the types of colluvial deposits discussed above (see also Figures 28.18a and b and 28.19a and b).

As far as we are aware there are no micromorphological investigations that characterize deposits resulting from aeolian processes and activated by land use. This might be because windblown deposits are usually identified by their sorting, which is obvious in the field, and grain size distribution, which is usually provided by laboratory analyses. However, aeolian deposits triggered by

Table 28.2 Micromorphological characteristics of colluvia and their environmental and anthropic soil management significance. (Based on: Mücher 1974; Gebhardt 1988, 1995; Bertran & Texier 1990; Macphail 1992a; Bednarz et al. 2006; Goldberg & Macphail 2006; Mücher et al. 2010.)

Relatively quick accumulation</td><td>Little or no tillage. human activity might have huge consequences, and the famous Dust Bowl of the 1930s in North America is an example of the potential scale. In archaeological contexts such deposits have sometimes been related to ancient agricultural practices (e.g., Limbrey 1975; French et al. 2007). Several geoarchaeological studies suggest that aeolian sand deposits were managed by Bronze Age agropastoral communities in northern Europe, and that eventually these sedimentary events had serious consequences for the occupation history of several sites (e.g., Mikkelsen et al. 2007; French 2010).

# 28.3.4.2 Possible Postdepositional Changes and their Importance

Colluvial sequences are often the result of several phases of erosion or sedimentation events, thus the absence of surface features is rather common. The postdepositional changes that are particularly relevant are those related to

pedogenesis. As indicated in Table 28.2, microstructure, b-fabric and pedofeatures are the elements that suggest postdepositional changes that can be used to discuss the intensity of anthropic activity during the accumulation of these soil-sedimentary deposits. As discussed above, individual features might be the result of several processes. Nevertheless, a thorough study of superposition of several indices and comparison with field and analytical evidence can give crucial information on evolution and land use changes recorded in often homogeneous colluvial deposits (e.g., Allen 1992; Bell 1992; Bednarz et al. 2006).

# 28.3.5 Particular Situations of Vegetation Clearance reflected in Micromorphology

# 28.3.5.1 Tree Falls

Tree uprooting resulting from windthrows is a natural phenomenon triggered by storm events, weight of snow

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/de85c19b1d68bb9f81b289490d7e11a4548158b5d611cab258fbc1d0ab097afa.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/d904a15a0c7d11a8b9509090c9f9e5bf293397f585919a6466c1074767eee5c8.jpg)  
Figure 28.19 Soil fragments in colluvium. (a) Limpid clay coatings observed in an undisturbed Bt horizon of a relict Luvisol. PPL. (b) Reworked Bt horizon fragment (arrow) observed in colluvium accumulated over a relict Bt horizon. PPL. Imling, Moselle, France.

or ice, or fall of another tree. They contribute substantially to forest regeneration, and are important in terms of disturbance and mixing of upper soil horizons (Langohr 1991; Holliday 2004). Tree uprooting can also be manmade as part of forest clearance or selection of wood for construction, although actual uprooting is not always a part of these activities. The uprooting of a tree creates a hollow in the soil and disturbance to all of the affected horizons, often over considerable depth depending on the tree species, age and depth of water table. The traces left are often visible in the field as infilled hollows or 'pits', particularly if the affected soil is

characterized by strongly differentiated horizons. The reader is referred to the work of Langohr (1991) for more details on the morphology of these features and their importance for the understanding of archaeological sites. Micromorphological features indicative of tree uprooting have been discussed in detail elsewhere (e.g., Macphail 1987; Courty et al. 1989; Macphail & Goldberg 1990; Macphail et al. 1990; Goldberg & Macphail 2006). The features potentially detectable in thin sections of the fills are: (i) mixed soil fragments; (ii) traces of bare surfaces / puddling (traces of particle sorting due to the splash effects of rainfall, crusts and dusty textural pedofeatures in the subsoil), and (iii) concentrations of charcoal, ash and possibly reddened/oxidized earth fragments if burning occurred (see section 28.2 above). Occasionally even limpid clay coatings have been reported (Cremaschi & Nicosia 2012), although these are normally associated with stable, vegetation-covered surfaces (see discussion in section 28.2.1 regarding how these form in a burning situation). Slager & Van de Wetering (1977) and Courty et al. (1989) suggest that the microfabric of the associated fills can give valuable information on the dynamics of hollow filling. For example, strongly homogenized, bioturbated fabric is likely to be related to slow infilling and reorganization, while a heterogeneous fill may be explained by quick deposition of the components. Treefall often occurs in wet areas such as bogs where shallow-rooted trees are overturned by wind. These hollows may show indications of ponding (e.g., layers of fine particle deposition, 'crusting', waterlogging etc.), since they are often exposed for long periods of time with standing water before infilling. Where ponding occurs, however, infilling should show fine lenses of heterogeneous deposits for some extended time, which may become mixed into later fills; heterogeneity cannot always be explained by quick infilling. For these reasons and to understand these and other types of pit fills it is important to investigate the nature of the microscopic layers and fill components. A subsequent ploughing event can also create homogenization, which affects both the depression morphology and the fill, while a rapid burial could prevent homogenization. Site-specific parameters are extremely important in order to avoid misinterpretation. All of these elements should be discussed individually for each case study, and the presence/absence of associated features should not be neglected in the discussion of treefalls as elements of vegetation clearance.

# 28.3.5.2 Charcoal Mounds

Vegetation clearance and associated soil disturbance occur in the particular case of the creation of charcoal mounds. Soil micromorphological study of several profiles located under ancient and recent charcoal mounds

reveals that the impact of charcoal preparation on the soil is not directly due to combustion effects, but related to local processes of deforestation for wood exploitation, the preparation and management of plots before, during and after the charcoal production phase, and the domestic activities of the charcoal burners (Gebhardt 2007). During this procedure the soil is cleared of organic remains and humus-rich surface horizons, sometimes down to  $20\mathrm{cm}$  depth, and vegetation or roots are extracted with hoes, spades and other implement types. Terraces are built on slopes. Under the mound at the contact with the soil, the wood is usually not completely burnt. After cooling, the stack is opened, charcoal is raked out, slightly broken, sorted and used or stored. The site is cleaned and the fine earth is sieved and kept for reuse in the next charcoal mound. Thus, besides the large amount of concentrated charcoal, the potential micromorphological traces of charcoal mound fabrication are those that relate to bare surfaces (traces of splash, surface crusts and dusty coatings and infillings) and those created by trampling and digging. Melted silica features are not expected to occur, as the required temperatures are not reached in the underlying soil. On the contrary, their presence and later integration into the soil could be related to the auxiliary activities (e.g., open fires for cooking, heating, metallurgy) around charcoal production.

# 28.4 Micromorphology of Structural and Textural Features Revealing the use of Implements

# 28.4.1 Introduction

Recent decades of ethno-archaeological and experimental archaeological research, followed by investigations of archaeological sites of various periods, support the view that the implements used for past cultivation can leave structural and textural traces, some of which may only be visible under the microscope (Gebhardt 1991, 1995; Macphail 1998; Lewis 1998, 2002; Bednarz et al. 2006). These features are reviewed here. Other agrarian processes such as the past inundation of fields for rice cultivation can also be traced by several lines of evidence visible under the microscope and they are summarized in the final part of this section.

Implements are used to disturb the soil for various reasons such as: (i) the creation of tilth; (ii) the digging of holes/ditches/pits etc., or (iii) the removal of topsoil for various reasons (e.g., construction, levelling) (Lewis 2012). Among these, recognition of the micromorphological traces left in soils by arable farming activities is a challenge. Since the 1990s several object-oriented

experimental studies have been conducted, resulting in the recognition of a range of soil micromorphological data on soil structure and textural pedofeatures generated by traditional implements (Gebhardt 1995, 1999; Lewis 1998, 2012). The observed features are mainly situated in two distinct zones of soils disturbed by implements: changes in surface horizons (section 28.4.2), and structural modifications and textural accumulation features at and around the zone where the implements interacted with the soil (sections 28.4.3 and 28.4.4). Research to date allows us to give an overview of the macro- and micromorphological characteristics of the use of various implements (section 28.4.5).

# 28.4.2 Structural modification of soils related to the use of implements

Implement disturbance creates a variety of macroscopic and microscopic features, and changes the characteristics of the soil profile. The relatively strong impact of implement use results in long-lasting features at various scales, from a field-identified ditch or spade mark feature, down to microscopic indicators of compaction, compression, 'loosening' (Lewis 2012), and various elements of disaggregation, surface processes and leaching, as discussed in previous sections. In certain cases, so-called profile inversion features are also seen, such as enrichment in organic matter at depth, or the presence of B or C (or even R) horizon materials in the topsoil, which may sometimes (but not always) be interpreted as relating to implement type (e.g., plough or spade versus ard). A general summary of the processes seen in experimental study with 'traditional' or reconstructed ancient implements and the microscopic structural and textural features characteristic of tilled horizons are summarized in Table 28.3.

# 28.4.3 Structural Zones created by Implementers

Ard tilling experiments show breakup of soil into angular, relatively large blocky clods found in ridges and occasionally in ard mark fills, and small, more rounded aggregates (Figure 28.20a, b and c) found in ridges and comprising much of the loose furrow fill (Gebhardt 1990; Lewis 1998, 2012). These types and arrangement of aggregates influence soil porosity and density, and echo those resulting from modern tillage with vertical and forward raked narrow tines, which bring larger clods to the surface and leave smaller ones below (Spoor 1969). Where untilled comparative material is available, such patterns might be recognized archaeologically, but variation in soil type and subsequent land use will usually add uncertainty to interpretations of ancient tillage based on these aspects of soil structure. Secondary processes seen in well-preserved archaeological implement marks

include enhanced leaching in feature fills due to their 'looser' structure, and subsequent alterations in c/f ratio within and adjacent to implement features (vs. in the surrounding untilled soil). Such leaching includes eluviation and illuviation of fine fraction materials in all studied soils, as well as the translocation of iron and organic matter in Podzols (Lewis 1998, 2012).

Aggregates from the lower part of the implement impact zone are drawn up into the surface of the furrow, and fragments from upper layers tumble into the furrow fill (Lewis 1998), mixing surface and subsurface horizons (Figure 28.21a and b). The bases of furrows are often lined with microaggregates moved down by gravity to a zone at the feature cut. These characteristics have been

noted with all types of traction implements, including reconstructed prehistoric ards. The movement of B horizon materials into the surface of implement mark features has been identified archaeologically in ancient spade marks (Lewis 1998, 2002) and mouldboard plough marks (see below).

An extremely important structural alteration is the creation of compaction or compression zones immediately under and around the soil mixed and loosened by implements (Figures 28.22a, b and c, 28.23 and 28.24), sometimes noted as compaction and 'panning' features observed in the field. The location of a compaction zone within the first centimetre underlying marks is in line with findings based on modern tillage (Jongerius 1983). Shear planes formed by the downwards and sideways pressure from the implement have been observed around ard, plough and spade mark cuts, at right angles to the side and echoing the cut under the base of the marks

(Lewis 1998, 2012). In cases with modern machinery, additional features such as striated b-fabrics and substantial dusty coating ('agricutan') formation are seen (e.g., Jongerius 1970, 1983), but the features discussed here are seen with even the simplest of digging or tilling implements, and are thus more relevant to archaeological studies. Shear planes created around the cut are very important to the location of microscopic features related to secondary processes such as illuviation (discussed below). These voids, as seen both experimentally and archaeologically, are often infilled around both traction and nontraction implement marks of many ages (Fisher & Macphail 1985; Macphail 1987, 1990, 1992a, 1992b; Lewis 1998, 2002, 2012; French 2003).

In certain cases, compressed peds (Figure 28.25) have been identified at the base of implement marks, particularly in relation to traction implements such as wooden ards. These rounded or 'squashed' aggregates are seen

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/bece602f5f661ce59ce0aab549ec72542b243d8be0bbe0763c9d4f4a980a3f63.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/71cdbc7ef0ca5a680872a0e3033e5d17dce48df46f20011c81587ae64de85fa7.jpg)  
Figure 28.21 Soil disturbance created by a traditional mouldboard plough. (a) Field image showing lighter coloured fragments of B horizon reworked upwards. (b) Detail of the lighter coloured fragments of B horizon. The clods are larger and more angular than the ones created by an ard (Morbihan, France).

under the location of passage of the ard shares, and especially under arrow-shaped shares. They are directly related to implement motion and pressure, and have not been observed in experiments with other implements (e.g., spades, hoes, etc.) (Lewis 1998, 2012).

# 28.4.4 Textural 'Panning' and Infilling Pedofeatures associated with Implement Cut Features

A number of secondary processes related to the creation of a 'compaction zone' within the soil at the edge of implement features result in characteristics frequently

identified in the archaeological field and microscopic record (Lewis 1998, 2012), including iron panning, clay infillings and sometimes crystalline infillings (Lewis 1998, 2003). The resulting 'loosened' and 'compacted' zones create a major change in the soil profile, seeing increased leaching internal to the feature, and illuviation of clay and iron at the edge of the feature, thus subsequently enhancing the impact of the compaction zone for the long term, except where there is strong bioturbation (Lewis 2012). In modern fields or well-preserved archaeological cases, plough pans and/or dusty coatings are observed at these locations, and archaeologically the edges and sides ('cuts') of implement marks have been associated with iron coatings and hypocoatings linked to clay accumulation in void spaces (Figure 28.26).

Translocated silty clay can occur at the base and sides of the tillage disturbance zone (Figure 28.27a and b). Thus, infillings and pans can be observed within a  $1\mathrm{cm}$  zone on either side, often in linear / planar voids created by the action of the implement (Jongerius 1983; Gebhardt 1990; Lewis 1998, 2012). The creation of a textural and density boundary at this point in the profile may play a major role in later secondary characteristics, such as root orientation, the development of iron and clay pans, and possibly, in less bioturbated and strongly leaching soils, even in soil horizon formation. These discrete panning features (Figure 28.26) are composed of unsorted very fine mixed microaggregates, silty clay infillings and some sand (Lewis 1998, 2012).

Soils affected by cultivation associated with irrigation and artificial drainage show additional characteristics (Moorman & van Breemen 1978; Yukawa 1989; Lee et al. 2014). The physical and chemical changes caused by frequent saturation and drainage cycles in paddy fields, for example, are discussed in detail by several scholars (Brinkman 1977; Greenland 1981; Barnes 1990; Bouma et al. 1990; Kyuma 2004; Pang et al. 2006, 2007; Adderley et al. 2010; Kogel-Knabner et al. 2010). The available micromorphological studies account for a large range of soil characteristics visible in thin section (Figure 28.28a-e) and they are summarized in Table 28.4. Individually these soil characteristics might also be present in other environmental contexts, but their associated presence seems to be significant for identifying this particular form of soil management. Two aspects seem to be particularly relevant: (i) ploughing a soil while it is being flooded or dried, a practice seen in paddy agriculture, causes the formation of many textural pedofeatures (dusty coatings) associated with soil characteristics indicative of various irrigation schemes, induced intensive and repeated oxidation and reduction conditions; (ii) the presence of textural pedofeatures described as greyish silty clay concentration features (Lee et al. 2014), suggesting the transformation of groundmass by intensive oxidation and reduction pro

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/e0e195e60aacc7ca2508b4b75c4b748fa7747e3358180cfe7baf0a04c7d4036b.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/9c374d2f41f442d887ef95b098eb57932bfea8e660e9344b6eb4b19ac9b21e27.jpg)  
Figure 28.22 Plough pan zone, created by compaction by an implement. (a) Plough pan detectable between lower, undisturbed soil (LUS) and upper, reworked soil (URS). ![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/37f353135749426d782b720fb9db34ddb7a4dbd97dca2a58b4688fd63badfb16.jpg)

cesses including ferrolysis (liberation of iron and alteration of clay minerals), and their subsequent mixing in the soil matrix due to argilloturbation processes. These are particularly active in such regularly alternating water saturated and drained conditions.

# 28.4.5 Distinguishing Implement Type through the Study of Feature Characteristics

As discussed by Gebhardt (1990, 1999) and Lewis (2002, 2012), variations in structure and organization can be

observed in relation to the use of different ancient implements. This general observation is unsurprising considering that various implements are designed specifically to interact with the soil in particular ways. Identifying such organizations and structures in ancient soils might be possible (e.g., Gebhardt 2004; Gebhardt & Langohr 2015), and is still under investigation. Distinguishing past implement types is readily done where macroscopic implement marks survive – e.g., distinguishing ard from mouldboard plough marks is often possible from feature depth, shape, angle and indicators of the soil having been

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/a3a1edb3b3177e4067d25f2d30752efeaa02ceb7a6ea69cac56c5b07e6ac40c0.jpg)  
Figure 28.23 Structural zones with rounded clods observed in an ancient furrow made by an ard. Protohistoric field, Gotesheim, France.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/7677230d437aea4c7a65b29a7deab4a9ac16d43a54121c4e470a1ba0a305b086.jpg)  
Figure 28.24 Compaction zone revealing an implement cut. Fine material is compressed along this surface. XPL. Neolithic surface horizon. Bevaix Plateau (Neuchâtel, Switzerland). Reproduced with permission from Bednárz et al. 2006, p. 115.

turned. Likewise, identifying spade marks is relatively simple in the field, although distinguishing these from hoe marks may be problematic at times.

Micromorphologically it is possible to clarify ancient marks where indicators are not obvious in the field – for

instance, micromorphological study of a furrow could help to identify whether a turning or pushing implement was involved in its creation through microfeatures indicating profile inversion (e.g., crusts, organic matter content and location, feature fill arrangements) (Lewis 1998, 2002, 2012). It may also be possible to suggest whether a traction implement was used on the basis of certain specific structural characteristics (e.g., flattened or 'rolled' aggregates in specific parts of the mark; Lewis 1998, 2012). On occasion, as noted above, indicators of past cultivation can be visible at depths where there are no macroscopic features; tillage or digging can then be proposed through the identification of implement mark zones (such as infilled planar voids) or bare surfaces (such as crusts) (Figure 28.29). The main field and soil micromorphological characteristics of furrows are summarized in Table 28.5 based on Gebhardt (1990, 1995, 1999) and Lewis (2012).

# 28.4.6 Postformation Changes and Possible Misinterpretations

Experimental studies show that cultivated structures are quickly altered in biologically active soils like Rendzinas, and the literature suggests that it is difficult to interpret ancient land use on the basis of structural indicators alone. Whether any of the structural characteristics will survive in archaeological situations depends on soil types and burial conditions. Implement marks are also subject to alteration over time, and can be virtually erased through bioturbation and subsequent land use. It should always be remembered that the impact of implements is not only related to cultivated fields. Indeed any use of an implement for preparing the soil can produce a structural and textural change, as for example the preparation of the soil before charcoal-making activities (see above), monument construction, or settlement activity.

# 28.4.7 Archaeological Examples

The features discussed in this chapter have been recognized in numerous micromorphological studies concerning archaeological sites of various periods. Mixing of surface and subsurface horizons has frequently been cited as a good micromorphological indicator of ancient possible use of implements or tillage (Macphail 1992a; Currie 1994; Lewis 1998; Gebhardt 2000; Lewis & French 2006; Huisman et al. 2009; Adderley et al. 2010; Nicosia et al. 2011). This is also one of the characteristics for differentiating ploughing from tracks or earthen pathways (see also Rentzel et al. 2017, this book). Some examples of soil structure relevant to ancient ard cultivation are found

Figure 28.26 Bronze Age ard mark with leached fill; cut is demarcated by amorphous organo-mineral panning, mainly of amorphous iron. In colour, the fill is grey, the cut edge reddish brown (thin section size  $6 \times 10\mathrm{cm}$ ). PPL. Reproduced with permission from Lewis 2012, p. 87.

Figure 28.27 Infilling seen at the cut of a typical tillage mark. These infillings are typically 'dusty', composed of very fine silt and clay, 'organic', isotropic, as seen both experimentally and archaeologically. (a) PPL. (b) XPL. Reproduced with permission from Lewis 2012, p. 63.
