![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/5caf27c0413ed26d2962ae3684ffe79bbd799c116fd7743d2f8457f92e15cabf.jpg)  
Figure 28.28 Soils subjected to paddy management. (a) Massive structure and vesicular porosity. The arrows indicate silty (white arrow) and clayey (red arrow) dusty coatings revealing, respectively, drier and wetter soil conditions in a paddy management environment. (b) Impregnative iron-manganese nodules in a strongly iron-depleted groundmass. (c) Silty clay intercalations with superimposed impregnative (amorphous) iron. (d) A silty clay concentration formed with silt-size particles including minute mica flakes and organic particles. The feature has a stipple-speckled b-fabric. (e) Multiple coatings of dusty clay and iron in a vugh. (a), (b), (c) and (e): PPL. (d): XPL.

Table 28.4 Micromorphological characteristics of soils subjected to paddy rice cultivation. (Based on Miura et al. 1992; Vepraskas et al. 1994; Pang et al. 2007; Bresson & Moran 2004; Pagliai & Stoops 2010; Lee et al. 2014.)  

<table><tr><td>Cultivation management</td><td>Pedogenetic processes</td><td>Micromorphological characteristics</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="6">Irrigation/ drainage</td><td rowspan="3">Collapse of soil structure due to repeated water saturation</td><td>• Massive or apedal soil microstructure (Figure 28.28a)</td></tr><tr><td>• Polyconcave vugs</td></tr><tr><td>• Vesicular porosity</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="3">Oxidation and reduction</td><td>• Iron-manganese nodules and reduced soil matrix (Figure 28.28b)</td></tr><tr><td>• Iron hypocoatings</td></tr><tr><td>• Silty clay concentration features (Figure 28.28c and d), formed as a result of intensive hydromorphism including ferrolysis: subangular features, with diffuse boundaries with the groundmass; grey and yellow (PPL), often weakly bright, clay micromass</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="4">Tillage</td><td>Argilloturbation</td><td>• Trapezoidal silty-clay aggregates incorporated in the matrix</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="3">Implement impact</td><td>• Silt concentrations in the clay micromass with diffuse extinction lines</td></tr><tr><td>• Clay aggregates in the groundmass as ‘residual clay’</td></tr><tr><td>• Complex dusty coatings and infillings associated with iron and manganese nodules and hypo-and quasi-coatings (Figure 28.28e)</td></tr></table>

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/8c2ad4020510447631bc63069b666b02d484de4cdb53efcd45c84dcc2cedd77c.jpg)  
Figure 28.29 Experimental context. Crusts and surface infillings (seen here) have a different character to infillings at implement mark cuts (Fig. 28.27); although they contain certain layers that are similar, they also show coarser infillings of silt and sand. (a) PPL. (b) XPL. Reproduced with permission from Lewis 2012, p. 65.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/92e23ec6c2b6c065adeab5bbcbca73413b91ee86635688a594efdd764531c16e.jpg)

in studies at Gotesheim (Schwartz et al. 2009), Entzheim and Moyenvic (Gebhardt 2010), all in France, and are illustrated in Figures 28.20b and c and 28.23. An example of a Neolithic compaction pan is also given from Les Pâquiers site in Switzerland (Bednarzetal. 2006; Figure 28.24). Micromorphological evidence of the impact of ard tillage on a Bronze Age podzolized soil from Denmark was discussed by Lewis (2012; see Figure 28.26), and has also been unravelled for a Punic site (mid-fourth to second century BC) in

Sardinia (Nicosia et al. 2013b). A general review of micromorphological evidence for archaeological marks created by ards, spades, hoes and ploughs has been presented by Lewis (1998, 2012). Paddy soil management in an archaeological context has been studied for the Late Neolithic site of Maoshan, China (Zhuang et al. 2014) and for the Gulhwa and Pyunggeo sites in South Korea, dating to the Bronze Age (c. tenth to fourth century BC) and historical periods (c. seventh to tenth century AD) (Lee et al. 2014).

Table 28.5 Profile forms and soil micromorphological aspects of furrows and marks. (Based on Gebhardt 1990, 1995, 1999; Lewis 1998, 2012.)  

<table><tr><td>Tool</td><td>Size and form of marks, plan and profile</td><td>Space between marks</td><td>Infill organisation of the furrow/mark</td><td>Characteristics of the furrow/mark</td><td>Micromorphological characteristics</td></tr><tr><td>Hoe</td><td>7 cm large, 5–6 cm depth U-shaped profile</td><td>Variable</td><td>Deeply reworked horizons, fine aggregates, fragmentation</td><td>Irregular cuts</td><td>Loose collapsed (starlike) porosity due to intensive watering in experimental observations</td></tr><tr><td>Ard</td><td>Furrows vary depending on shares; usually less than 10 cm wide, up to 15 cm wide with arrow-shaped share, 6–13 cm depth Linear, undulating; sometimes furrow skidding seen. Deeper and wider with iron ploughshare. U or V shape depending on the type of ard; stepped profile with two shares Symmetrical profile</td><td>15–20 to 25–30 cm</td><td>Infill of fragments coming from all horizons interacted with by the tool, substrate fragments mix into higher layers, fine aggregates from upper layers fall down into the furrow base; compaction/compression at the furrow edge; shear planes. Angular and rounded microaggregates of variable size</td><td>Compaction at the base of the furrow; planar voids created at cut outline, and running at right angles to cut; zone underneath cut usually compacted/compressed by implement force; infilling of planar voids marking cut commonly seen</td><td>Loosened and ‘compacted’ zones; flattened/rolled aggregates at ‘cut’; mixing of surface and lower horizons; planar voids at cut and in shear planes; infillings in planar voids, both primary (gravity acting on fine aggregates during tilling) and secondary (leaching)</td></tr><tr><td>Plough</td><td>5–30 cm wide, 5–60 cm depth Linear, undulating, sometimes furrow skidding U or V, asymmetric profile, large</td><td>12–50 cm</td><td>Inverted and mixed horizons (substrate upper, organic lower). Comma shaped inclusions. Large angular aggregates, straight cuts, compacted on certain edges, macroscopically visible</td><td>Compaction at base and edges of the furrow; cuts asymmetric (often one side sharper/staughter, one side wider angled/curved)</td><td>Mixing of horizon materials; enhanced organic nature of fill; inversion; planar voids at cut and as shear planes on sides; infilling of planar voids</td></tr><tr><td>Spade</td><td>5–20 cm depth. Triangular, elongated or rectangular D shape 10 to 27 cm wide V or U profile shape Often found in groups or lines</td><td>Variable</td><td>Inverted horizons (substrate upper, organic lower); large angular aggregates, and mixed smaller angular and subangular aggregates</td><td>One vertical edge and one sloping/slanted edge in plan; rounded or pointed base</td><td>Lower horizon materials brought up into surface; general enhanced organic fills versus surrounding soil; planar voids at cut and in shear planes; planar voids often filled postdepositionally</td></tr></table>

# 28.5 Final Remarks

This review shows that study of past land management techniques through examining microfeatures is particularly challenging. The complexity of the interactions and the resulting features might give the impression that in this research field the glass is more than half empty, and one possibility for dealing with this is to avoid any attempt to use micromorphology. Nevertheless, our research experience and the numerous studies published so far lead us to the conviction that the glass is half or more than half full. Three major aspects of past field and landscape management were discussed in detail: vegetation clearance by fire, bare land surfaces, and soil disturbance by implements, and these are summarized in Table 28.6. This outline does not intend to propose a checklist for easy recognition of

these types of activities. On the contrary, we would like to emphasize the complexity involved in the identification of these ancient practices, which can only be handled through a broad, but in-depth knowledge of the many processes involved, elimination of other options, and careful matching of the various elements in each study context to a set of processes. Throughout this overview we also highlighted the fact that for an accurate interpretation it is equally important to consider the absence of features, as nonpresence can be related to soil properties or explained by taphonomic processes. Last but not least, we reiterate the statement that the 'micromorphologist is a team player' (Macphail & Cruise 2001); micromorphological evidence is a key part of a suite of approaches that constitute essential tools for understanding past human impacts upon and interactions with the soil.

# Animal Gathering Enclosures

Ruth Shahack-Gross

Laboratory for Sedimentaryary Archaeology, Department of Maritime Civilizations, University of Haifa, Israel

# 29.1 Introduction

Animal gathering enclosures are fenced areas where domestic animals are corralled. They have been ubiquitous in the archaeological record since animals were domesticated. The process of animal domestication took place in various parts of the world in different times. The best studied area of animal domestication is the Old World, and especially the Near East where the first domesticated animals were sheep, goats, cattle and pigs. Since the Neolithic, animals, mostly domestic, have been kept in a variety of gathering enclosures, either natural shelters or built ones with differing degrees of roofing, i.e., roofed, semiroofed and unroofed structures. A common phenomenon in all types of animal gathering enclosures is the accumulation of dung deposits within these structures.

Experimental, ethnoarchaeological and archaeological studies of stabling dung deposits are the focus of this chapter. The majority of studies of this specific deposit centre in the Near East and Western Europe, with a focus on herbivore dung of cattle, sheep and goats. Studies of stabling deposits from horse, pig and other farm animals (e.g., chicken) are rare. Studies of stabling dung deposits of domestic herbivorous herd animals from other regions of the world (e.g., llama and alpaca in South America; camel in Arabia; yak in Central Asia; reindeer in northern Europe) are rare as well. Stabling deposits generally form from large amounts of dung, which may be mixed with other materials related to the maintenance of (mostly roofed) gathering enclosures (e.g., straw bedding, lime spread). The following chapter focuses on the micromorphological studies so far conducted on herbivore stabling deposits of sheep, goats and cattle in Old World contexts, paying special attention to the dung component of these deposits. The guidelines given below are general and thus may be applicable to the study of

dung-dominated stabiling deposits of other herd animals across the globe.

Herbivore dung derived from domestic sheep, goats, and cattle has been identified at a number of sites in the Old World. The types of dung deposits can be roughly divided into organic rich and organic poor, which reflect the state of dung preservation (Shahack-Gross 2011; Bronnimann et al. 2017, this book). In general, organic-rich dung deposits are found in archaeological sites that are either waterlogged or ultra-arid. These are usually rare, thus most archaeological remains of domestic herbivore dung are found after their organic matter has degraded or burnt, in the form of organic-poor dung deposits.

One key observation that made possible the identification of dung in archaeological sites was by Brochier (1983) who described the so-called 'dung spherulites', mineral spheres that form within the intestinal system of certain herbivores (see more in Canti & Brochier 2017, this book). Brochier stressed that an association between these microscopic indicators for dung and phytoliths (opal as well as calcium-oxalate; see more in Vrydaghs et al. 2017, this book), the latter material indicative of a plant-based diet, allows identification of dung of domestic animals. The next published studies employed micromorphology in archaeological contexts (Wattez et al. 1990; Courty et al. 1991), identifying dung on the basis of spherulites, phytoliths and microlaminated structure. It became clear that in order to identify and understand dung deposits in archaeological sites it is important to consider the formation processes (taphonomy) of dung deposits, from animal nutrition through defecation, trampling and degradation. Brochier et al. (1992) were the first to conduct such a systematic study of dung, in its cultural context, through an ethnoarchaeological study of dung deposits in open-air and naturally sheltered modern herding sites in Sicily. Although they did not use

the technique of micromorphology (rather they utilized petrographic analysis of minerals in grain mounts), their study made an important contribution (the identification of phosphate minerals associated with degraded dung deposits) and set the stage for more research. During the 1990s, a surge of studies into archaeological dung took place (e.g., Matthews et al. 1996; Macphail et al. 1997; Boschian & Montagnari-Koklej 2000), as well as work on the systematics of dung spherulites (Canti 1997, 1998, 1999) and opal phytoliths (e.g., Piperno 1988). Research published during the 2000s seems to have focused more on large-scale systematic studies of formation processes of dung deposits, taking into account their cultural context and utilizing micromorphology as one

technique among several (Shahack-Gross et al. 2003; Macphail et al. 2004; Shahack-Gross & Finkelstein 2008; Milek 2012). To date, the study of archaeological dung deposits encompasses a large variety of techniques and allows researchers to deduce important and significant archaeological interpretations regarding pastoralism, agro-pastoralism, urbanization processes, site spatial arrangement, seasonality, herding practices, the secondary products revolution and livestock domestication (see more in Shahack-Gross 2011).

The information presented below builds quite extensively on modern and submodern experimental and ethnoarchaeological case studies of organic-rich dung deposits, which illustrate how dung deposits form.

The chapter then focuses on extensive archaeological examples that illustrate the outcome of organic matter degradation and formation of organic-poor dung deposits.

# 29.2 Organic-Rich Dung Deposits

Dung is primarily an organic material. It is composed of undigested and partially digested food remains, dead intestinal epithelial cells and intestinal bacteria and parasites. The type of fodder and type of digestion affect the form and composition of dung. There is a difference, for example, in the size of fibres excreted by ruminants (foregut fermentation, e.g., sheep, goats, and cattle) and nonruminants (hindgut fermentation, e.g., horses, camels and donkeys) fed on the same diet, the former producing smaller sized fibres than the latter (see also Bronnimann et al. 2017, this book).

Yet, once deposited, herbivore dung across the globe is composed primarily of organic vegetal matter associated with high quantities of mostly biogenic materials, including opaline phytoliths (see also Vrydaghs et al. 2017, this book), calcium oxalate phytoliths and calcareous dung spherulites (see also Canti & Brochier 2017, this book) (Figure 29.1). The relative proportions of these three materials changes according to the type of herbivore diet: animals feeding primarily on monocotyledonous plants (i.e., grass) will produce dung that has a very high proportion of opal relative to calcium oxalate phytoliths, whereas animals feeding primarily on dicotyledonous plants (i.e.,

herbs and woody shrubs) will produce dung that has a higher proportion of calcium oxalate relative to opal phytoliths. This difference is especially pronounced across climatic areas where animals are free ranging; in humid climate regimes their diet will be dominated by grasses whereas in arid areas it will be dominated by shrubs (Shahack-Gross 2011, and references therein). The relative abundance of dung spherulites varies between animal species and within the same species (Canti 1997), a phenomenon that is not yet understood and may be related to the mineral intake in the animals' diet (Canti 1999).

Fresh dung has a variety of shapes that appear to be species specific (pellets, patties; see Bronnimann et al. 2017, this book) but may be affected by the amount of water in the fresh excrement. Sheep and goat dung is relatively less moist than cattle dung, yet any type of excrement may be saturated by additional fluids such as urine in roofed enclosures and / or rain in unroofed enclosures. Moist conditions are generally unhealthy for corralled hoofed animals, so maintenance activities such as replacement of flooring/beding and liming are conducted by herd owners to keep roofed enclosures as dry as possible (e.g., Milek 2012). Once deposited within an enclosure, the fresh organic dung becomes trampled by the animals. Trampling produces a laminar structure in cases where the animals have been fed on grasses. In these contexts, elongated grass fibres reorient perpendicular to the force of trampling (i.e., a vertical force), resulting in fibres being oriented subhorizontally (i.e., parallel to the surface; Figure 29.2; see also Figure 4a in Macphail et al. 2004 and

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/a55a84be09ffebf977a076dd3f38269d18d4ae58e65f127c57ae728a5a18df64.jpg)  
(a)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/e7b10a064b0453746ce3d4e9a01c8d991aff801b7ea7f623df79ce7477c3bd4a.jpg)  
(b)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/efe653788bc7ba7ce068d5442c1059237bc65f51036be5ba71f67d860e345cd9.jpg)  
Figure 29.2 Macroscopic laminar structure in modern dung deposits in a cattle enclosure in a Maasai settlement, Kenya. (a) Scan of thin section. (b) Close up; composition is dominated by organic matter but includes mineral fragments. PPL. (c) Close up; note two subparallel grass fibres. Such orientation contributes to the formation of the laminar structure. PPL.  
(c)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/5bfb1868da1031b38c31c642de0efc8e0ab927b525b02a1e01617aaefd3f85a2.jpg)  
Figure 29.3 Premodern (Bedouin) trampled sheep / goat dung containing an intact faecal pellet. Negev Highlands open-air camp, Israel. Transmitted light scan.

Figures 9a, b and 10a in Milek 2012). Where sheep and goats have been penned, faecal pellets may be preserved intact despite trampling (Figure 29.3). Macphail et al. (2004) noted that, whereas extensive trampling in central, high-traffic areas of stables results in absence of preserved whole pellets, better chances of whole pellet preservation may be found close to walls. It has also been observed that planar voids in clay-rich enclosure substrate (or stable floor), oriented subparallel to the surface, develop in the reactive zone – the sediment zone that undergoes structural change as a reaction to trampling (Gé et al. 1993). This microlaminated structure occurs as a result of compaction due to the weight load of heavy animals (Figure 29.4). Under certain conditions, organic-rich dung deposits may undergo spontaneous ignition, also known as smouldering (slow, low-temperature surface combustion of condensed fuel materials producing little smoke and no flames), whereby dung deposits are burnt in situ (Figure 29.5).

Organic-rich dung deposits degrade primarily through bacterial activity under oxidizing conditions. In abandoned thick deposits oxidation starts at the surface and moves downwards as an oxidation front (Figure 29.6). As the organic-rich portions of dung in an enclosure start

(a)  
Figure 29.4 Planar voids developed as a result of compression of soil substrate beneath a cattle enclosure (Kenya). (a) Transmitted light scan. (b) Detail of central part with abundant subparallel planar voids. Note the lensoid shape of compacted soil aggregates. PPL in greyscale.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/42737dce94699152f36dd1bf50a4ff135e196ed04e303edb6491deb1e144a7db.jpg)  
(b)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/f847eb30e67b754fd1279acc6f8391a0bf29c92b66f634add506b5187a54063d.jpg)  
(a)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/3ad14ed07133e50a2d66fa137a7770e21cc1d5e8337d4908c238676892a22754.jpg)  
(b)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/d7bffc2ac15d9b23bb376e8aef18d7af031aab988658473b338da5c58018526f.jpg)

(b) Close up of the ashed portion, showing elongated charred plant fibre with dendritic phytoliths at its lower edge along with a mostly calcitic groundmass dotted with rounded dark-coloured spheres (carbonized dung spherulites). PPL. (c) Same as (b), XPL.  
(c)

to degrade, two fabric units form, recognized as macroscopic mottles of (i) dark-coloured (organic-rich) sediment and (ii) light-coloured (organic-poor) sediment (Figure 29.7). Bacterial and fungal degradation of organic matter releases acids and certain elements into the stabilizing deposit solution. Hydromorphic conditions may develop where enclosure sediments are saturated by liquids (urine, water) under conditions of poor drainage. Pedofeatures, mostly in the form of authigenic mineral formations, may thus appear in early stages of dung degradation. The most ubiquitous compound released during decomposition is phosphate  $(\mathrm{PO}_4)$ . This may

react with various elements in the microenvironment of degrading dung deposits that, depending on chemical conditions, will form authigenic phosphate minerals (Karkanas & Goldberg 2010). Calcium phosphates (e.g., carbonated hydroxyapatite) are prevalent in neutral to alkaline pH and under aerobic conditions while iron-containing minerals (e.g., vivianite, an iron phosphate; and siderite, an iron carbonate) may form under anaerobic conditions (Shahack-Gross et al. 2003; Macphail et al. 2004; Milek 2012). These authigenic phosphate minerals precipitate within the organic mass, possibly replacing organic tissues as pseudomorphs (Figure 29.8; see also

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/15509b97fd3978df61ae4aab40fd4f1d17344e81acc1fdc7c7f90024474816a8.jpg)  
Figure 29.6 Oxidation occurring from the surface downwards, in early stages of in situ degradation. Field photograph. (a) Fully oxidized dung deposits. (b) Partially oxidized dung deposits forming a mottled appearance. (c) Fully organic, nonoxidized dung deposits. (d) Local soil on which enclosure has been built (Kenya). The thin blue lines indicate the soil-dung contact.

Figure 1c in Macphail et al. 2004). In fully degraded deposits phosphate mineral nodules and crusts may form (Figure 29.9; see also Figures 9, 12, 13, and 16 in Karkanas & Goldberg 2010). Nodules, coatings and hypocoatings composed of a variety of other authigenic minerals (e.g., calcite, goethite and other iron oxyhydroxides, or clay minerals) may form as well, notably along channels, desiccation cracks and/or horizontal planes (Figure 29.10; see also Figures 10c and 10d in Milek 2012), the latter possibly being products of trampling. The localized release of acids during organic matter degradation may cause dissolution of calcareous materials (e.g., dung spherulites, wood ash, lime, limestone / chalk fragments) that may reprecipitate and infill voids (see Figure 10e in Milek 2012). Certain salts have also been reported to form within degrading organic-rich dung deposits, such as gypsum and sylvite (Brochier et al. 1992; for gypsum see Figure 9e in Milek 2012). These have low potential for preservation due to their high solubility (except for possibly in arid environments).

# 29.3 Organic-Poor Dung Deposits

Organic-poor dung deposits may form in two main ways: first, by natural degradation of organic matter, and second, through burning (whether accidental or intentional). As stated above, dung is mostly composed of organic matter; however, it includes a small amount of inorganic materials. These form the bulk of organic-poor dung deposits, after most or all of the organic components have degraded. The inorganic components in herbivore livestock dung originate from the ingested fodder, ingested dust and biologically formed minerals within the animals' intestinal system (i.e., dung spherulites).

Inorganic components within the ingested fodder are mainly phytoliths. Opal phytoliths are ubiquitous in grasses and sedges but are also found in the leaves, wood and bark of dicotyledonous plants. The potential for preservation of opal phytoliths is high, and certain distinctive opal phytolith shapes can be used to infer the type of fodder eaten by the animals. Another type of phytolith found mostly in dicotyledonous plants is composed of calcium oxalate (both mono- and dihydrate; i.e., the minerals whewellite and weddellite, respectively). The potential for preservation of this type of phytolith is low as calcium oxalate is palatable to bacteria (Garvie 2003). However, during combustion, calcium oxalates transform in solid state to calcite, forming pseudomorphs of the original phytolith (see details of this transformation in Shahack-Gross & Ayalon 2013 and references therein, and in Canti & Brochier 2017, this book). This microscopic material has little or no taxonomic value (Franceschi & Horner 1980; Prychid & Rudall 1999) but is informative to the micromorphologist about formation processes and the general type of animal diet (i.e., a dicotyledonous-containing diet).

Other materials ingested by feeding herbivores are dust particles (silt and clay) that attach to plant material such as leaves, or are ingested with drinking water. Mineralized algae, such as diatoms, may be ingested with drinking water as well (Brochier et al. 1992). The latter are usually not found in large quantities. Mineral soil components may be brought into enclosures on the hooves of the animals and become incorporated into dung deposits through trampling. Human maintenance such as scooping out of stabling deposits and / or reflooding can also introduce external soil material that will be incorporated into newly forming stabling deposits.

Lastly, dung spherulites, which are formed within the animals' intestinal tract, are also excreted with dung. These are composed of calcium carbonate minerals, probably in the form of monohydrocalcite  $\mathrm{(CaCO_3H_2O)}$  Shahack-Gross et al. 2003). Spherulites are relatively unstable and may dissolve in neutral to slightly acid conditions (Brochier et al. 1992; Canti 1999; see also

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/8b594524e798ba494f4cca4c6ba3fba07a1aadac2d036a11cde27667783c0889.jpg)  
(a)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/818cd9069b8e4d89d6809c3803f9b1264465a207d0a58801715554e84d3ba09a.jpg)  
(b)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/8251c83478c498c7cb2017dcac701c1308db8013e86d179caea8ad0e23edfcdc.jpg)  
Figure 29.7 Early stages of in situ degradation. (a) Mottles of organic poor dung deposits (yellowish light areas) floating in a mass of organic-rich dung deposits (orange-brown areas). Note the faint preservation of macroscopic lamination (Kenya). Transmitted light scan. (b) Close up; note that degraded (light-coloured) areas appear to follow voids of various types (e.g., channels, vughs) where oxygen penetrates and thus localized degradation is accelerated. PPL. (c) Same as (b), XPL. Note that the light-coloured degraded areas are dominated by calcite whereas the dark-coloured areas are still dominated by organic matter.  
(c)

Gur-Arieh et al. 2014 for a quantitative measurement of spherulite dissolution in dung ashes). Moreover, because degradation of organic matter involves release of acids, dung spherulites may dissolve while the dung is being degraded. One means for their preservation is burning (see more in Canti & Brochier 2017, this book) – this process not only eliminates organic matter and thus bacterial activity but also probably transforms the monohydrocalcite into a more stable mineral such as calcite. Another means for spherulite preservation is being buried under relatively dry conditions.

Overall, with organic matter oxidation (through either bacterial degradation or burning) and formation of organic-poor dung deposits, the concentration of the inorganic components rises (Figure 29.11). The end product is almost totally mineral and, depending on its mode of formation, is expected to include different inorganic components and exhibit different microstructures:

1) Dung deposits formed from a monocotyledonous-dominated diet, by natural degradation and without burning, will result in formation of phytolith-rich layers. These deposits are common in pastoral and agro-pastoral archaeological sites (e.g., Matthews et al. 1996; Macphail et al. 1997; Matthews 2005; Shahack-Gross et al. 2005; Albert et al. 2008). The subparallel arrangement of phytoliths along originally compacted (trampled) plant fibres is generally maintained (including localized bioturbated areas), forming a characteristic microlaminated structure (Figures 29.12a and c). Calcium oxalate crystals, which may have been present in small amounts in the ingested vegetation, are unexpected due to bacterial degradation. The concentration of dung spherulites will differ according to environmental conditions: in humid environments it will be low due to dissolution (Figures 29.12b) and

in dry environments it will be high (Figure 29.12d). Phosphate nodules and crusts may be present and gypsum nodules may form and preserve in arid environments (see below).

2) Dung deposits formed from a monocotyledonous-dominated diet, by burning at temperatures below  $650^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , will be phytolith- and dung spherulite-rich (see Canti & Brochier 2017, this book). It will also include variable amounts of charred plant remains, heated soil aggregates, as well as small amounts of pseudomorphs of calcite after biogenic calcium oxalate phytoliths (Figure 29.13). If burnt in situ, for example from the practice of intentional burning of corrals to eliminate pests and parasites (Brochier et al. 1992), or if chunks of compacted enclosure deposits have been scooped out and used as fuel in cooking/heating installations (e.g., Gur-Arieh et al. 2014), a microlaminated structure may still be observed in the burnt dung deposits (Figure 29.13).  
3) Dung deposits formed from a dicotyledonous-dominated diet, by natural degradation without burning, will

include low amounts of opal phytoliths (unless tree leaves have been ingested). Calcium oxalate crystals are not expected due to bacterial degradation. Dung spherulite concentrations will vary depending on the environment, being better preserved in arid environments (Figure 29.14). Due to the small amounts of elongated grass opal phytoliths, a massive microstructure is typical, unless organic fibres are preserved, which typically occurs in arid environments (Figure 29.14).  
4) Dung deposits formed from a dicotyledonous-dominated diet, with burning, will be poor in opal phytoliths, and rich in both dung spherulites and in calcitic pseudomorphs after calcium oxalate phytoliths (Figure 29.15). Charred vegetal matter and heated soil aggregates are expected as well. An overall massive microstructure is typical, due to scarcity of elongated grass phytoliths (Figure 29.15).

The above pathways of formation of organic-poor dung deposits can be differentiated primarily based on

microstructure. Pathways 1 and 2 are characterized by an undulating microlaminated structure. This microstructure is a 'ghost' of the laminar structure in organic-rich dung deposits. While the laminae in organic-rich dung deposits are composed primarily of compressed, trampled organic fibres, the microlaminae in organic-poor dung deposits are composed of anatomically

ordered phytoliths left after the degradation of the compressed, trampled organic fibres (see also figure 6c-d, in Shahack-Gross et al. 2005). Pathways 3 and 4 are characterized by a generally massive microstructure (Figure 29.14 and 29.15).

Pedofeatures in organic-poor dung deposits may either form synchronously with the degradation of the organic

matter or afterwards. The former are typically phosphatic in composition and the latter may be composed of other minerals that precipitate from the sediment solution, depending on the local sediment chemistry. Figure 29.16 presents two examples, one of post-degradation gypsum nodules that formed in a site located in a hot and dry environment during most of the year, and the other of a phosphate crust that formed at the same time as organic-matter degradation in a site located in a humid Mediterranean environment.

Bioturbation, during organic-matter degradation or afterwards, may at least partially destroy the typical undulating microlaminated microstructure of phytolith-rich trampled dung. Earthworm granules may or may not

be present, depending on the geographical environment (note that clearly defined earthworm granules have thus far not been found in Mediterranean soils in the Near East).

# 29.4 Conclusion

Dung deposits in animal gathering enclosures can be recognized using micromorphology by identifying typical compositions and structures. The accurate identification of ancient dung deposits is invaluable to archaeology as it allows one to make further inferences about human subsistence, daily activities, herding practices and more (Shahack-Gross 2011).

# 30

# Trampling, Poaching and the Effect of Traffic

Philippe Rentzel<sup>1</sup>, Cristiano Nicosia<sup>2</sup>, Anne Gebhardt<sup>3</sup>, David Bronnimann<sup>1</sup>, Christine Pümpin<sup>1</sup> and Kristin Ismail-Meyer<sup>1</sup>

<sup>1</sup> IPAS – Integrative Prehistory and Archaeological Science, University of Basel, Switzerland  
<sup>2</sup> Centre de Recherches en Archéologie et Patrimoine, Université Libre de Bruxelles, Belgium  
<sup>3</sup> UMR 7362, LIVE, Équipe Dynamique des Paysages, Université de Strasbourg, INRAP, France

# 30.1 Introduction

Micromorphologists are often interested in identifying surfaces intercalated within stratified sequences, and in analysing the activities that took place on them. Humans and animals walking on a surface bring about modifications that can be seen under the microscope and that can be broadly defined as 'trampling' (see Miller 2017). Trampling can be also a depositional formation process where sediment is deposited, particularly in doorways of structures in temperate zones, leading to the accumulation of 'trample' (Banerjea 2015a, b; see also Matthews 1995; Matthews & French 2005). This depositional outcome of the trampling process is discussed in sections 30.2.3 and 30.5. Trampling in thin section is most often inferred on the base of the postdepositional alterations that it causes (see Table 30.1). In this chapter, we will focus more on this aspect of the process, by differentiating:

- Trampling stricto sensu, i.e. taking place on dry or prevalently dry conditions, as for example in roofed spaces, on both constructed or nonconstructed floors.  
- Poaching (occasionally referred to as 'puddling' - see for example Gebhardt & Langohr 1999, p. 607), which indicates trampling on very wet, preferentially water-saturated sediments.  
- Traffic, i.e. the effect of the passage of wheels (e.g., of carts).

The present chapter aims at illustrating the micromorphological features deriving from trampling, poaching and traffic through a series of examples from archaeological contexts. A last section will be devoted to experimental situations.

# 30.2 Trampling

An analysis of the conspicuous body of micromorphological literature mentioning trampling (see Table 30.1) allowed us to identify three main classes of modifications attributed to this process:

- Microstructural modifications. These include compaction, giving rise to massive microstructures, and the horizontal alignment of planar voids, resulting in a platy or laminar microstructure.  
- Development of laminations, bedding or layering (also defined as 'fine layering' or 'microlayering'). The terms are used interchangeably but basically indicate: (i) the strong horizontal orientation and close packing of plant fragments (including grass, peat or turf), phytoliths (as in mats), dung, charcoal and inorganic components; or (ii) alternating sequences of fine layers with different compositions, such as for example turf layers interdigitated with layers of charcoal and ash (Milek 2012 - see Table 30.1), or superimposed thin layers of ash.  
- Effects on coarser components such as charcoal, bone, chipped stone, ceramic material, shell, eggshell, etc. Such effects include: (i) integration or embedding in the groundmass; (ii) snapping or crushing of bone material and shell fragments, formation of microcharcoal; (iii) horizontal orientation and parallel distribution.

# 30.2.1 Trampling on Nonconstructed Floors

The group of nonconstructed floors includes trampled minerogenic deposits. A typical feature of such hardened surfaces consisting of local material is that they are generally no more than a few millimetres thick and are

usually only faintly visible in the field (Bresson & Zambaux 1990; Miskovsky 2002, p. 545; Goldberg & Macphail 2006, p. 246; Rentzel 2009, p. 575; Ismail-Meyer et al. 2013, p. 327). In the case of the royal tomb of Qatna, which was cut out of Tertiary limestone (Pfälzner 2011), traces of digging were preserved on top of the bedrock by a thin layer of sand and fine gravels originating from the soft lime marl (Figure 30.1). Due to mechanical abrasion by trampling (Banerjea et al. 2015a, p. 105), parts of the marl were detached from the bedrock resulting in a continuous  $0.5\mathrm{mm}$  thick planar void. The weakly stratified material showed a porosity of  $5 - 10\%$  and had a granular structure in some parts and a crack structure in others. This layer was overlain by a compacted accumulation of fine lime marl sand and soft sediments characterized by elongated lenses of clay and silt, which had been worked into the floor by trampling. Rare anthropogenic debris such as charcoal and bone splinters was embedded parallel to the basal layer. The deposit showed a massive structure with mainly planar voids (porosity  $5 - 10\%$ ). All the encountered features gave the sediment a microlaminated aspect. Similar observations, albeit with more distinct

compaction features, were made in the access areas of Mycenaean tombs in Greece (Karkanas 2012, p. 2729).

A surface compacted by human activity on sandy loam was found preserved in a roofed zone of activity in a depression in front of an Iron Age ceramic kiln in Basel (Figure 30.2). The topmost trampled zone was only some  $3\mathrm{mm}$  thick and very compact  $(< 2\%)$  porosity). Unlike the unmodified lower areas, the clay and silt fraction was evenly distributed and impregnated by iron oxides. The lower boundary of this topmost compacted area was diffuse whilst the surface was sharply levelled and covered by a  $0.2\mathrm{mm}$  thick and very compact layer of ash (Figure 30.3). Microcharcoals that lied on top of this surface were horizontally aligned.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/0b2c3e1f89fc857d3834f38c1b4cdd6dd74ac84707e98cbf09ff7f94a9de22be.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/ce56f2466bff61a14bb5ec4446bdb78bac204b852891761592d4134020a8d1b2.jpg)  
Figure 30.4 (a) and (b) Occupation layers, with ceramics. (c) Trampled occupation layer (massive microstructure, fine bedding, horizontally oriented coarser components). (d) Loam floor, sandy loam textured (massive microstructure), with basal bark layer. (e) Occupation deposit. Neolithic house, Lobsigesee, Bern (Switzerland). Thin-section scan.

# 30.2.2 Trampling on Constructed Floors

Prepared loam floors of allochthonous material are constructed features with an initial activity surface on top of the trampled floor (Courty et al. 1989, p. 124). Besides loam floors, this group also includes mortar floors, lime and gypsum plaster, peat or turf floors, 'brickearth' floors, and so forth, which will not be dealt with in detail here (see Adam 2011; Goldberg & Macphail 2006; Milek 2012; Matthews 2012; Huisman & Milek 2017, this book). The use of a loam floor typically results in a certain degree of alteration of the floor surface due to human activity. Consequently, the initial activity surface is covered gradually by reworked floor material on one hand and accumulated archaeological components, including organic and minerogenic soil material, on the other (Matthews 1995, 2003; Macphail et al. 2004; Milek 2012; Shillito & Ryan 2013; Banerjea et al. 2015a, b).

The constructed floors discovered at Bern-Lobsigesee (Figure 30.4) and in the Mithraeum of Biesheim

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/688ab3bdecb840c498470620561d148e74e05a831eb77e45c534af4718733f2d.jpg)  
Figure 30.5 (a) Loam floor, silty sand textured (platy microstructure, locally massive, horizontally oriented lenses of elongated peds). (b) Soot layer (temple activities). (c) Loam floor, silty sand textured (same characteristics as (a)). (d) Mortar layer (renovation). (e) Loam floor, silty sand textured (same characteristics as (a)). Roman Mithraeum of Biesheim (France). Thin-section scan.

(Figure 30.5), in Switzerland and France respectively, consisted of several centimetres of horizontal deposits of fine sediment with sharp upper and lower boundaries. The constructed floors exhibited characteristic traits such as a massive microstructure and low porosity. Loam fragments, which in the case of the floor uncovered at Bern-Lobsigesee originated from a Luvisol, showed a granostriated b-fabric.

The constructed floors encountered at Biesheim consisted of fine grained, carbonate rich floodplain deposits (Fortune 2011). The floors were characterized by elongated lenses of silty sand. They also showed a massive microstructure and subhorizontal cracks separating the clods. The topmost parts of the floors were strongly compacted and overlain by trampled-in remains from activities associated with the renovation or use of the temple. The latter was attested to by the presence of very thin layers of carbon black (illumination), microcharcoals, rare burnt bones and sand (Figure 30.6). A compact layer of ash below a loam floor at Zug-Unteraltstadt (Switzerland) might have been added prior to the construction of the floor (e.g., preparation and drainage of the surface – Milek 2012; Ismail-Meyer 2012). (Figure 30.7).

# 30.2.3 Trampled Occupation Deposits

An accumulation of various mineral and organic components takes place, particularly in sheltered areas, during a phase of occupation (Matthews et al. 1997). Courty and Fedoroff (2002) have termed this a continuous succession

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/746a1507fe113a555d4036f433c59bff4399e949228844471144753bf3abf70d.jpg)  
Figure 30.6 Detail of Figure 30.5, soot layer (b). In the central part trampled artefacts and microcharcoal resulting from temple activities appear on top of the dense loam floor. PPL.

of activity surfaces. The experimental observations made by Banerjea et al. (2015a), who characterized the accumulated sediment, termed 'compacted trample', in more detail (see Table 30.1), are interesting in this context. Analogous microscopic observations were made on the several centimetre-thick occupation layers unearthed in Zurich, Zug and Bern, Switzerland (Figures 30.4, 30.7 and 30.8 respectively). Such compact accretion layers generally consist mainly of (micro-)charcoal, organic fine material, ash and minerogenous components such as sand and gravel whereby the nature of the archaeological and minerogenic components depends, of course, on the respective activities. They contain areas with horizontal lenses of loam clods and show a pronounced microlamination, planar and/or polyconcave voids, a strong fragmentation of the components and a general tendency towards a horizontal orientation of the components. Moreover, sorting and a bimodal texture can also occur in places, as can a platy or sometimes blocky structure.

# 30.3 Poaching

At the macroscopic scale, convolutions or involutions are often associated with poaching (Figure 30.9). Such structures translate in thin section in undulating or wavy thin layers with distinct sorting of the coarse and fine fractions (Gebhardt & Langohr 1999) or with the formation of finely laminated crusts (Courty et al. 1989). Dusty clay coatings and crusts are normally ubiquitous and indicate surface slaking (Courty et al. 1989). Gebhardt & Langohr (1999) mention the presence, in areas where animals were herded (small cattle), of FeP crusts and coatings under the wavy structure and of strong reduction indicated by vivianite and pyrite.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/9aa9b798ef00301bfdd1111a2fef11a57a6e2e6c677b932f76144363462d34d4.jpg)  
Figure 30.7 (a) Succession of activity surfaces: trample with ash, (micro)charcoal, bone and eggshell fragments (platy microstructure, planar and polyconcave voids, bedding, horizontal orientation of charcoal and elongated peds). (b) Same as (a) (massive and platy microstructure, planar and polyconcave voids, bedding, horizontal orientation of charcoal and elongated peds). (c) Succession of activity surfaces: trample with ash, (micro) charcoal, bone and burnt loam aggregates (compact grain microstructure, complex, planar and polyconcave voids, horizontally oriented coarser components). (d) Loam floor (massive microstructure, complex, planar and polyconcave voids, subhorizontally oriented coarser components). (e) Ash layer (massive microstructure, horizontally oriented coarser components). (f) Fe-P impregnated succession of activity surfaces rich in ash, (micro) charcoal and bones (massive microstructure, microlaminations, horizontally oriented coarser components). Medieval site of Zug-Unteraltstadt (Switzerland). Thin-section scan.

# 30.4 Traffic

Wheel ruts are mainly linear and parallel, sometimes crossing each other, and set within wide trackways (Gebhardt & Langohr 2015). Animal drawn carts can be very heavy, so ruts are usually several centimetres deep and show three main characteristics (Figure 30.10): (i) strong compression, able to lead to platy structure formation if cold conditions intervene (see Van Vliet-Lanoë 2010); (ii) common redoximorphic mottling in the compressed earth within the wheel ruts, caused by localized reduction due to impeded drainage - reduction can

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/3bc7bebda457fbd1e616406750fb5cbc441b8dc453f002f7c0728485293b609e.jpg)  
Figure 30.8 All units microlaminated and with coarser components horizontally oriented, except (c) and (g). (a) Trample of ashy loam with microcharcoal. (b) Trample of ashy loam with (micro)charcoal. (c) Trampled loam lenses (massive, partly subangular blocky microstructure). (d) Same as (b). (e) Trample of weathered daub and phytoliths (partly prismatic structure, several vesicular voids). (f) Same as (b) and (d). (g) Trampled loam lenses (massive, partly subangular blocky microstructure). (h) Trample of ashy loam with (micro-)charcoal (massive microstructure). Medieval roofed artisanal building, Munsterhof site, Zürich (Switzerland). Thin-section scan.

cause the mobilization of Fe and P and the formation of secondary features (Macphail et al. 2016); (iii) occasional anthropic artefacts within the trackways themselves (Paccolat et al. 2011).

These main micromorphological characteristics can help in differentiating wheel ruts from plough marks, as the latter are characterized by better aerated conditions, lack of redoxymorphic features and inclusions derived from manuring (see Deak et al. 2017, this book).

# 30.5 Experimentally Trampled Sediments and Surfaces

Experimental studies represent an approach frequently employed in geoarchaeology (e.g., Bell et al. 1996; Lawson et al. 2000; Goldberg & Macphail 2006, p. 247 - see Table 30.1). There have also been several experimental studies on the effects of trampling on artefact distributions (e.g., Stockton 1973; Courtin & Villa 1982; Nielsen 1991; Driscoll et al. 2016) and artefact morphology (e.g., Nicholson 1992; McBrearty et al. 1998; Lopinot & Ray 2007; Eren et al. 2010). However, such studies were carried out with geoarchaeological support only in recent years. This is different for ethnoarchaeological research

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/afb852203f0d0854be7804e0931f7fc0ef053434cb4bf3c4d9eaac5668a1ddf9.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/2a5b7126dd37ed15054c0225499d6d20c41ec58cf40541da3fb2ec1ebbfbe7f9.jpg)  
Figure 30.9 (a) Profile picture showing convolutions and involutions linked to animal poaching (Image courtesy: Y. Devos). (b) Thin-section scan, highlighting coarse sands (upper layer) protruding into fine grained organic sediments (lower layer). Note the undulating or wavy layering and the limit between the two layers. (c) Microscope view of an involution (area marked in red in Figure 30.9b). Medieval site of Petit Rue des Bouchers, Brussels (Belgium - see Devos et al. 2016). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/a51c43a74eb4184893187970615cadc75e1aa931b37202e252f8c1a47e4fc1e2.jpg)

on occupation surfaces, which have provided modern analogies for understanding archaeological site formation processes (e.g., Goldberg & Witbread 1993; Boivin 2000; Shahack-Gross et al. 2003; Milek 2012). Such ethnoarchaeological studies have enabled the development of models (Gé et al., 1993) and have refined interpretive approaches to archaeological contexts (Matthews et al. 1997, Cammas 1998; Matthews 2010). Pedological investigations relevant to the subject of trampled sediments and surfaces have also addressed processes (e.g., crust formation) on exposed surfaces (Jongerius 1983; Boiffin & Bresson 1987; Valentin & Bresson 1992; Kwaad & Mücher 1994; Cattle et al. 2004) and have described the effects of compaction and consolidation on soil microstructure and porosity (Kooistra 1987; Bresson & Zambeaux 1990; Andrews 2006).

The following section presents observations on the effects of experimental trampling on different substrates, taking into consideration various circumstances (duration, environment etc.), but without the negative effects of postsedimentary processes (for a review of which see Wood & Johnson 1978). The first part describes the

effects of trampling in three archaeological sites with greatly varying substrates; the second part presents experiments conducted under laboratory conditions.

# 30.5.1 Field Experiments

Basel Gasfabrik (Switzerland) A field test on an intensively trodden pathway within the excavation site of Basel Gasfabrik over a 6-week period resulted in an accumulation of 5 to  $8\mathrm{cm}$  of reworked soil material brought in by trampling (Rentzel & Narten 2000). Two experiments simulated the following processes: trampling and compaction of an initial surface, then accumulation and redistribution of sandy loam and charcoal-rich occupation deposits both in an area protected by a tent and in an area exposed to the weather (Figure 30.11).

In the roofed area, the traces of compaction reached down from the initial surface to a depth of  $10\mathrm{mm}$ , exhibiting a diffuse lower boundary. The trampled sediment showed a modified soil structure with distinctly decreased porosity, polyconcave pores, a diminution of the pore diameter and neoformation of some weakly developed

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/84ae58a1eb383387cc68714e15c94b89a2c39d80d7d81e05ec26fe6250108aa6.jpg)  
Figure 30.10 (a) Compacted dusty clay occurring in paddy context due to water stagnation within the ruts, with slightly sorted graded bedding, and small shrinkage cracks (D). Vissèche (France). PPL. (b) Involutions (I) due to compression in the muddy sediment caused by cattle pulling the carts. Vissèche (France). PPL. (c) Compaction features (T) and particle sorting. Mittelhausen (France). PPL. (d) Compaction features and particle sorting (T), with iron concretions and impregnations (I). Vissèche (France). PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/4de56c3cb2b149e7d6bed69c084001df1d8f5ad8693ed8166ae1ba734d567d1c.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/44cce13f3a8516ffcac0cbb587c38f8f83022624dc49cc6baaaebcda2ddbbfe4.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/cc7fdceddc6835eb3c18087e523408759c77665f55bb4fa77d5f6923bb5ebe4b.jpg)

interlaced silty intercalations. The initial surface, displaying a sharp horizontal boundary, was covered by microcharcoal that originated from the archaeological occupation deposit. The overlying  $80\mathrm{mm}$  thick trampled sediment was characterized by irregular horizontal bedding, horizontal cracks and horizontally oriented gravel components. Also typical are zones with massive microstructure and polyconcave pores. Most of the trampled soil peds exhibited a compressed, lenticular shape.

The second soil sample was taken from the unprotected area beyond the excavation tent (Figure 30.12). It displayed more pronounced compaction features, reaching  $20\mathrm{mm}$  deep into the sediment. In the top few millimetres of the initial surface there were horizontally aligned components and plastic deformation of the clay. Interlaced silty intercalations had also formed (Figure 30.13 and 30.14). During the field experiment a  $50\mathrm{mm}$  thick trample layer formed. The latter is characterized by massive microstructure, polyconcave pores and silty intercalations. In comparison to the roofed area, a more pronounced mixing was observed, locally resulting in complete homogenization and disruption of clay coatings. Horizontal cracks, horizontally bedded components

and abrupt sediment changes give the impression of a stratified and successively accumulated deposit.

Brig (Switzerland) The sampling site at Brig is located on the bottom of the inner alpine valley of the Valais, where an excavation team walked daily on a several meters wide path during the summer months. Three samples were taken at a gently sloping stretch with a loose growth of trees. The underlying C horizon consisted of a loose sandy silt with some gravel, while the trampled A horizon was slightly humic (grassland use), with channel and chamber microstructure and a porosity of  $20\%$ . The scan of section EXP7 shows the following succession from top to base (Figure 30.15): (a) Trampled loose dust layer with silty-sandy texture, poorly vegetated. (b) Trampled crust, developed on recently dumped sediments (c) and (d). (e) Buried trampled crust of a former surface.

The topmost layer, (a), which represented the recent surface, had been trampled during the summer. Human trampling had led to disaggregation of the topsoil and reworking of silt and sand. The underlying compacted layer,  $2\mathrm{mm}$  in thickness, (b) indicates the influence of

Figure 30.11 Polished block from a modern pathway in a roofed area. (a) Accumulated trample with horizontally bedded gravel and massive microstructure. Sharp lower boundary to layer (b) (initial compacted surface). (c) Natural soil. Excavation site, Basel Gasfabrik (Switzerland). Sample length:  $13\mathrm{cm}$ .

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/484edd234508f3bc33fc891d5a2ed21f4b7ad458f85c9952e08779c6fa7ece40.jpg)

water puddling, which led to crust formation and lamination (Figure 30.16). Trampling is suggested by the low porosity and the platy structure overprinting the fining upward sequence that had formed in the puddle, leading partially to the deformation of the crust. Mollusc shells and leafs were highly fragmented, whereas sand grains were horizontally aligned. An older surface (e) which was preserved under a modern dump, exhibited similar characteristics.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/539749f7959d2dcfae75d86c8f0b4f3b41961463df47b614d27edc06cfe9a8a9.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/702e9fa4a40d461f0a7acebb7e9e0f56e8e72f91885c49fc4c94953b66c0c12f.jpg)

Risch (Switzerland) During the excavation of the Neolithic lakeside settlement of Risch, situated on the border of Lake Zug, an exposed bare horizontal surface of lake marl (carbonate mud) was trampled by excavators over the course of four weeks (Figure 30.17). The original lake marl (c) consisted of a freshly exposed, moist sandy silt with a porosity of ca.  $15 - 20\%$ . It contained calcium encrusted chara stems and well preserved mollusc shells.

The initial surface was markedly changed by trampling and exhibited a slight relief with flat depressions of several cm in size, which were filled by puddles with

laminated sediments (fining upward cycles – Figure 30.18). The puddling seems to be a direct consequence of the compaction that also impeded the drainage. In the trampled lake marl layer above (b) compaction features such as low porosity and planar voids were visible to a depth of  $10\mathrm{mm}$ , and had a diffuse lower boundary with the underlying lake marl. Subsequent trampling during the excavation led to an accumulation of reworked and compacted material derived from archaeological layers. This ca.  $20\mathrm{mm}$  thick heterogeneous layer (a) was horizontally bedded and contained compressed peds of lake marl and morainic clay, mixed with sand grains, gravel and organic

remains. Note that the most recent puddles were well expressed in the topmost part of the sample.

# 30.5.2 Laboratory Experiments

In a series of laboratory experiments, Kubiena boxes  $(530\mathrm{cm}^3)$  were used to sample a weakly developed Luvisol from Basel Gasfabrik (Switzerland), and the soil within them was subsequently compacted with a stamp corresponding to a person of  $70\mathrm{kg}$  for periods of 0, 5 and 15 minutes, under both dry and moist conditions. Three samples were first oven-dried  $(40^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  during one week, then compacted (60 strikes/minute). Water  $(100\mathrm{cm}^3)$  was added to three other samples before trampling was simulated.

The control sample consisted of an unstratified, decalcified loamy sand, with channels and chambers (porosity:  $15 - 20\%$ ) that were up to  $3\mathrm{mm}$  in diameter (see Table 30.2 and Figure 30.19). The clay fraction was dispersed in the groundmass (cross-striated b-fabric) and formed clay coatings.

Compaction for  $5\mathrm{min}$  produced microstructural changes in the topmost  $3 - 5\mathrm{mm}$  of this dry soil horizon. In this zone, macropores had largely disappeared and porosity decreased by around  $5\%$ . The largest pores still measured  $0.5\mathrm{mm}$  (mean  $0.1 - 0.2\mathrm{mm}$ ) in diameter, with an increase in elongated or polyconcave voids (Figure 30.20a). There was also a tendency towards horizontal alignment of particles and slight fragmentation of the iron oxides. Compaction for  $15\mathrm{min}$  resulted in a further decline in porosity and the development of a granular microstructure at the worked dry surface. Microstructural changes were most distinct in the top  $3\mathrm{mm}$ , where concentrations of aligned sand particles and horizontal cracks dominated (Figure 30.20b). Traces of compaction were visible to a depth of  $9 - 12\mathrm{mm}$ , with a diffuse lower boundary. Within this area, increased mechanical stress resulted in the fragmentation of iron precipitations and destruction of clay coatings. The clay fraction was homogenously distributed and exhibited a cross-striated b-fabric.

Five-minute compaction of the moist soil led to deformations detectable in the topmost  $10\mathrm{mm}$ , with formation of polyconcave voids, destruction and integration of clay coatings into the groundmass. A massive microstructure and horizontal cracks dominated this soil (Figure 30.20c), and horizontal lenses of sand grains indicate diffuse deformation structures. Fifteen-minute compaction created strong deformation phenomena and a mostly massive microstructure, again with collapse and

redistribution of clay coatings. This advanced stage is expressed by the appearance of parallel surface crusts and the formation of microdepressions with uplifted marginal areas that had a tendency to migrate towards the centre of depression (Figure 30.20d).

# 30.6 Conclusion

A comparison between the key features of trampling and poaching that were observed in the archaeological and experimental contexts mentioned in this chapter, and the published literature (Table 30.1) allows us to consider as reliable the following micromorphological indicators:

- Microstructural modifications, such as the reduction in overall porosity, the formation of massive or platy microstructures and of polyconcave voids. These are generally more marked and extend further down in moist sediments in comparison to dry deposits. In contrast, trampling effects are limited to the topmost few millimetres of the trampled surface under dry conditions, accompanied by an increased fragmentation of less durable components, including soil peds. Overall, the laboratory tests made it evident that the effects of trampling events of limited duration – in the absence of accumulation of introduced material – are confined to the top 2–3cm below the original surface.

- Development of bedding (e.g., at Basel Gasfabrik in the experimentally trampled deposits). Successive accumulations of 'trample' (see Banerjea et al. 2015a, b), generally heterogeneous and containing compressed elongated peds, also result in bedding or layering of trampled deposits. In outdoor areas, depending on soil moisture and intensity of trampling, the accumulated trample can have a homogeneous aspect.  
- Horizontal orientation of coarser components, including sand grains, charcoal, bone and mollusc shells. Mechanical influence ('crushing' or 'snapping') on bones or molluscs and formation of microcharcoal.  
- Formation of crusts of silty clay or dusty clay with normal grading or microlaminations, and of convolutions as the result of poaching.

Traffic (section 30.4) brings about features related to strong compaction (microstructural changes, reduction) within the wheel marks, and to poaching and topsoil disturbance in the trackways.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/40dc43ae1a7230a34704996f4f12a125519a5fe4adbe83027710ba3602d763e8.jpg)

# 31

# Combustion Features

Carolina Mallo1,2, Susan M. Mentzer3,4,5 and Christopher E. Miller3,5

$^{1}$ Departamento de Geografia e Historia, Universidad de La Laguna, Campus de Guajara, Tenerife, Spain  
$^{2}$  Instituto Universitario de Bio-Organica Antonio González, La Laguna, Campus de Guajara, Tenerife, Spain  
<sup>3</sup> Institute for Archaeological Sciences, University of Tübingen, Tübingen, Germany  
$^{4}$  School of Anthropology, University of Arizona, Tucson, USA  
5 Senckenberg Centre for Human Evolution and Paleoenvironment, University of Tübingen, Tübingen, Germany

# 31.1 Introduction

Combustion features are sedimentary deposits or structures that contain physical remnants of fire. In archaeological sites, combustion features are typically – but not always – anthropogenic in origin, resulting from both intentional and unintentional burning. Their formation processes and states of preservation can vary considerably: they can be found as burnt residues in their original position or they can be significantly reworked by humans and other agents and altered by chemical diagenesis. Combustion features are also present in sites of all ages, with the oldest examples dating to ca. 1.0 million years before present (Berna et al. 2012). As evidenced by a considerable body of literature on the topic (summarized in Mentzer 2014), micromorphology is one of the strongest analytical techniques used in the study of combustion features.

In this chapter we will first provide definitions for many different types of combustion features and their identifying characteristics in the field (sections 31.2 and 31.3). Later sections contain guidelines for their micromorphological study organized by the steps of analysis: identifying burnt components (section 31.4), describing common fabrics and microstructures of intact (section 31.5) and reworked (section 31.6) combustion structures, identifying common diagenetic processes (section 31.7) and synthesizing micromorphological and other microstratigraphic data for the interpretation of formation processes (section 31.8). We will illustrate these concepts with examples from our own work on archaeological sites of a range of ages and geographic settings, as well as experimental contexts.

Understanding the formation histories behind combustion features is not just an issue of site taphonomy. In Lower and Middle Palaeolithic sites, it is important to understand the relationship between humans and burnt materials, as well as the degree of human control of fire (Roebroeks & Villa 2011). Combustion features are also evidence of different types of pyrotechnological knowledge and can aid archaeologists in reconstructing the level of time or energy investment in certain activities (Goren & Goring-Morris 2008). In addition, humans frequently contribute to the production of reworked combustion features, such as middens and ash dumps, with varying degrees of intentionality. The distinction between intact and reworked features – both single use and reused – and indications of function are helpful for reconstructing activity areas and understanding varying degrees of intensity of site occupation (Miller et al. 2013). Furthermore, the types of burnt materials that are present in secondary position can be useful for reconstructing activities that may not be evidenced otherwise. For example, rake-out deposits in middens can provide evidence for maintenance of both domestic and ritual spaces elsewhere in the site (Matthews 2005, Shillito & Matthews 2013), whereas in other contexts redistributed burnt materials can reflect their use as cleaning agents (Milek 2012). Finally, studies of combustion features also have implications beyond archaeology. Matthews (2010) highlights how the presence of different types of burnt plants in thin section can aid in reconstructing aspects of the local vegetation community. In addition, the application of micromorphology to intact combustion features in archaeological sites has proven useful for reconstructing changes in the geomagnetic field over time (Kapper et al. 2014).

Integration of micromorphological observations with other analytical techniques may be essential for the thorough study of combustion features. Several different types of analysis can be conducted directly on the thin sections or corresponding resin-impregnated sediment tiles: microdrilling of calcareous ashes for isotopic measurements (Mentzer & Quade 2013; also Mentzer 2017a, this book), organic petrology of polished tiles for the study of charred materials (Ligouis 2017, this book), micro-FTIR for the reconstruction of burning temperatures from bone inclusions and heated substrates (Berna 2017, this book), and micro-FTIR paired with micro-XRF (Mentzer 2017b, this book) for documentation of mineralogical changes and elemental gains and losses as a result of postdepositional chemical digenesis. In addition, researchers have been able to identify plants using articulated phytoliths visible in thin section (Wadley et al. 2011; Vrydaghs et al. 2017, this book).

# 31.2 Classification of Combustion Features

Combustion features can be intact, with their components in the primary position of burning, or reworked. Both intact and reworked features can range considerably in complexity, from fuel remnants atop a former ground surface to a constructed kiln or oven, and from material redeposited by wind or water to elaborate cremation burials. Micromorphology is key for the characterization of primary (intact) anthropogenic features, which can be organized by their degree of artificial containment or structuring, and their formation process and function. The two main categories of primary combustion features are uncontained features and contained features. Uncontained combustion features result from natural processes and both controlled and uncontrolled use of fire by people (see also Deak et al. 2017, this book). Contained combustion features result from controlled burning of some type. Containment may be provided by the lateral limitation of a fuel that is intentionally placed, or by construction of walls and other structural elements. Different types of uncontained and contained features are described in Table 31.1, with detailed micromorphological descriptions in section 31.5.

Each of the physical types of combustion features listed in Table 31.1 may be further classified by primary and secondary function, both of which may be suggested, but not defined by structure. For example, simple hearths, pit structures, prepared surfaces and ovens might all be used for cooking food; however, the mode of heat transfer from the fire to the food may vary. Likewise, both pit structures and kiln installations can be used to process

limestone into lime; however, neither type of feature provides light. Finally, open simple hearths, prepared surfaces, and installations can all function as fire altars, depending on their broader social context. Secondary functions of fires and combustion features are likewise varied.

# 31.3 Analytical Strategy

Sampling, identification, and interpretation of combustion features in archaeological sites, including their classification by physical properties and function, proceeds following several steps. In the field, one should describe the physical arrangement of burnt materials with respect to the substrate and any containment systems. Micromorphology samples should ideally span the entire vertical section of the feature, including the upper few cm of the substrate. Analysis of a combustion feature using micromorphology is accomplished by: (i) identifying the microscopic products of combustion, as well as other heated materials, inclusions and secondary minerals; (ii) describing the stratigraphy, fabric and overall structuring of the feature; (iii) interpreting the fabric and structure in terms of primary and secondary depositional processes; and (iv) synthesizing the results with the aim of interpreting the life history and function of the feature.

# 31.4 Common Microscopic Products of Combustion

The first step in the micromorphological investigation of a combustion feature is the identification of the burnt materials. Although a majority of burnt components in combustion features derive from fuel, some components may accidentally enter an active fire during use. Other components may be tossed into a fire as part of site maintenance behaviour (Clark & Ligouis 2010). Finally, components of an occupation surface may be unintentionally burned by an overlying fire made directly on the surface (Mallol et al. 2013a).

When organic material is burned, it first undergoes dehydration and then carbonization, producing a continuum of thermochemical degradation products including char, charcoal, soot, graphitic carbon and black carbon (Jones et al. 1997; Braadbaart & Poole 2008; Braadbaart et al. 2012). The thermochemical process leading to these burning products is termed pyrolysis, which takes place under reducing conditions. In contrast, combustion refers to burning in an oxidizing environment, leading to oxidation of organic compounds until only nonvolatile, mineral constituents remain, forming 'ash' (see Canti & Brochier 2017a, this book).

Table 31.1 Physical types of combustion feature.  

<table><tr><td>Combustion feature type</td><td>Description</td></tr><tr><td>Uncontained combustion features</td><td>Resulting from natural processes and both controlled and uncontrolled use of fire by people.</td></tr><tr><td>Natural fire deposits</td><td>Produced by uncontrolled burning related to forest and brush fires, lightning strikes of trees and spontaneous ignition of accumulations of dung and guano.</td></tr><tr><td>Destruction layers</td><td>Usually in urban sites; they are examples of laterally widespread and uncontained burning of buildings and other structures. These types of deposits result from accidental or intentional ignition by humans and variable degrees of control of the resulting conflagration.</td></tr><tr><td>Maintenance and cleaning fires</td><td>Fumier, burned bedding and closure deposits. These features are laterally extensive, but result from intentional ignition, and sometimes, ongoing control of the burning process.</td></tr><tr><td>Contained combustion features</td><td>Resulting from controlled burning of some type. Containment may be provided by the lateral limitation of a fuel that is intentionally placed, or by construction of walls and other structural elements.</td></tr><tr><td>Open hearths</td><td>Combustion features in which burning is contained by the lateral limitation of fuel placement, sometimes in association with perimeter lining or exploitation of an available wall. Simple open hearths are unlined with earthen or bedrock substrate. Shallow excavation may be the only form of substrate preparation or modification, either as a result of intentional formation of a depression, or evolution of a depression over time due to maintenance. Lined open hearths have perimeter stones.</td></tr><tr><td>Prepared burning surfaces</td><td>Features that exhibit flat or concave prepared anthropogenic substrates, with or without low perimeter linings. The positioning of prepared surfaces can exploit available walls, but these are not constructed intentionally for the purposes of containing the fire. There are different types including griddles, clay-lined hearths, plastered hearths, pebble-lined hearths, paved hearths, braziers, flat altars and platform hearths. During use of these features, the fuel is typically placed on top of the burning surface, but can be removed later in order to transfer heat from the prepared surface to another object (e.g., cooking).</td></tr><tr><td>Pit structures</td><td>Combustion features in which burning occurs underground, using the local substrate to contain the fire in three dimensions and control its oxygenation conditions. Different types of pit structures include roasting pits, charcoal pits, and some simple forms of kilns.</td></tr><tr><td>Fire installations</td><td>Constructed containers that range from partially subterranean to freestanding. Fire installations have prepared substrates, as well as walls and a roof or ceiling (either constructed or temporary, as with the addition of a cooking vessel) that contain the fire in three dimensions and reflect heat. Ventilation systems are also constructed in the substrate, walls, or roof. These systems can include vent covers or flues, and may function passively, as by wind or convection through an opening, or actively, as with bellows.</td></tr></table>

Both carbonized organic material and ash – and their diagenetic derivatives – form the bulk of constituents of archaeological combustion features. Carbonized materials and ash can take multiple forms depending on the original material (Table 31.2).

Burnt components can be organic or inorganic. Burnt organic components of plants include charcoal, charred plant material, peat, turf and coal (Figure 31.1); those derived from animals can include burnt flesh, fat, bone and hair. Inorganic components can include bone and shell (Figure 31.2). Other inorganic materials are typically classified as burnt mineral constituents. Mineral constituents are encountered in combustion features in several forms. Those of plants include calcareous and siliceous phytoliths, and calcium oxalate crystals, the latter present within charcoal fragments (see Figure 31.1a), or transformed by combustion and later recarbonation

to calcareous ash (Figure 31.3; see also Canti & Brochier 2017a, this book). Other burnt mineral constituents derive from the substrate, materials used to pave or line the feature, and inclusions (Figure 31.4). Unburnt mineral components, such as secondary phosphates, may also be present and are discussed in section 31.7.

# 31.5 Stratigraphy, Fabric and Classification of Intact Combustion Structures

The second step in the micromorphological analysis of combustion features is description of the relationship of the burnt components to each other. Aspects of description include internal stratigraphy, fabric and structure.

Table 31.2 Common burnt constituents of archaeological combustion features.  

<table><tr><td>Component</td><td>Description and identification</td><td>Figures</td><td>References</td></tr><tr><td colspan="4">Burnt organic components of plants</td></tr><tr><td>Char and charcoal</td><td>Char is black carbonaceous residue derived from solid organic material used as fuel. Some types of charred plant material reported in thin section include burnt mosses and lichens, vascular bundles, charred seeds and leaves. Fat-derived char does not exhibit cellular structure, but appears porous as a result of numerous vesicles of varying size and distribution with small fissures or cracks typically radiating outward from their walls. These characteristics and a &#x27;droplike&#x27; morphology of most fragments suggest that this material was a fluid, which has subsequently cooled.</td><td>Figure 31.2b</td><td>Goldberg et al.
