Table 31.2 (Continued)  

<table><tr><td>Component</td><td>Description and identification</td><td>Figures</td><td>References</td></tr><tr><td>Phytolith slag</td><td>When phytoliths melt at temperatures of ~850–1700°C, depending on the presence of alkali fluxes, phytolith slag forms. This material can be identified by conspicuous internal vesicles, white-grey colour in OIL, isotropy in XPL and negative relief.</td><td>Figure 31.3c</td><td>Folk &amp; Hoops (1982); Canti (2003); Macphail &amp; Crowther (2007); Zerboni (2011)</td></tr><tr><td>Siliceous aggregates</td><td>These materials are composed of quartz and other crystalline phases within a poorly ordered matrix containing silicon, aluminium, potassium and iron. Siliceous aggregates are present in fresh ashes, possibly produced by trees as defensive mechanisms and have also been reported as weathering products of ashes, phytoliths and clay minerals. They are small (&lt;30 μm) and difficult to identify when contained in a sedimentary matrix.</td><td></td><td>Schiegl et al. (2011)</td></tr><tr><td>Heat-altered and fire-cracked rocks</td><td>Heat-altered rocks and fire-cracked rocks can source from the substrate, from constructed portions of the feature, or when rocks are intentionally added to a fire, (e.g., pot boiling or lime production). Mineralogical changes and fissures may be visible.</td><td>Figure 31.3f</td><td>Gur-Arieh et al. (2012); Goren and Goring-Morris (2008: Figure 8); Mentzer et al. Some articulated ashes contain both calcareous pseudomorphs and carbonized organic material, implying that they preserve a phase between carbonization and complete transformation to ash. These can be referred to as partially carbonized or incompletely calcined tissues.</td><td>Figure 31.4</td><td>Wattez (1988: Figure 11); Courty et al. (1989: Figure 34, 35); Brochier &amp; Thinon (2003: Figure 1, 2); Canti, (2003); Mallol et al. (2007); Berna et al. (2012); Mentzer (2012); Shahack-Gross &amp; Ayalon (2012: Figure 2); Canti 2017b, this book; Canti &amp; Brochier 2017a, this book</td></tr><tr><td>Ash from alternative fuels</td><td>Peat ash contains calcareous pseudomorphs, as well as phytoliths and minerogenic components. Dung ash composition depends on the type of dung that was burnt. Calcareous spherulites and phytoliths make up the bulk of dung ash produced from animals that regularly produce spherulites in their digestive systems. The ash of dung can also preserve characteristic fabrics. Fly ash results from the burning of coal.</td><td>Figure 31.4e
Figure 31.4f</td><td>Brodaart et al. (2012); Angelucci 2017; this book; Shahack-Gross 2017, this book</td></tr></table>

Each of these characteristics can be used in the third step - the interpretation phase - to aid in classifying features by physical type and function, and to determine whether the feature and its components are intact or reworked.

# 31.5.1 Basic Stratigraphy

The most common stratigraphic expression of an intact combustion feature is a sedimentary sequence consisting of two or three horizontal layers (often more than one cm in thickness) bounded by sharp contacts (Figure 31.5;

Courty et al. 1989; Mallol et al. 2013a, 2013b; Shillito & Matthews 2013). The top layer, here referred to as the burnt residues, contains the products of combustion, as described in section 31.4. The underlying layer or layers comprise the combustion substrate, which is the heat-altered portion of the surface on which the fire was made (Figure 31.6).

While the original thickness of the burnt residue layer is correlated with the amount of fuel burnt, the thickness of the combustion substrate is related to its original composition, water content and porosity, which lead to different degrees of heat conduction and both water

Figure 31.5 Combustion feature stratigraphy in field and thin section. (a) In the field, combustion features are typically recognized by the presence of a combustion substrate (CS) overlain by one or more layers of burnt residues (BR). The CS may or may not exhibit visible evidence of heating, such as rubification or blackening. Multiple BR layers may source from differential combustion during a single burning event (as with layers of charcoal overlain by ash), or from multiple uses of the feature. Open hearth, ethnoarchaeological context. (b) An intact hearth in thin section. Here the CS, at  $8.7\mathrm{cm}$  in thickness, is composed of loose, sandy sediment that is dark in colour due to the presence of fine organic matter that became charred in place. The BR layer  $(0.9\mathrm{cm}$  thick) contains calcined tufa, burnt bone and charcoal in an ashy matrix. Middle Paleolithic (MP) open hearth, Spain. Incident light scans. (c) Multiple open hearths stacked one atop another are typically identified in the field by alternating layers of dark and light sediment that in thin section can be identified as complicated sequences of combustion substrates and burnt residues. MP open hearths, Spain.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/2d6315c99ced330472c73f6cb76e0b77aabe191bdbde8dadb5035a5e7b49575f.jpg)

vaporization and condensation. These factors determine the vertical extent of heat penetration and thermal alteration (Chandler et al. 1983; Canti & Linford 2000; Sievers & Wadley 2008). At the macroscopic scale, the combustion substrate may exhibit chromatic zonation (Figures 31.5b and 31.6a) or surface reddening (Figure 31.6c).

# 31.5.2 Composition and Fabric of the Burnt Residues

The main components of the top layer of a combustion feature are burnt fuel and inclusions, whose composition, porosity and microstructure are related to their sources. In samples of unweathered experimental open

hearths fuelled by wood, the fuel layer is composed of calcareous ashes (see Table 31.2). This layer is generally massive and may locally exhibit undulating, horizontal bedding, with the common presence of articulated ashes and partially carbonized tissues (see Figure 31.3c and d). Ash layers derived from silica-rich fuels may also contain isolated or articulated phytoliths (see Figure 31.4a and b; Cabanes et al. 2010: Figure 2c-d; Goldberg et al. 2009: Figure 5f; Shahack-Gross 2011: Figure 7c). Like articulated ashes, articulated phytoliths are fragile and can be used to identify features that have not been significantly impacted by postdepositional reworking. In other types of intact combustion features, the constituents of the top layer are commonly unsorted and arranged in an open porphyric c/f related distribution.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/360d1474a176b4ef008b014df5e3d66e4bda76ef9b2f84d06eb943f31d03ccfb.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/c7c22175048446004e9a99f27aae3412740c2db1eb199f0bc9613df2848a623f.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/ad54d288c152650786c2ceac755118641f2e5953644711e5ef96cb957c872312.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/85db37030f935a07418746aee8d4bc9acd09fece24d416e53bf1721b6394da06.jpg)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/6a9a388f11a4c5619ee3bc4f0646578b647d0c39c7fe4f29635a6288f40bfa43.jpg)  
Figure 31.6 Geogenic and biogenic substrates. (a) The combustion substrate (CS) and burnt residues (BR) produced in an experimental open hearth constructed on a dry, unvegetated, loose calcareous sediment with common inclusions of fine organic matter. The fire burnt for approximately 3 hours and yielded a heat-altered zone of  $1 - 2.3\mathrm{cm}$  in thickness that contains charred plant tissues. Incident light scan. (b) An experimental open fire constructed on top of a dry, partially cemented calcareous sediment without organic inclusions. Although, as in (a), the fire burnt for 3 hours, the altered portion of the substrate is only two mm in thickness. Incident light scan. (c) An open hearth constructed on a wet surface yielded an altered zone that is reddened and contains horizontal fissures immediately beneath the hearth, and charred fine organic material at depth. Incident light scan. (d) An experimental open hearth constructed on a sparsely vegetated soil yielded a CS with a well-developed intergrain microaggregate microstructure. PPL. (e) A hearth in a similar setting as (d) yielded a CS with a granular microstructure. PPL. (f) An experimental open hearth constructed on top of a vegetated surface yielded an altered geogenic substrate composed of heated sediment (CSb), overlain by the biogenic portion of the substrate containing grass char and moss char (CSa; see also Figure 31.1(c)), and burnt residues (charcoal and wood ashes) sourced from the fuel. In this example, the grass and moss char are components of the substrate, however this distinction would be nearly impossible to make in an archaeological setting, and it is likely that the different types of charred plants would be interpreted as different types of fuel. PPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/a2440abfe3064105d36f115e48ba4a4814bc80c62574a34eeecfa72f9f5a6433.jpg)

# 31.5.3 Fabric of the Combustion Substrate

The substrate of a combustion structure comprises the material surface on which fire is made. It is reminiscent of its nature (i.e., a soil, sediment or anthropogenic deposit), which in turn plays a significant role in heat penetration and thermal alteration, leading to many different possible microstructural features. The substrate also includes any kind of surface occupied or made by humans, from natural soils to house floors. Any organic components that the substrate may contain are thermally altered under a reducing atmosphere and therefore subjected to pyrolysis rather than combustion. This difference results in a high proportion of charred products, which contributes a generally dark brown or black colour to the sediment (see Figures 31.5a and b and Figure 31.6a; Mallol et al. 2013a). Combustion substrates with little or no organic content might exhibit different colours in accordance with thermally induced chemical reactions of their sedimentary components. For example, rubification results from enrichment in ferric oxide (hematite), which can be visible in thin section (see Figures 31.3f and 31.6c and Röpke & Dietl 2017, this book). Often in archeological contexts, all that is left of a combustion structure is its intact, eroded or postdepositionally transformed substrate. Table 31.3 describes a series of common combustion substrate types that can be encountered in archaeological contexts.

# 31.5.4 Interpretation and Classification of Intact Combustion Features

In the third step of combustion feature analysis, the micromorphologist integrates macro- and microscale observations to classify the features and deposits by type and, if possible, function (see Table 31.1). In archaeological sites, functions of primary combustion features may be approached by a variety of macro- and microscale attributes. In some cases, inclusions may be the only clues as to the function(s) of the feature (e.g., Goren & Goring-Morris 2008). Examples of inclusions related to function include burnt bone and fat-derived char in a sacrificial altar (Mentzer 2014: Figure 5), burnt blubber and fur in the cooking hearth of a sealing camp (Villagran et al. 2013: Figures 6, 7 and 8), burnt grain and seeds in domestic ovens (Matthews 2010: Figure 2), and metallurgical slag in industrial features (Banerjea et al. 2015: Figure 6; see also Angelini et al. 2017, this book). Functions may also be investigated via other means, such as historical texts or iconography (e.g., Angelucci 2008; Mentzer et al. 2015).

Uncontained natural fires, such as brush fires and forest fires can contribute burnt materials to archaeological sites (see also Deák et al. 2017, this book). However, these materials will rarely be documented in

primary position, as surface runoff and aeolian processes tend to quickly redistribute charred and ashed plant materials following burning. Reworked materials from forest and swamp fires have been documented in micromorphological studies at the sites of Prato Mollo (Italy), where the forest fires were intentionally started by people (Courty et al. 1989), Umm el Tlel (Syria; see Courty 2001), and near Guadalupe Ruin (USA; French et al. 2009). Lightning strikes can produce isolated burnt trees and tree stumps (James 1989); however, to our knowledge these features have not been documented using micromorphology and thus their similarity to anthropogenic fires is unknown. The only micromorphological investigation of a tree stump fire was published by Zerboni et al. (2013), who studied a tree stump that was intentionally burned in place by people for the purposes of charcoal production. They observed significant variability in the extent of combustion within the feature with some areas of incomplete combustion, others with a large quantity of ash, as well as discontinuous alteration of the sedimentary substrate. Additional types of uncontained natural fires in sheltered areas, such as spontaneous ignition of dung or guano in caves (e.g., Onac et al. 2007), could produce combustion features that are microscopically indistinguishable from those produced intentionally by humans (see discussions in Berna et al. 2012; James 1989; Roebroeks & Villa 2011).

Destruction layers are uncontained combustion features that are found in village or urban settings. Their identification begins with macro-scale observations, which often include documentation of collapsed structures in association with burned residues such as charcoal. Destruction layers can comprise individual structures or entire occupation levels. In thin section, destruction layers have been observed in a variety of settings. The oldest example with micromorphological data comes from the Upper Palaeolithic site of Ohalo II where burning of a hut ignited and charred the upper portion of a grass bedding layer, as evidenced by charred plant tissues visible in thin section (Nadel et al. 2004: Figure 6). Ismail-Meyer et al. (2013) observed the burnt remnants of a Neolithic wooden structure in thin section and noted the presence of large pieces of charcoal, aggregates of burnt loam and ashes. Berna et al. (2007) sampled a destruction layer at Tel Dor, and observed fragments of construction materials, such as mudbricks and plaster, burned to various degrees and exhibiting subvertical orientations, as well as burnt dung, ashes and charcoal. FTIR analyses conducted on loose materials from the layer indicate temperatures above  $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Similar analyses were conducted by Namdar et al. (2011), who demonstrated that portions of a destruction horizon at Tell Safi (Israel) were associated with burning of a structure, with some localized reworking of the burnt materials

Table 31.3 Common combustion feature substrate types.  

<table><tr><td>Substrate type</td><td>Context and description</td><td>Figures</td><td>References</td></tr><tr><td>Soils with vegetated surfaces</td><td>Observed in open air archaeological and experimental contexts. Experiments involving simulation of a wildfire and construction of simple fires on sparsely vegetated soil show that the organic particles in the top mm of the substrate and up to one cm depth are charred and their matrix exhibits an intergrain microaggregate or granular microstructure. At magnification, samples contain horizontal concentrations of anatomically connected phytoliths and charred moss and charred grass at the contact between the combustion and substrate layers. Some of mineral grains and rock fragments in the soil may be fire cracked.</td><td>Figure 31.6d-f</td><td>Courty (2012); Mallol et al. (2013a); Friesem et al. (2014b)</td></tr><tr><td>Sparsely vegetated and unvegetated earth</td><td>Observed in caves, rock shelters and other protected environments, and dynamic settings such as shorelines. Studies of experimental fires have revealed that a lack of organic substances in the top mm of the ground lead to the production of reddened, fissured substrates. In these cases, reddening possibly results from the thermal transformation of ferrous oxides to ferric oxides, although ferric oxides can also be pedogenic in origin. In a different experiment, fire on a wet surface yielded reddened zones within the combustion substrate. Analysis of these zones showed presence of plant tissues unaffected by burning, suggesting that reddening might have resulted from release of iron oxides upon evaporation of the water present in the topsoil.</td><td>Figure 31.6c</td><td>Mallol et al. (2013a); Stahlschmidt et al. (2015)</td></tr><tr><td>Guano</td><td>Bat or bird guano is a common element on the ground surfaces of caves and has been documented as a combustion substrate at several sites. Burnt bat guano is characterized by a dark yellow or reddish matrix in PPL, isotropy in XPL, and the presence of char within the aggregates.</td><td></td><td>Goldberg &amp; Macphail (2000); Bergadá et al. (2013)</td></tr><tr><td>Anthropogenic occupation surfaces</td><td>Occupation surfaces are characterized by a presence of anthropogenic remains burnt at different intensities embedded in a sedimentary matrix with or without signs of trampling. These contexts commonly show open porphyric c/f related distributions and can be distinguished from reworked combustion-derived material by the absence of ash and calcined elements. Instead, heat-altered anthropogenic surfaces are overlain more typically by upper layers containing such elements.</td><td>Figure 31.7</td><td>Miller et al. (2013)</td></tr><tr><td>Other anthropogenic materials</td><td>Materials such as ceramic tiles, plaster and mudbricks can be used in the construction of prepared surfaces and installations. Identification using composition and fabric follows guidelines published elsewhere. Heat alteration and burning residues be observed in thin section and supported by geochemical analyses. These include rubification and sesquioxides, charring or ashing of plant tempers, cracking and sloughing of the surface, soot layers and mineralogical changes.</td><td>Figure 31.3f</td><td>Berna et al. (2007); Goodman-Elgar (2008: Figures 11 and 12)</td></tr></table>

evidenced by rounding of charcoal fragments in thin section. Macphail et al. (2004) coupled micromorphological and magnetic susceptibility data and showed the presence of strongly burnt daub debris and charred stable deposits reflecting destruction layers in Roman sites in London (see also Goldberg & Macphail, 2006: Figure 11.9). Finally, Friesem et al. (2014a) describe some microscopic characteristics of a conflagration layer within an ethno-historical mudbrick structure in Greece. These characteristics include a mixture of materials burnt at different temperatures, as well as evidence for heating of the mudbrick walls.

Ritual closure deposits are a special kind of destruction layer in which burning of a structure, or materials therein, were used to signify a change in status. These

combustion features can have similar microscopic characteristics as the layers described above; however, the macro-scale characteristics can differ. For example, ritual closure deposits may not include complete destruction of the structure. Although these types of deposits have been documented in many sites (e.g., Matthews 2005), their micromorphological study is limited. One ritual closure deposit within a ceremonial building (a kiva) has been documented in thin section by Van Keuren & Roos (2013) who observed an intentionally burnt layer of plant materials, evidenced by oriented and charred plant fragments overlain by ashes.

Maintenance fires are uncontained fires used for hygiene and other purposes (Figure 31.7). Types of maintenance fires include periodic burning of plant

Figure 31.7 Maintenance fires. (a) Multiple layers of burnt bedding, consisting of aligned fragments of charred grasses and sedges, some with aligned vascular bundles (arrow). MSA, South Africa. PPL. (b) Same view as (a), XPL. The low birefringence of the feature can be attributed to its composition, which is dominated by charred plant material and phytoliths. (c) A layer of burnt dung on top of a substrate composed of mixed anthropogenic debris. The dung layer is  $2 - 3\mathrm{mm}$  in total thickness and exhibits areas that are intact, evidenced by aligned charred plant fragments, as well as areas that are locally reworked. The substrate contains mixed anthropogenic and biogenic debris including fragments of charcoal, ashes, aggregates of unburnt dung (D) with internal microlaminated fabric and phosphatic nodules (arrow). The box indicates the location of images (d) and (e). Neolithic, Turkey. PPL. (d) Detail of the base of the burnt layer in (c). Charred, aligned plant tissues are interbedded with spherulites. A large concentration of spherulites (sph) is visible at the base of the image. PPL. (e) Detail of the spherulites in (d). XPL.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/266123ae4d664aa02ea433d31bdd08b912a89f360cda8b51e692f337668e8e34.jpg)

bedding or animal dung (fumiers). Recurrent and therefore likely intentional burning of bedding has been documented at the MSA site of Sibudu, South Africa (Goldberg et al. 2009), where several microfacies types consist of finely laminated charred organic material and laminated, articulated phytoliths (Figure 31.7a and b). These features also contained heterogeneous stringers of charcoal, burnt bone, lithics, eboulis, aggregated sediment and phosphatic grains. Experiments involving construction and burning of plant bedding yielded similar types of sediment and demonstrated that the thin layers identified in archaeological context might represent much larger volumes of burnt bedding (Miller & Sievers 2012). Comparable micromorphological features have been described at the Upper Palaeolithic site of Cendres Cave, Spain (Bergadà et al. 2013). For microscopic characteristics of fumiers, see Figure 31.7c-e and Courty et al. 1991, Macphail et al. 1997 and Canti & Brochier 2017b, this book.

Open hearths are the simplest form of contained combustion features. In the field and thin section, open hearths are characterized by a lower layer of substrate and one or more overlying burnt residue layer (see

Figure 31.5a and b, sections 31.5.1-31.5.3). Open hearths may be present as discrete features or as sequences. Sequences of overlapping or 'stacked' combustion structures are common in the Palaeolithic archaeological record, appearing either as successions of white and black/brown layers (see Figure 31.5c) or as laminated ash layers (Goldberg 2003; Meignen et al. 2007; Berna & Goldberg 2008; Goldberg et al. 2009; Mentzer 2011; Wadley et al. 2011). A priori, these stacked sequences represent polyphased hearths (Wattez 1992) or successive combustion events carried out in a single place or activity area. Experimental relighting of open hearths (immediately and up to 15 days apart) did not produce diagnostic stratigraphic features, suggesting that frequent relighting can easily go unnoticed, even at microscale (Mallol et al. 2013b). In contrast, more infrequent use of a feature, with erosional, depositional, and postdepositional processes taking place in between firings may be observed using microcontextual analysis. Furthermore, because open hearths lack formally prepared substrates it is possible for anthropogenic materials unrelated to combustion to become incorporated into the structures. For example, when hearths are constructed on top of

Figure 31.8 Anthropogenic substrates. (a) A stratified succession of three intact combustion features (two paired substrates and residues overlain by a third substrate). In these features, the substrates contain abundant anthropogenic materials and can thus be interpreted as occupation surfaces. The large burnt bone fragment visible at the centre of the image is associated with the middle combustion substrate (CS2, 4 mm thick) and exhibits in situ breakage from trampling. Vertical pressure from above caused the bone fragment to migrate downwards into the underlying ashy combustion residue layer (BR3, 3 mm thick) of the lowest combustion feature. The lowest feature (CS3 and BR3) also exhibits evidence of trampling in the form of undulating fabric and fragmented bones. Open hearths, MP, Spain. Incident light scan. (b) Detail of the trampled bone originally associated with CS2, now within BR3. PPL. (c) Detail of the undulating fabric and bone fragment at the top of CS3. PPL. (d) Field photograph of a combustion feature that was excavated into generalized anthropogenic occupation debris. Neolithic roasting pit, Turkey.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-03/aa5e25dc-bdc2-4d32-bc40-7a118c1adb2f/b8b6a6a0177cbc7165dd3ca4839776ad41312c604aa70a7470b858fd21c03ac3.jpg)

human occupation surfaces (see Figure 31.8, section 31.5.3), the anthropogenic remains in the lower layer might represent human activity carried out prior to the combustion event (Malol et al. 2013a).

Prepared burning surfaces are another type of contained combustion feature (Figure 31.9). Although many varieties of prepared burning surfaces have been identified in archaeological sites (see Table 31.1), only some of these have been documented in thin section. The earliest prepared burning surfaces are the Upper Palaeolithic clay-lined hearths of Klissoura Cave, Greece (Karkanas et al. 2004). Micromorphological analyses revealed that the clay used to construct the surfaces was not available

within the cave (Karkanas et al. 2004: Figure 31.6). In Dust Cave, USA, Sherwood (2008) has also documented the microscopic characteristics of clay-lined hearths interpreted as griddles. She observed unique lithologies of the surfaces, which exhibit distinctive unconformable boundaries and support the hypothesis that these surfaces were intentionally prepared. Furthermore, two other types of lined hearths are illustrated here: a stone-paved hearth (Figure 31.9a-c; see also Gur-Arieh et al. 2012) and a calcareous plaster-lined hearth (Figure 31.9d-f), both from the Neolithic site of Aşkıkh Hüyük, Turkey. Similar types of features have been sampled and described in thin section by Matthews (2005) at Çatalhöyük, Turkey.
